201 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Foley


(674) KA 98-2083.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


257 A.D.2d 243; 692 N.Y.S.2d 248; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7100



June 18, 1999, Decided  
June 18, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from judgment of the Oneida County Court (New York) rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of promoting sexual performance by a child and attempted dissemination of indecent materials to minors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of promoting sexual performance by a child and attempted dissemination of indecent materials to minors. Defendant's convictions were based on his contact with an undercover police officer over the Internet. The officer pretended to be a minor and defendant attempted to recruit the fictional young girl for illicit sex. Defendant also sent the undercover officer sexually explicit materials containing pictures of minors having sex with adults. On appeal, defendant contended that the statute under which he was convicted was vague and overbroad and violated the Commerce Clause. The court held that the statute was narrowly tailored to accomplish the legislature's objectives. Further, the state had a compelling interest in protecting children from exposure to sexually explicit materials. The court held that the statute in question had no Commerce Clause implications. Defendant's convictions were affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for promoting sexual performance by a child and attempted dissemination of indecent materials to minors were affirmed, and the court upheld the statute under which he was convicted. The court held that the statute was not impermissibly vague or overbroad.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Overbreadth

1. Penal Law § 235.22, disseminating indecent material to minors, is not unconstitutionally overbroad, but is a precise means of protecting children from high-tech cybersex abuse and actual sexual abuse, since under the statute, adults may send sexual images to another adult, and may also invite that adult to engage in sexual relations without risking prosecution under the statute; it is only when an individual sends graphic images to a minor and then attempts to lure that minor into sexual activity does he risk conviction under the statute. Because of the two-pronged nature of the statute, communication between adults would not be chilled. Moreover, the statute defines what graphic images would be "harmful to minors" and proscribes specific acts by individuals. Although one may imagine instances in which the statute could be unconstitutionally applied, those situations would not occur with such frequency that the statute should be considered overbroad, and whatever overbreadth might exist can be cured by a case-by-case analysis. Any impermissible applications of the statute would represent only a tiny fraction of the conduct within the statute's reach and, thus, the overbreadth is not substantial.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Vagueness

2. Penal Law § 235.22, disseminating indecent material to minors, is not unconstitutionally vague because it employs the terms "sexual contact", "importunes, invites or induces", "sexual conduct", and "harmful to minors". "Sexual contact", while not defined in the statute, is defined elsewhere in the Penal Law as "any touching of the sexual or other intimate parts of a person not married to the actor for the purpose of gratifying sexual desire of either party. It includes the touching of the actor by the victim, as well as the touching of the victim by the actor, whether directly or through clothing" (Penal Law § 130.00 [3]). In addition, the meaning of "sexual contact" can be inferred from the other prohibited conduct, i.e., sexual intercourse and deviate sexual intercourse. The terms "importunes, invites or induces" are common terms with common ordinary meanings. "Sexual conduct" is defined as "acts of masturbation, homosexuality, sexual intercourse, or physical contact with a person's clothed or unclothed genitals, pubic area, buttocks or, if such person be a female, breast" (Penal Law § 235.20 [3]). All the terms within that definition are commonly used terms and are not vague. Finally, "harmful to minors" is defined in Penal Law § 235.20 (6) and has been held sufficient to give adequate notice to individuals.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Content-Based Restriction

3. Penal Law § 235.22, disseminating indecent material to minors, is intended to protect children from the primary effects of the transmission of offensive speech and, thus, is a content-based restriction on speech. However, it is well settled that a State's interest in safeguarding the physical and psychological well-being of a minor is compelling, and in light of the two-pronged nature of the statute, which requires both the sending of graphic images to a minor and the attempt to lure that minor into sexual activity, the statute is a precisely drawn means of serving a compelling interest and is no broader than necessary. Accordingly, it is constitutional under the First Amendment.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Constitutionality - Commerce Clause

4. The purpose of Penal Law § 235.22, disseminating indecent material to minors, is to protect children who use the Internet from the primary effects of the transmission of offensive speech, and not to regulate commerce; it is not an economic protectionist measure, but rather is directed at a legitimate local concern. The requirement in the statute that a defendant lure his victim into sexual activity narrows its scope and lessens any burden on commerce. The court cannot conceive of any legitimate commerce involving the sending of graphic images to minors while at the same time attempting to lure them into engaging in sexual activity. Accordingly, any incidental effects that section 235.22 may have on commerce are not unduly burdensome in relation to the compelling interest of the State in protecting children, and thus it does not violate the commerce clause of the US Constitution.

Crimes - Disseminating Indecent Material to Minors - Sufficiency of Evidence

5. In a prosecution arising from defendant's use of the Internet on his personal computer, Penal Law § 235.22, disseminating indecent material to minors, does not require the People to prove that defendant intended his communication to be received by a minor. Defendant's conviction under Penal Law § 263.15 for promoting a sexual performance by a child, was based on legally sufficient evidence and the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence where the People submitted sufficient proof that the graphic images sent by defendant were pictures of actual children and had not been altered; the jury had the pictures before it to review, and its determination that the pictures represented actual children is supported by the record.

Crimes - Witnesses - Cross-Examination

6. In a prosecution arising from defendant's use of the Internet on his personal computer in attempting to arrange a sexual encounter with a State Trooper posing as a minor, the court did not improperly restrict the cross-examination of the Trooper regarding his alcohol addiction since it would be improper to so cross-examine the Trooper where such alcohol use had no relation to his ability to recall or perceive the events in question. The court also properly denied defense counsel access to the Trooper's personnel file after an in camera inspection thereof revealed that it contained no exculpatory material, and even if the material should have been disclosed, there is no reasonable possibility that the failure to disclose contributed to the verdict. Moreover, the court properly admitted a computer disk containing the images sent by defendant, and the conversations between the Trooper, who posed as a 15-year-old girl, and defendant, since the contents of the disk were unique and the Trooper was able to authenticate it.

 

202 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Medina


10506, 10969


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


262 A.D.2d 708; 693 N.Y.S.2d 632; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6493



June 10, 1999, Decided  
June 10, 1999, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the County Court of Chemung County (New York), which convicted defendant of promoting prison contraband in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a prison inmate, was found in possession of a sharpened metal lid of a can with part of it wrapped in electrical tape, and charged with promoting prison contraband in the first degree. The lower court denied defendant's motion to vacate the conviction. The court affirmed. The court rejected defendant's argument that N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25 and the Department of Correctional Services Standards of Inmate Behavior were unconstitutionally vague. Both the statute and the inmate rules pertaining to contraband made provided adequate warning and were sufficiently clear to inform a reasonable person of the nature of the offense prohibited. Defendant's failure to admit that the contraband was dangerous did not render defendant's guilty plea invalid. Defendant's recitation of the facts did not cast doubt upon his guilt, the voluntariness of his plea or negate an element of the crime charged. Defendant's ineffective assistance of counsel claim and his claim that his constitutional right to a speedy trial was denied were without merit. There was only a five-month delay between the time of the offense and presentation to the grand jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision, finding that state law and inmate rules concerning contraband were not unconstitutionally vague because both provided adequate warning to inform a reasonable person of the nature of the offense charged. Defendant's failure to admit all the facts did not render defendant's guilty plea invalid, because defendant's version of the facts did not cast doubt upon his guilt.

 

203 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Green v. Board of Educ.


98-05636


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


262 A.D.2d 411; 691 N.Y.S.2d 187; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6332


April 28, 1999, Submitted  
June 7, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, probationary teacher, appealed from a judgment by the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), that denied his petition for wrongful termination against defendant Board of Education.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a probationary teacher, brought an action against defendant Board of Education for wrongful termination but the petition was denied and the proceeding dismissed. Plaintiff appealed the denial of his petition. On appeal, the court stated that the plaintiff was a probationary employee and could be terminated without a statement of reasons provided that the termination was not in bad faith, in violation of statutory law, or for unconstitutional or illegal reasons. The plaintiff had to establish such bad faith or illegal reasons and conclusory allegations of bad faith were insufficient to meet this burden or to warrant a hearing. Plaintiff failed to meet this burden. Therefore, the petition was properly denied because the defendant's termination did not constitute bad faith or illegal conduct.


OUTCOME: Denial of petition was affirmed because the plaintiff was a probationary employee who could be terminated without a statement of reason provided the termination was not in bad faith, in violation of a statute, or for unconstitutional or illegal reasons.

 

204 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bothwell


899


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


261 A.D.2d 232; 690 N.Y.S.2d 231; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5506



May 18, 1999, Decided  
May 18, 1999, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name of this Case has been Corrected by the Court June 30, 1999. The Name of this Case has been Corrected by the Court August 2, 1999.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of New York appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence of 35 bags of crack cocaine seized from defendant.


OVERVIEW: Three police officers whose duties were to police quality of life offenses observed defendant drinking a beer on the sidewalk. Upon seeing the police officers, defendant fled into a nearby luncheonette. The officers followed defendant and asked him to step outside in order to issue him a summons for public consumption of alcohol. After defendant refused, the officers forced defendant outside, whereupon baggies of cocaine fell out of defendant's clenched fists. The trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress the evidence of the 35 bags of crack cocaine recovered from defendant. On appeal, the court reversed the grant of the suppression motion because the officers had a reasonable cause to believe that defendant had committed an offense in their presence. It was reasonable for the officers to believe that defendant was holding an open bottle of beer with intent to drink it in public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's granting of defendant's motion to suppress the evidence of 35 bags of crack cocaine seized from defendant because police officers had reasonable cause to believe that defendant had committed the offense of possessing an open container containing an alcoholic beverage in public.

Steinmann v. Village of Spring Valley


98-02265


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


261 A.D.2d 548; 691 N.Y.S.2d 66; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5389


April 1, 1999, Submitted  
May 17, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court, Rockland County, (New York), which denied a petition and dismissed a proceeding brought by petitioner pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to review a determination of respondent village reassigning petitioner to a non-detective position within the village police department.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a judgment denying a petition and dismissing a proceeding brought by petitioner police officer, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to review a determination of respondent village reassigning petitioner within the village police department to a non-detective position. Petitioner had contended that he could not be removed from the position of detective pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 58(4)(c), 75(1)(e), and 59-a, as he had performed detective duties for a period in excess of 18 months. The court rejected this contention. Petitioner was not entitled to the benefits of 1997 N.Y. Laws 134, because the statute applied only to those people who performed investigative duties and not to those people, like petitioner, who worked in supervisory capacities. Further, the Rockland County Police Act took precedence over inconsistent provisions of the New York Civil Service Law and, under the Act, police chiefs had authority to revoke detective assignments at any time. Finally, the right to permanent appointment to a detective position under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 59-a was not triggered, because the local civil service commission had not classified the position of "detective."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment denying a petition and dismissing a proceeding brought by petitioner police officer to review a determination of respondent village reassigning petitioner within the village police department to a non-detective position, because the provisions of the New York Civil Service Law did not prevent the police chief from removing petitioner from the position of detective.

 

206 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Ulster Home Care, Inc. v. Vacco


83571


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


255 A.D.2d 73; 688 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4222



April 22, 1999, Decided  
April 22, 1999, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended April 23, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The orders are affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Ulster County, (New York) which granted a motion by plaintiff for a preliminary injunction and enjoined defendant state from enforcing the "public charge" regulation, 18 N.Y. C.R.R. 505.14(h)(7)(ii), on a retroactive basis, and an order of said court which found defendant state in contempt of court.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was an agency enrolled in the Medicaid program. Defendant state alleged plaintiff had sought reimbursement for care services at a higher rate than charged to the general public, purportedly in violation of 18 N.Y. C.R.R. 505.14(h)(7)(ii) (the "public charge" regulation). Plaintiff commenced an action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief. The trial court granted a motion by plaintiff for a preliminary injunction and enjoined defendant state from enforcing the public charge regulation on a retroactive basis, and found defendant state in contempt of court. The court affirmed. The court held that defendant state was properly found in civil contempt because it clearly violated the trial court's order prohibiting defendant state from prosecuting plaintiff. The court held that until judicial relief to stay or vacate the order was successfully obtained, defendant state was duty-bound to honor it. The court held further that the trial court had the authority to enjoin the criminal prosecution because the constitutionality of the regulation was in question and no question of fact was involved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's orders granting plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction and finding defendant state in contempt of court, holding that the trial court had the authority to enjoin defendant, and defendant had clearly violated the trial court's order prohibiting defendant state from prosecuting plaintiff.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions - Preliminary Injunction - Enjoining Criminal Prosecution of Licensed Home Care Agency for Medicaid Fraud

1. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the "public charge" regulation governing medical assistance payments to personal care services providers (18 NYCRR 505.14 [h] [7] [ii]) in which plaintiff, the operator of a licensed home care agency enrolled in the Medicaid program, sought declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent enforcement of the regulation, Supreme Court had the authority to grant a preliminary injunction enjoining any criminal prosecution of plaintiff for violating the regulation which would have automatically terminated it from the Medicaid program (see, 18 NYCRR 515.7). A declaratory judgment in this case would not run counter to the principle that jurisdiction over criminal matters rests solely in courts of law and may not be interfered with by courts of equity. Declaratory relief is available in cases where, as here, the constitutionality or legality of a statute or regulation is in question and no question of fact is involved. In this case, where the constitutionality of the "public charge" regulation has been implicated, the nature and purpose of the regulation sought to be tested is not disputed and the only questions involved are those of law, equity should not hesitate to define the rights of the parties. Accordingly, there is no basis to disturb the order granting injunctive relief.

Contempt - Civil Contempt - Attorney General's Violation of Order Enjoining Criminal Prosecution of Licensed Home Care Agency for Medicaid Fraud

2. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the "public charge" regulation governing medical assistance payments to personal care services providers (18 NYCRR 505.14 [h] [7] [ii]) in which plaintiff, the operator of a licensed home care agency enrolled in the Medicaid program, sought declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent enforcement of the regulation, Supreme Court, after granting a preliminary injunction enjoining any criminal prosecution of plaintiff for violating the regulation, properly held defendant Attorney General in civil contempt for submitting charges to a Grand Jury and obtaining an indictment against plaintiff despite the clear mandate of the order granting the preliminary injunction. Such order explicitly enjoined criminal prosecution of plaintiff in order to protect plaintiff's equitable rights and to avoid irreversible injury to it "as a result of prosecution". Until judicial relief to stay or vacate the order was successfully obtained, defendant was duty-bound to honor it. Moreover, filing a notice of appeal from Supreme Court's order did not invoke the automatic stay provisions of CPLR 5519 (a) (1) as to cloak defendant with legal protection from the subsequent contempt. A prohibitory injunction, which operates to restrain the commission or continuance of an act and to prevent a threatened injury, thereby ordinarily having the effect of maintaining the status quo, is not automatically stayed under CPLR 5519 (a) (1). To countenance defendant's position that CPLR 5519 (a) (1) permitted a criminal prosecution to go forward in its ordinary course would not maintain the status quo and would make any decision in plaintiff's favor in the underlying action barren.

 

207 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Avon Bard Co. v. Aquarian Found.


2826, 2827


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


260 A.D.2d 207; 688 N.Y.S.2d 514; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3960



April 13, 1999, Decided  
April 13, 1999, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court, First Department (New York), which affirmed two orders of the lower court granting defendant's motions to dismiss plaintiff's holdover petitions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought to recover possession of his leased premises, two adjacent residential apartments subject to rent stabilization. Petitioner filed holdover petitions against respondent, the tenant of the premises. The lease was held in respondent's name and respondent was a religious corporation. The lower court found various defects in the non-renewal notice, which it denominated fatal infirmities, and dismissed the petitions. The appeals court affirmed the ruling, but on different grounds, finding that it was manifest that the apartments at issue in the proceeding were rented for the benefit of an identifiable individual, respondent's reverend. Petitioner appealed and the court reversed, reinstated the petitions, and awarded judgments of possession to petitioner. The court held that the summary proceeding commenced by petitioner was properly predicated on non-primary residence grounds because there was not an identifiable individual who had the right to demand a renewal lease and the lease did not specify any individual as occupant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of respondent, reinstated the holdover petitions, and granted possession of the apartments to petitioner because under the rent stabilization scheme, only the person designated on the lease was entitled to renewal.

 

208 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Order of Teachers of the Children of God v. Trs. of the Estate of Diocese of Long Island


98-02122


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


260 A.D.2d 356; 687 N.Y.S.2d 684; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3560


February 16, 1999, Argued  
April 5, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by, Claim dismissed by Order of the Teachers of the Children of God, Inc. v. Diocese of Long Is., 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2030 (N.Y. Sup. Ct., Feb. 27, 2002)



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff religious order appealed a denial of summary judgment from the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), on its claim for extinguishment of a contingent reverter on the ground that defendant trustees had failed to record intent to preserve the reverter as required under N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 345.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a denial of summary judgment to plaintiff religious order in a suit under N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 345 seeking the invalidation of a contingent reverter in a deed. The property at issue was conveyed from defendant trustees many years earlier and provided for reversion if plaintiff ceased to use the property as a motherhouse. Plaintiff urged that defendant's failure to record a declaration of intention to preserve the reverter, as required by N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 345, extinguished it. The lower court denied summary judgment on the basis that § 345 was unconstitutional as applied to a reversionary interest which did not mature until after the applicable recording deadline. Because there was a factual dispute as to when plaintiff ceased using the property as a motherhouse, the appellate court ruled that summary judgment was properly denied because it could not be determined whether defendant's reversionary interest matured prior to the deadline for recording a declaration. Accordingly, it would be premature to resolve the issue of whether § 345 could be constitutionally applied to the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of summary judgment to plaintiff religious order in its action seeking invalidation of a reversion contingency in its deed from defendant trustees, on different grounds. The court concluded that determining the constitutionality of the application of the statute at issue was premature in light of the existence of a material fact issue as to when plaintiff ceased using the property as a motherhouse.

 

209 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Brooklyn Union Gas Co. v. Commissioner of Taxation & Fin.


81705


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


255 A.D.2d 80; 687 N.Y.S.2d 478; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3304



April 1, 1999, Decided  
April 1, 1999, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended September 16, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The determination is confirmed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public utility sought review of a determination by the Tax Appeals Tribunal (New York), which denied its claim for a refund of a corporation tax assessment imposed under N.Y. Tax Law art. 9.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a public utility that sold natural gas to residential and commercial customers in New York. Petitioner sought a partial refund of its gross receipts tax imposed under N.Y. Tax Law art. 9. The Division of Taxation and Finance denied petitioner's request, on the basis that the deduction was not allowable under Tax Law art. 9 since by statutory construction, gross receipts taxes were not taxes imposed on customers and, therefore remained includable in the corporation's gross earnings. Petitioner's challenge to the denial was rejected by an Administrative Law Judge whose determination was upheld by respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal on administrative appeal. The court confirmed the Tribunal's determination, concluding it was not arbitrary and capricious nor an abuse of discretion. Petitioner failed to prove that the legal incidence of the gross receipts tax, imposed by Tax Law art. 9, shifted to consumers after adoption of the gas import tax. While the economic burden of the gross receipts tax and gas import tax fell on the consumer, the gross receipts tax statutes explicitly imposed the legal incidence of the gross receipts tax on utilities. N.Y. Tax Law § 186, 186-a.


OUTCOME: Determination denying petitioner's claim for a refund of a corporation tax assessment confirmed without costs and petition dismissed because petitioner failed to prove that the enactment of the gas import tax required the court to shift the legal incidence of the gross receipts tax from gas utilities to customers.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Franchise Tax on Business Corporations - Gross Receipts Tax on Natural Gas Sales - Effect of Enactment of Gas Import Tax on Utility's Right to Partial Refund

The determination of respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal that petitioner, a public utility involved in the sale of natural gas, was not entitled to a partial refund for gross receipts taxes imposed on its gas sales within New York because the enactment of a separate gas import tax in 1991 (see, Tax Law § 189), requiring gas utilities to remit to the State import tax revenues collected from certain gas consumers, did not effect a shift in the legal incidence of the gross receipts tax to gas consumers as to make the pass through of gross receipts tax recoupment to gas customers deductible in the calculation of petitioner's gross earnings, was not arbitrary and capricious nor an abuse of discretion and should therefore be confirmed. The gas import tax was crafted with the legal incidence of the tax on gas importers, with the utility operating as the collection agent for the State in a manner analogous to sales tax collection. Since the legal incidence of the gas import tax falls on the gas importer rather than the utility, State tax regulations allow petitioner to exclude gas import tax collections from corporate taxable receipts when calculating the amount of gross receipts taxes owed. In contrast, the gross receipts tax is explicitly imposed upon utilities and is considered part of each utilities' operating costs (Tax Law § 186-a [6]). Moreover, in further contrast to the gas import tax, the gross receipts tax and surcharge statutes (see, Tax Law §§ 186, 186-b, 186-c, 188) make no reference to the utility acting as a "trustee" for collection purposes, thereby indicating that the Legislature did not intend to hold gas consumers liable for nonpayment of the utility's gross receipts taxes. Lastly, petitioner lacks standing to assert that the gas import tax will be rendered unconstitutional as violative of the Commerce Clause if the Tribunal's determination is upheld. Petitioner's claim for a partial refund of its gross receipts tax does not properly place the constitutionality of the gas import tax before the Appellate Division. Accordingly, the petition seeking a review of respondent's determination should be dismissed.

 

210 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Dockum v. Syroco Inc.


82764


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


260 A.D.2d 688; 687 N.Y.S.2d 759; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3294



April 1, 1999, Decided  
April 1, 1999, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended April 12, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The decision is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant challenged a judgment from the Workers' Compensation Board, which ruled that claimant did not suffer a compensable injury and denied workers' compensation benefits, after finding that claimant's injury was solely mental and thus barred by N.Y. Work. Comp. Law § 2. Claimant contended that his physical injuries resulting from his attempted suicide were compensable.


OVERVIEW: Claimant appealed a judgment that denied his application for workers' compensation benefits on grounds that claimant's physical injuries, which arose after he attempted to commit suicide after he was discharged from his employment, were solely mental and thus not compensable. N.Y. Work. Comp. Law § 2. The court affirmed the judgment that denied workers' compensation benefits. The court first ruled that respondent employer's testimony that claimant was discharged for failing to follow his supervisor's instructions, in light of the fact that claimant received three prior disciplinary warnings, was sufficient to support respondent Workers' Compensation Board's finding that claimant's discharge was the product of a lawful personnel decision undertaken in good faith. The court then ruled that because claimant's suicide attempt itself was not compensable under section 2(7), it necessarily followed that the physical complications that resulted from the suicide attempt were not compensable either.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that denied him worker's compensation benefits. Because there was sufficient testimony to support the finding that claimant was discharged was the product of a lawful personnel decision undertaken in good faith, and because claimant's attempted suicide clearly was not compensable under the applicable workers' compensation statute, the physical complications that resulted therefrom were not compensable either.

 

211 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Klepper v. Christian Coalition, Inc.


83342


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


259 A.D.2d 926; 686 N.Y.S.2d 898; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2529



March 18, 1999, Decided  
March 18, 1999, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent not-for-profit corporation challenged an order of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which denied its motion for summary judgment in petitioner's proceeding to have respondent declared a political committee as defined by N.Y. Elec. Law § 14-100.


OVERVIEW: Based upon the political activities of respondent not-for-profit corporation, petitioner commenced an action pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 16-114, seeking to have respondent declared a "political committee" under N.Y. Elec. Law § 14-100. Respondent moved for summary judgment and sought further review after the trial court denied its motion. In affirming, the court rejected respondent's contention that § 14-100 violated its rights under U.S. Const. amend. I as restricting its right to engage in issue advocacy. Section 14-100 expressly exempted issue advocacy from its restrictions; as such, respondent enjoyed an unencumbered right to engage in issue advocacy, and respondent's facial challenge to the constitutionality of this statute lacked merit. Further, because the record had not been fully developed concerning the scope of respondent's activities, it would have been premature to rule upon whether the statute was unconstitutional as applied to respondent.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court denying respondent not-for-profit corporation's motion for summary judgment was affirmed on issue of whether political committee statute was unconstitutional as applied to respondent, as summary judgment was premature where the underlying record had not yet been fully developed. Respondent's facial challenge to the constitutionality of the statute lacked merit.

 

212 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Sudarsky v. New York State Div. of Hous. & Community Renewal


3309


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


258 A.D.2d 405; 685 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1888



February 25, 1999, Decided  
February 25, 1999, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent landlord and petitioner tenants appealed the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), granting a petition pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 filed by petitioners and holding that the decision of the division of housing and community renewal upholding an order deregulating petitioners' apartment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landlord and the Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR) requested income verification for petitioner tenants. The DHCR notice stated that failure to file a timely answer would result in deregulation of the apartment. Petitioners failed to respond. The district rent administrator issued a deregulation order. Petitioner husband filed a petition for administrative review. DHCR denied the petition. Petitioners commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 seeking vacatur and annulment of the DHCR order. The trial court granted the petition. Respondent and petitioners appealed. The court modified the trial court's order, dismissed the petition, confirmed DHCR's order, and dismissed petitioners' appeal, holding that the apartment was properly deregulated pursuant to the Administrative Code of the City of New York, tit. 26, ch. 4, because petitioners failed to supply the income verification and failed to provide a valid excuse for the failure, petitioners' cross appeal was untimely, and petitioners' failed to preserve the claim that they were entitled to a renewal lease because their lease expired during the pendency of the administrative proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order, dismissed the petition, confirmed the deregulation order, and dismissed petitioner tenants' appeal, holding that the apartment was properly deregulated because petitioners failed to provide income verification and failed to provide a valid excuse for the failure, petitioners' cross-appeal was untimely, and petitioners failed to preserve the claim that they were entitled to a renewal lease.

 

213 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Grippo v. Martin


82939


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


257 A.D.2d 952; 686 N.Y.S.2d 118; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 734



January 28, 1999, Decided  
January 28, 1999, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court for Schenectady County (New York), that dismissed her suit challenging her termination pursuant to a disciplinary procedure for employees who tested positive for drug and alcohol use.


OVERVIEW: Respondent employer enacted a disciplinary procedure which provided for the termination of any employee who tested positive for drug and alcohol use twice in a ten-year period. Respondent had negotiated with petitioner's union for the implementation of the drug and alcohol policy prior to its enactment. After petitioner employee was terminated pursuant to this policy, he brought suit alleging the procedure violated his due process rights and N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75. The trial court dismissed the suit, and petitioner appealed. The court affirmed. A contract provision in a collective bargaining agreement could modify, supplement, or replace the more traditional forms of protection afforded public employees. Such a waiver of statutory rights in disciplinary matters was not against public policy, and members of the bargaining unit were bound thereby.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing petitioner employee's suit alleging that respondent employer's disciplinary procedure violated his due process rights was affirmed because petitioner's statutory rights were waived under a collective bargaining agreement.

 

214 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Mainello v. McCall


82227


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


252 A.D.2d 235; 684 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1999 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 478



January 21, 1999, Decided  
January 21, 1999, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner assistant deputy superintendent of the state police appealed the decision of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), dismissing his application for review of respondent comptroller of the state of New York's determination that petitioner had to retire from his position because he reached 57 years of age.


OVERVIEW: When petitioner, a state police assistant deputy superintendent, joined the special pension and retirement program for state police officers, N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 381-b(e) provided that the mandatory retirement age for an assistant deputy superintendent was 60 years of age. Then, the law was changed to fix a uniform mandatory retirement age of 57 for all state police officers. When petitioner turned 57, respondent comptroller of the state of New York determined that he had to retire pursuant to the amended § 381-b(e). Petitioner sought review of respondent's decision, contending that the amended law violated the Nonimpairment Clause of N.Y. Const. art V, § 7 because the law shortened his term of employment by three years. The application was dismissed. Petitioner appealed the dismissal of his application for review. The appeals court affirmed the dismissal because the fact that there could be no constitutional impairment of pension system benefits did not create a constitutional right to stay in public employment for any specified period of time. Moreover, the court found the change in the mandatory retirement age was enacted to further a legitimate public policy goal.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of petitioner's application for review of the determination that he had to retire because he was 57 years of age was affirmed because, although the Nonimpairment Clause of the N.Y. Const. prohibited the impairment of pension system benefits, this prohibition did not create a constitutional right for petitioner to stay in public employment for any specified period of time.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service - Retirement and Pension Benefits - Mandatory Retirement of Members of State Police - No Unconstitutional Impairment of Pension Benefits

Petitioner, who attained his position as Assistant Deputy Superintendent with the Division of State Police in 1994 at the age of 55, was properly required to retire at the conclusion of the year in which he attained age 57 in accordance with Retirement and Social Security Law § 381-b (e) (as amended by L 1988, ch 759), notwithstanding the fact that when he became a member of the 20-year special pension and retirement program for State Police in 1969 the statute provided an extended mandatory retirement age of 60 for members holding certain high-ranking positions within the Division of State Police, including petitioner's position. The application of the 1988 amendment establishing the uniform mandatory retirement age of 57 for all members of the State Police to petitioner did not unconstitutionally diminish or impair petitioner's contractual rights in the pension system in violation of the Nonimpairment Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art V, § 7). The fact that there can be no constitutional impairment of pension system benefits does not create a constitutional right to stay in public employment nor does the Nonimpairment Clause prohibit the State from establishing mandatory retirement ages. The subject change in the mandatory retirement age was enacted to further a legitimate public policy goal. Petitioner merely seeks to extend the term of his public employment, thereby effecting but a "minor and entirely incidental" influence on his retirement benefits. Accordingly, the petition challenging petitioner's involuntary retirement was properly dismissed.

217 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Gordon v. Urbach


82975


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


252 A.D.2d 94; 682 N.Y.S.2d 711; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14117



December 30, 1998, Decided  
December 30, 1998, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended April 20, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The order is modified, on the law, without costs, by declaring that it has not been shown that defendant Commissioner of Taxation and Finance, defendant Deputy Commissioner of Taxation and Finance or defendants John Does 1-100 illegally or improperly retained defendant Cooper, Erving, Savage, Nolan & Heller LLP to perform certain legal services in this matter, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff executive officer challenged an order of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which dismissed his complaint against defendants, Commissioner of New York Department of Taxation and Finance, Deputy Commissioner, and outside counsel, that sought a declaration that the payment of legal fees for the performance of certain collection services constituted a misappropriation of state funds.


OVERVIEW: Effort to collect unpaid taxes by the New York Department of Taxation and Finance proved unsuccessful against plaintiff executive officer's company. Defendants, Commissioner of the New York Department of Taxation and Finance and Deputy Commissioner, decided to retain defendant outside counsel to represent it in the enforcement proceedings. The attorney general and the comptroller executed and approved the retainer agreement. Plaintiff challenged this decision, and the trial court dismissed his complaint. On appeal, plaintiff argued that defendants, commissioner and deputy commission, had no authority to retain defendant outside counsel to pursue collection matters against plaintiff's company and that the attorney general's authority in this regard could not be delegated. The court held that the language in N.Y. Tax Law §§ 1414(a), 1449(1) was sufficiently broad to permit the attorney general to retain outside counsel to perform enforcement and collection services. While the language in N.Y. Tax Law § 692(h) was not as expansive, it did not preclude the attorney general from retaining outside counsel to pursue enforcement mechanisms as to taxes.


OUTCOME: The court modified and affirmed the order dismissing plaintiff executive officer's complaint against defendants, Commissioner of the New York Department of Taxation and Finance, Deputy Commissioner, and outside counsel, for a declaration that the payment of legal fees for certain collection services constituted a misappropriation of state funds. The attorney general was permitted to retain outside counsel for collection services.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Misappropriation of State Funds - Payment of Legal Fees to Outside Counsel to Collect Unpaid Taxes

1. While plaintiff has standing as a citizen-taxpayer pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b to challenge the State's payment of legal fees to outside counsel to collect certain unpaid taxes and penalties owed by plaintiff and his corporation as a misappropriation of State funds, the standing conferred by the statute does not extend to any challenge to the process by which outside counsel was retained. Plaintiff's improper delegation claim does not in any way implicate the wrongful expenditure, misappropriation or misapplication of State funds. In addition, since the Attorney-General essentially ratified the actions of respondent State Tax Commissioner by granting oral approval of the underlying arrangement and then by executing the retainer agreement, which was approved by the Comptroller, plaintiff's challenge to the process employed in retaining outside counsel is academic.

Taxation - Enforcement - Retention of Outside Counsel to Engage in Collection Services

2. In an action pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b to declare the State's payment of legal fees to outside counsel to collect certain unpaid taxes and penalties owed by plaintiff and his corporation illegal as a misappropriation of State funds, the State defendants are entitled to a declaration in their favor that the Attorney-General is not prohibited from retaining outside counsel to engage in collection services with respect to the taxes owed in this case. As to the real property transfer gains taxes and real estate transfer taxes owed, the relevant statutory provisions authorizing the Attorney-General, upon the request of the Tax Commission, to "bring or cause to be brought" an action to enforce payment of the taxes imposed (Tax Law former § 1449 [1]; § 1414 [a]) are sufficiently broad to permit the Attorney-General to retain outside counsel to perform enforcement and collection services. Furthermore, while the language contained in Tax Law § 692 (h) with regard to the collection of the personal income taxes owed is not as expansive, that statute cannot be read as precluding the Attorney-General from retaining outside counsel to pursue available enforcement mechanisms with respect to the personal income taxes owed.

 

218 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. State


82532


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


250 A.D.2d 314; 681 N.Y.S.2d 170; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12993



December 3, 1998, Decided  
December 3, 1998, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Brown v. State, 2004 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6707 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, May 13, 2004)



DISPOSITION: The order is modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as imposed upon claimants the burden of proof requiring them to identify a similarly situated nonminority group that was treated differently; the burden of proof is imposed upon the State to prove that the means used in the investigation were necessary to promote a compelling State interest; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimants challenged a decision of the Court of Claims (New York), which held that the claimants had to establish they were treated differently than a non-minority group in a similar situation to prevail on their race-based equal protection claims. Defendant State challenged a decision declaring the litigation a class action and determining that claimants who filed a claim but not a notice of claim met the jurisdictional requirements.


OVERVIEW: The police received a report that a woman had been attacked by an African-American male and determined that the assailant may have cut his hand or forearm. They stopped African-American males in and around the city, checking for the injury. The claimants commenced a constitutional tort action, alleging the actions of the police were racially motivated. The claims court set forth the standard of proof and declared the suit a class action. The court found that the claims court properly tolled the statute of limitations based on its earlier dismissal of the claims, which dismissal was reversed, and that the notices of intention to file a claim qualified as claims. Further, the claimants satisfied all the requisite criteria for a class action as common questions of law and fact with respect to liability predominated over questions affecting only individual class members. The claims court erred, however, in imposing the wrong burden of proof on the claimants as discriminatory enforcement was established as a matter of law and the strict scrutiny test placed the burden on the State to prove that the means used in the investigation were necessary to promote a compelling state interest.


OUTCOME: The court modified the claims court's order by reversing the imposition upon the claimants of the burden of proof requiring them to identify a similarly situated non-minority group that was treated differently and imposing the burden on the State to prove that the means used in the investigation were necessary to promote a compelling state interest. The court then affirmed the order as modified.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions - What Statute Governs - Constitutional Tort Claim

1. The three-year Statute of Limitations for personal injury actions ( CPLR 214 [5]) applies to a constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated. The Court of Appeals has held that a civil rights claim brought under 42 USC § 1983 is governed by the three-year limitations period for personal injury actions and the same Statute of Limitations is appropriate for a constitutional tort under the State Constitution.

Limitation of Actions - Tolling - Constitutional Tort Claim

2. The Statute of Limitations for a constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated, should be tolled where the Court of Claims initially dismissed the claim on the merits on the ground that constitutional torts are not cognizable in the Court of Claims and the claim was thereafter reinstated on appeal by the Court of Appeals. Under these circumstances, the Court of Claims properly tolled the Statute of Limitations for the reinstated claim because filing the claim within the applicable two-year period (see, Court of Claims Act § 10 [3]) would have been meaningless in light of its prior order which had dismissed the claim. While a court may not extend a Statute of Limitations or invent tolling principles, the Court of Claims in this case appropriately applied the common-law doctrine that the Statute of Limitations is tolled where the limitations would run against a person unable to bring an action based on a prior ruling.

State - Claim against State - Notice of Intention to File Claim - Notice of Intention Treated as Claim

3. The Court of Claims properly determined in a constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated, that those claimants who filed notices of intention to file a claim but who did not timely file a notice of claim met the jurisdictional filing requirements of Court of Claims Act § 10 (8), which provides that "a claimant who timely serves and files a notice of intention but who fails to timely serve or file a claim may, nevertheless, apply to the court for permission to treat the notice of intention as a claim." The Court of Claims may treat a notice of intention to file a claim as a notice of claim as long as the notice of intention adequately sets forth the time and place and as long as the State is not prejudiced by an omission. Here, the notice of intention states that the rights of each individual were violated in or near the college campus during a five-day period. Moreover, the State has been aware of the lawsuit, has been investigating the incidents since shortly after they occurred and the issues related to liability are generally identical with respect to each claimant. The State has thus not been deceived or misled and its rights have not been prejudiced. Accordingly, the notices of intention to file a claim qualify as claims.

Actions - Class Actions - Constitutional Tort Claim

4. A constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated, meets the statutory prerequisites for class certification. The Court of Claims properly exercised its discretion in concluding that claimants satisfied all the requisite criteria detailed in CPLR 901 (a) since common questions of law and fact with respect to the issue of liability are substantial and predominate over any questions affecting only individual members of the class.

Appeal - Matters Appealable - Standard of Proof Determination in Constitutional Tort Claim - "Law of the Case" Doctrine

5. The sua sponte determination of the Court of Claims with respect to the standard of proof ultimately to be applied at trial of a constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated, is appealable under the "law of the case" doctrine. The court determined that claimants must demonstrate that they were treated differently than a similarly situated nonminority group. Where, as here, a court directly passes upon an issue which is necessarily involved in the final determination on the merits, it becomes "the law of the case" and is appealable. In addition, the issue of what must be proven by claimants in order for them to prevail on their equal protection claim is more than advisory, it is a decision which directly relates to the final determination of the merits of this case and, as such, affects a substantial right of claimants; it is therefore appealable (see, CPLR 5701 [a] [2] [v]).

Torts - Constitutional Tort - Burden of Proof

6. The Court of Claims erred in determining that the standard of proof in a trial of a constitutional tort claim against the State, in which claimants allege that the conduct of law enforcement officials in singling out all the African-American students enrolled at a State college for questioning about a knife-point assault was racially motivated, requires that claimants establish that they were treated differently than a nonminority group in a similar situation in order to prevail on their equal protection claim. Since the State has admitted that each person was stopped on the basis of race alone, discriminatory enforcement is established as a matter of law. Consequently, claimants, in proving their cause of action, should not be held to the "formidable task" of demonstrating that they were treated differently than a similarly situated nonminority group. Rather, the proper standard for the Court of Claims to apply is the strict scrutiny test, which places the burden on the State to prove that the means used in the investigation were necessary to promote a compelling State interest.

People v. Epps


78275


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


255 A.D.2d 840; 682 N.Y.S.2d 247; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12765



November 25, 1998, Decided  
November 25, 1998, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 29, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the County Court of Franklin County (New York), which convicted him of attempted promoting prison contraband in the first degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 205.00, 205.25.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an inmate, pleaded guilty to attempted promoting prison contraband and waived his right to appeal all issues except for sentencing. After sentencing defendant sought review, arguing that the waiver was not made knowingly, intelligently, or voluntarily, that he was denied effective counsel, and the statutes under which he was convicted were unconstitutional. On appeal the court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court found that defendant's contentions that his waiver was not knowingly, intelligently, or voluntarily made and that he was denied effective assistance of counsel had not been preserved for review because he had not made a motion to vacate or withdraw his plea. The court nonetheless found that defendant knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily waived his right to appeal after being advised of his rights and the consequences of the waiver, and that defendant received meaningful representation from which he received the benefit of an advantageous plea agreement. The court found that the statutes under which defendant was convicted were neither unconstitutionally vague nor unconstitutional as an improper delegation of legislative authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempted promoting prison contraband in the first degree.

R.A. Bronson, Inc. v. Franklin Correctional Facility


81107A, 81107B


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


255 A.D.2d 723; 680 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11904



November 12, 1998, Decided  
November 12, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the judgments of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), that dismissed petitioner's applications to review the determinations of respondent comptroller that denied petitioner's requests to set aside contracts entered into by respondents for the collection of solid waste.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought to set aside two contracts for the collection of solid waste entered into between respondent waste management authority and two correctional facilities upon the ground that respondent lacked the statutory authority to enter into the contracts. Petitioner argued that respondent exercised its power solely by legislative grant, and absent an express legislative delegation of power, its actions were ultra vires and void. It contended that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 2051-e(9) authorized respondent to contract with state agencies for the purpose of receiving, treating, and disposing of solid waste but omitted the word collecting from the enumerated powers, leaving respondent without authority to enter into underlying contracts with facilities for collection of waste. The appellate court held that interpreting § 2051-e (9) in that fashion would have effectively nullified those provisions of the statute authorizing respondent to collect solid waste in the first instance. The court ruled that the authority to contract with the state or its agencies for such services was found in N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law §§ 2051-e(12) and (18), and the order dismissing the petitions was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the supreme court dismissing the petitions were affirmed.

McGrath v. Town Bd. of N. Greenbush


82572


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


254 A.D.2d 614; 678 N.Y.S.2d 834; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11191



October 22, 1998, Decided  
October 22, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as dismissed the petitions/complaints of petitioner Kathleen A. McGrath; said petitioner's motion for a preliminary injunction is denied and matter remitted to the Supreme Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants landowners challenged an order by the Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed their petitions for declaratory, and injunctive relief in the combined zoning proceedings. Landowners claimed that appellee zoning board violated constitutional limitations regarding land use and zoning changes.


OVERVIEW: The landowners claimed that rezoning of property was unconstitutional, and dismissal of their complaints was in error as they each had standing and had met all requirements of the preliminary injunction. The court disagreed. The court found that one of the landowners failed to specifically allege actions which were sufficient to demonstrate that the harm it would have suffered upon rezoning, would have been different from that of the public at large. It said that unless the allegations were contained in the complaint, no standing was found and dismissal was proper. However, the landowners' allegations of traffic congestion and its consequential increase in noise were potential noneconomic environmental concerns within the zone of interest which established standing to challenge the enactment of Local Law No. 22. Accordingly, the court reinstated the landowners' complaints. The court said that the zoning board only had to prove the zoning amendment was adopted for a legitimate governmental purpose and that there was a reasonable relation between the end sought, and the means used to achieve that end in order for the rezoning to withstand attack.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment, reversed the dismissal of the landowners' complaint, but denied the landowners' request for injunctive relief. The court also affirmed the remainder of the judgment, and remitted to the supreme court for further proceedings.

Zobel v. New York State Bd. of Elections


83201


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


254 A.D.2d 520; 678 N.Y.S.2d 794; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10649



October 9, 1998, Decided  
October 9, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidates filed an independent nominating petition with the respondent state board of elections. The board invalidated a number of signatures, and the petition then contained less than the 15,000 required to be valid under N.Y. Elec. Law § 6-142. The candidates filed suit to validate the petition, arguing the statute was unconstitutional. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) dismissed the suit. The candidates appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the candidates argued that the statute was unconstitutional because the requirement that an independent nominating petition include the town or city in which each signor or subscribing witness resided, imposed an undue burden upon the electoral process. The appellate court held that the town or city requirement imposed by N.Y. Elec. Law § 6-140 was not unconstitutional. Strict compliance with the town or city requirement served the legitimate purpose of facilitating the discovery of fraud and allowed the rapid and efficient verification of signatures within the restrictive time periods imposed by the Election Law. Moreover, the reasonable, nondiscriminatory requirement imposed by the statute did not restrict access to the State ballot or place an unconstitutional burden on the candidates' U.S. Const. amend. I and XIV rights to associate. Therefore, the court found that the petition contained less than 15,000 valid signatures and was invalid.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

The court remitted the matter to the trial court for the entry of a judgment making the appropriate declarations.

Brukhman v. Giuliani


1759


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


253 A.D.2d 653; 678 N.Y.S.2d 45; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9478



September 17, 1998, Decided  
September 17, 1998, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted plaintiffs' motion for class certification and preliminary injunctive relief staying enforcement of the Work Experience Program, and denied defendants' cross-motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were public assistance recipients of either home relief or aid to families with dependent children who had been required to participate in New York City Department of Social Services' Work Experience Program. Plaintiffs claimed that the nature of the tasks to which they were assigned effectively made them city employees and that, as such, they were entitled to equivalent wages and benefits. Further, plaintiffs claimed that defendants committed several statutory and constitutional violations by denying them compensation equivalent to comparable city employees. Defendants denied that plaintiffs were not meant to be employees performing regular work in a regular manner and that the nature of their participation rendered them eligible for prevailing rates of compensation. On appeal, the court granted defendants' cross-motion and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint. The court found that there could not be any unconstitutional taking resulting from wage restrictions because labor was not a property right. Further, the court found that there was a rational distinction between qualifying public employees selected from civil service lists or by provisional hiring and plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the supreme court's order denying defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint, on the law, without costs. The court granted defendants' cross-motion and dismissed the complaint.

Schulz v. Town Bd.


81633


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


253 A.D.2d 956; 677 N.Y.S.2d 826; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9533



September 17, 1998, Decided  
September 17, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Warren County (New York), which granted the motions to dismiss filed by defendants, municipal town and associated citizens (town), in the declaratory judgment action filed by the citizen which sought a declaration that a resolution was unconstitutional because the town was without authority to close a section of a public highway.


OVERVIEW: The town adopted a resolution, pursuant to N.Y. High. Law § 171(2), to close a section of a public highway that had become unnecessary and useless. The citizen sought to reopen the highway and filed an action, which sought a declaration that the municipal town was without authority under § 171(2) to close the highway and that the resolution was unconstitutional. The town filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, and the trial court granted the motion. The citizen sought review, and the court affirmed on appeal. The court held that the resolution was not a true legislative enactment, rather, it was a quasi-legislative act. Thus, the court found that the citizen's claim was capable of being resolved pursuant to a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7803(3) proceeding because the citizen's claim was essentially that the resolution was affected by an error of law. The court ruled that the action was, therefore, barred by the applicable four-month statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order that granted the municipal town's motion to dismiss the citizen's action seeking a declaration that the resolution was unconstitutional.

Capers v. Giuliani


1083


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


253 A.D.2d 630; 677 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9331; 1998 OSHD (CCH) P31,662



September 10, 1998, Decided  
September 10, 1998, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of New York County (New York) granted the motion for injunctive relief, filed by plaintiff public-assistance recipients. The Supreme Court also granted them certification as a class. The motion by defendants, the city mayor and related parties (city), to dismiss the complaint was denied. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The public-assistance recipients were assigned, under New York City's Work Experience Program (WEP), to clean streets and remove debris in WEP assignments. As participants in the WEP, the public-assistance recipients were considered public employees, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 330(5). The public-assistance recipients alleged that their work conditions exposed them to possible death or serious physical harm. The public-assistance recipients did not avail themselves of the administrative procedures set forth in New York's Labor Law. Therefore, the court found that dismissal was procedurally required. The public-assistance recipients were required to exhaust all possibilities of obtaining relief through administrative channels before appealing to the courts. Although there were exceptions to that rule, including when an agency action was unconstitutional, or when an agency went wholly beyond its grant of power, or when resort to an administrative remedy would have been futile, none of the exceptions applied to the public-assistance recipients. In addition, injunctive relief was not warranted when there was no fact-finding done to support that form of relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of injunctive relief and the certification of the class, both of which had been in favor of the public-assistance recipients. The court reversed the city's cross-motion to dismiss the complaint. The court dismissed the complaint and directed the clerk of court to enter judgment in favor of the city, dismissing the complaint against it.

Port Jefferson Health Care Facility v. Wing


97-08519


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


253 A.D.2d 548; 677 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9215


June 12, 1998, Argued  
August 31, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised March 16, 1999.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is affirmed insofar as appealed from, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, social services commissioner and related parties, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York) that granted respondent residential health care facilities' (RHCF) motion for summary judgment, and declared that the additional tax assessments on the RHCF's gross receipts pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii), (iii), violated the RHCF's rights to equal protection.


OVERVIEW: The social services commissioner challenged the trial court order that declared that the additional tax assessments on the RHCF's gross receipts pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d were unconstitutional as violative of the RHCF's rights to equal protection. The court affirmed the trial court order. The court held that the challenged assessments were not uniformly imposed and that they disparately impacted medical facilities, such as the RHCF's, that did not cater to clients on Medicaid, despite the fact that all facilities were engaged in the same activity and were required by Federal regulations to provide the same quality of care. The court found that the majority of the revenues of facilities that had a large Medicaid population were not subject to the additional assessments, while RHCFs that had a large proportion of gross receipts from non-Medicaid sources, shouldered the burden of the assessments. The court found that the favorable treatment of facilities with a greater proportion of Medicaid patients was not pursuant to a reasonable distinction between all facilities and violated the RHCF's rights to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order that granted the RHCF's motion for summary judgment and that declared the statute imposing additional assessments on the gross receipts of RHCF's unconstitutional.
