Schulz v. New York State Legislature


81812


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


244 A.D.2d 126; 676 N.Y.S.2d 237; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8682



July 30, 1998, Decided  
July 30, 1998, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 21, 1998.
Certiorari Denied May 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 3274.
Related proceeding at Schulz v. New York State Legislature, 252 A.D.2d 896, 676 N.Y.S.2d 311, 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8692 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 1998)
Appeal dismissed by Schulz v. New York State Legislature, 92 N.Y.2d 917, 703 N.E.2d 267, 1998 N.Y. LEXIS 3134, 680 N.Y.S.2d 456 (1998)
Appeal dismissed by Schulz v. New York State Legislature, 92 N.Y.2d 946, 704 N.E.2d 229, 1998 N.Y. LEXIS 3681, 681 N.Y.S.2d 476 (1998)
Appeal denied by Schulz v. New York State Legislature, 92 N.Y.2d 818, 707 N.E.2d 444, 1998 N.Y. LEXIS 4348, 684 N.Y.S.2d 489 (1998)



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers challenged a decision from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendants, legislature, speaker of the assembly, senate majority leader, and governor (state), city, and the New York City Transitional Finance Authority (authority), in an action to have the New York City Transitional Finance Authority Act (Act) and N.Y. State Fin. Law § 123-b(1) declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Under the Act, the authority was authorized to issue bonds to fund capital improvement projects in the city. The authority's primary resource was personal income taxes. The taxpayers filed a declaratory judgment action against the state. The trial court allowed the city and the authority to intervene. The trial court granted summary judgment to the state, the city, and the authority. The court affirmed. The court held that to the extent that the taxpayers contended that the Act's financing scheme ran afoul of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11 and deprived them of a state-wide public referendum, each taxpayer was afforded voter standing. The court found that the payments made to the authority under the Act were permissible gifts and did not obligate either the city or the state to repay any debt incurred by the authority. The taxpayers failed to meet their heavy burden of demonstrating beyond a reasonable doubt that, pursuant to the Act, the state and city were legally obligated to the holders of the bonds in the event of the authority's default and, thus, incurred debt in contravention of the relevant constitutional mandates.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the state, the city, and the Authority in the taxpayers' declaratory judgment action.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Voter Standing - Challenge to Constitutionality of New York City Transitional Finance Authority Act

1. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the New York City Transitional Finance Authority Act (Public Authorities Law § 2799-aa et seq.), Supreme Court erred in concluding that voter standing should be limited only to those plaintiffs who reside in New York City. While it is true that plaintiffs allege that the Act is nothing more than an elaborate attempt to evade the constitutional limitations on debt incursion imposed upon the City by NY Constitution, article VIII, § 4, plaintiffs' over-all challenge to the Act is much broader in scope. Thus, to the extent that plaintiffs contend that the financing scheme imposed by the Act runs afoul of NY Constitution, article VII, § 11 and deprives them of a State-wide public referendum in this regard, each plaintiff here should be afforded voter standing on that basis; such standing does not, however, extend to plaintiffs' claims that the Act violates NY Constitution, article VII, § 8 (gift or loan of State money or credit), article VIII, § 12 (limits upon local indebtedness) or article X, § 5 (restriction on assumption of obligations of a public corporation), as such provisions are not linked to any voting rights. Moreover, plaintiffs' arguments that they have citizen-taxpayer standing pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b are without merit since it is clear that regardless of the manner in which plaintiffs' claims are styled, they essentially are challenging the validity of the Authority's issuance of bonds to fund capital improvements projects identified in New York City, and the plain language of section 123-b precludes standing under such circumstances.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Contracting State Debt without Public Referendum - Debt Contracted by Public Authorities

2. In an action challenging the constitutionality of the New York City Transitional Finance Authority Act (Public Authorities Law § 2799-aa et seq.), plaintiffs' constitutional claims are premised upon the erroneous belief that any debt incurred by the Authority pursuant to the Act is a debt of the State and the City. The basis for this belief is plaintiffs' assertion that the State and City are obligated, through the payments made to the Authority by the Comptroller, to fund the debt service on the bonds issued by the Authority. Plaintiffs' argument on this point, however, ignores not only the plain language of Public Authorities Law § 2799-gg (10) and § 2799-ii, but also the decisions in Wein v City of New York (36 NY2d 610) and Schulz v State of New York (84 NY2d 231, cert denied 513 US 1127), which are entirely dispositive of this matter. A review of the funding mechanisms at issue in Wein and Schulz, each of which contained language virtually identical to that set forth in Public Authorities Law § 2799-gg (10), plainly reveals that the payments made by the Comptroller are "permissible gifts" to the Authority. Plaintiffs have failed to meet their heavy burden of demonstrating beyond a reasonable doubt that, pursuant to the Act, the State and City are legally obligated to the holders of the bonds in the event of the Authority's default and, thus, have incurred debt in contravention of the relevant constitutional mandates.

 

229 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Mary L. v. State Dep't of Soc. Servs.


81846


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


244 A.D.2d 133; 676 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8339



July 16, 1998, Decided  
July 16, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, alleged child abuser and union, appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, which dismissed an application to expunge the alleged child abuser's name from the State Central Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment (register). The 1996 amendments to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 422, known as the Elisa's Law Child Protective Services Reform Act of 1996 (Act), were challenged.


OVERVIEW: The act provided that unfounded reports of suspected child abuse, previously expunged or destroyed were retained and legally sealed by the register. N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 422(5)(L 1996, ch. 12, §§ 8-12), to help detect and investigate a "pattern of abuse." The alleged child abuser was informed by respondent State of New York Department of Social Services (department) that she was the subject of a suspected child abuse report received by the register after the effective date of the Act. The report was unfounded and was legally sealed. Petitioners sought the expungement of the unfounded report and to permanently enjoin the department from retaining unfounded reports as violating constitutional rights. The trial court dismissed the amended petition. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the Act did not violate due process rights because petitioners did not satisfy the "stigma plus" test of infringement because maintenance of sealed unfounded reports was not sufficient "stigma." The court also found that substantive due rights were not violated because no "fundamental right" was implicated and the Act was reasonable. Last, equal protection rights were not violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the application by petitioners to expunge the name of the alleged child abuser from the register, in favor of the department.


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment - Retention of Unfounded Reports of Abuse - Procedural Due Process

1. Amendments to the Child Protective Services Reform Act of 1996, whereby unfounded reports of suspected child abuse or maltreatment, previously expunged and destroyed, are retained and legally sealed in order to assist child protective workers to detect and investigate a "pattern of abuse" (see, Social Services Law § 422 [5]), do not violate the procedural due process rights of petitioner, an aide for the Division of Youth who was the subject of a report of suspected child abuse which was determined to be unfounded. Although an individual is stigmatized by being branded as a child abuser, and is stigmatized when an indicated report of abuse or maltreatment is placed on the Central Register, because prospective employers have access to the list, there is not the same level of stigma (if any) attached to the maintenance of sealed unfounded reports. Furthermore, the concern about future potential employers is not implicated because such employers only have access to indicated reports. In any event, even if there is a stigma attached by merely maintaining such reports as unfounded and sealed in the Central Register, petitioners herein did not demonstrate that the "plus" requirement of the "stigma plus" test, which requires a tangible element in addition to damage to one's good name, has been met in that there has been no specific deprivation of an employment opportunity.

Social Services - Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment - Retention of Unfounded Reports of Abuse - Substantive Due Process

2. Amendments to the Child Protective Services Reform Act of 1996, whereby unfounded reports of suspected child abuse or maltreatment, previously expunged and destroyed, are retained and legally sealed in order to assist child protective workers to detect and investigate a "pattern of abuse" (see, Social Services Law § 422 [5]), do not violate the substantive due process rights of petitioner, an aide for the Division of Youth who was the subject of a report of suspected child abuse which was determined to be unfounded, since no "fundamental right" is implicated and the Act is reasonable in relation to its subject and adopted in the interests of the community. Maintaining the sealed, unfounded reports for purposes of subsequent investigations by the Department of Social Services is rationally related to the community interest of improving the safety of children in both family and institutional settings. In any event, petitioners have failed to establish that the Act effectuates an arbitrary deprivation of liberty.

Social Services - Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment - Retention of Unfounded Reports of Abuse - Equal Protection

3. Amendments to the Child Protective Services Reform Act of 1996, whereby unfounded reports of suspected child abuse or maltreatment, previously expunged and destroyed, are retained and legally sealed in order to assist child protective workers to detect and investigate a "pattern of abuse" (see, Social Services Law § 422 [5]), do not violate the equal protection rights of petitioner, an aide for the Division of Youth who was the subject of a report of suspected child abuse which was determined to be unfounded, where petitioners argue that because the Act only applies to reports registered after a certain date, petitioner is being treated differently from employees who were the subjects of unfounded reports prior to the effective date of the amendments, since the Legislature has the inherent ability to amend its enactments. To the extent that petitioners argue that the only fair way to apply these amendments would be to do so retroactively, this argument not only raises notice issues as to those individuals, but would also be impossible to administer in that all prior unfounded reports were destroyed. Speculation as to the consequences in the event that the Act is illegally administered cannot satisfy petitioners' burden of establishing the unconstitutionality of the statute.

Raum v. Restaurant Assocs.


61421


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


252 A.D.2d 369; 675 N.Y.S.2d 343; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8126



July 9, 1998, Decided  
July 9, 1998, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff executor filed an individual wrongful-death claim against defendant corporations in the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), alleging a homosexual partner had the right to bring the claim under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-4.1. The corporations filed motions for partial summary judgment, which the trial court granted. The executor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The executor filed an individual wrongful-death claim against the corporations. The corporations filed motions for partial summary judgment, contending that homosexual partners did not have a right to sue. The trial court granted the corporations' motions and dismissed the executor's claim. The executor argued that it would be unconstitutional to interpret the relevant statutes to deny homosexual life partners the right to sue. The court held that the wrongful-death statute, N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-4.1, did not give an individual not married to a decedent, other than certain blood relatives, a right to bring a wrongful-death action. The court found that § 5-4.1 operated without regard to sexual orientation in that unmarried couples living together, whether heterosexual or homosexual, similarly lacked the right to bring a wrongful-death action, and as such, the statute did not discriminate against same-sex partners in spousal-type relationships.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing the executor's wrongful-death claim against the corporations was affirmed.

 

231 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Grumet v. Pataki


81319


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


244 A.D.2d 31; 675 N.Y.S.2d 662; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8139



July 9, 1998, Decided  
July 9, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the boards of education of two school districts, state officials, and state agencies, challenged an order of the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) which granted a motion by plaintiff citizen taxpayers for summary judgment and declared unconstitutional 1997 N.Y. Laws 390, which created a school district serving Hasidic children. The boards of education also challenged an order denying their motion for a change of venue.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted 1997 N.Y. Laws 390 as its third attempt to create a school district providing special education services to the children of a Hasidic community after two previous statutes had been held to violate U.S. Const. amend. I. The citizen taxpayers filed an action against two boards of education, state officials, and state agencies alleging that ch. 390 violated the Establishment Clause. The boards of education appealed from the denial of their motion for a change of venue and the boards, officials and agencies appealed from an order granting the citizen taxpayers' motion for summary judgment. The court affirmed both orders. The venue order was within the trial court's discretion because there were conflicting statutory provisions controlling venue. Chapter 390 was not a neutral law curing the constitutional deficiencies of its predecessors. It applied to only two of the state's 1,545 municipalities and the likelihood that it would apply to other municipalities in the future was remote at best. Despite its facial neutrality, the statute was unconstitutional because its primary effect was to endorse the religious beliefs of the residents of the challenged district.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court orders denying the motion of the boards of education for a change of venue and granting the citizen taxpayers' motion for summary judgment. The statute which created a school district serving Hasidic children violated the Establishment Clause of the U.S. Constitution.


HEADNOTES

Trial - Place of Trial - Action against School District and State Officials

1. Supreme Court, Albany County, did not abuse its discretion in denying defendants' motion for a change of venue to Orange County where plaintiffs, as citizen-taxpayers, are challenging the constitutionality of a State statute establishing a school district which would primarily, if not solely, benefit a sect of Hasidic Jews. There are conflicting statutory provisions: actions against school boards shall be brought in the county where the school district is located ( CPLR 504 [2]); however, State Finance Law § 123-c (1) requires that a citizen taxpayer's challenge be brought either where the disbursement of funds occurred or is likely to occur or where the State official has his or her principal office; and CPLR 509 states that venue, unless changed, shall be in the county chosen by the plaintiff. Here, five of the eight named defendants are State agencies or State officials with principal offices in Albany County, and plaintiffs chose Albany County. Accordingly, Supreme Court did not abuse its discretion in denying a change of venue.

Constitutional Law - Establishment of Religion - Validity of Statute Permitting Creation of School District Solely within Municipality to Meet Required Educational Needs - Governmental Neutrality

2. Chapter 390 of the Laws of 1997, which is the latest in a series of attempts to create a school district which would provide special educational services to a sect of Hasidic Jews, is not a neutral law which cured the constitutional deficiencies of its predecessors, since it lacks the neutralitytoward religion mandated by the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution. Chapter 390 applies to only two of the State's 1,545 municipalities and, thus, can hardly be said to be a truly religious-neutral law of general applicability available to a broad array of eligible municipalities. Moreover not only does the new statute apply to too few existing municipalities, its application to other municipalities in the future is speculative: despite provisions which extend the benefits of the statute to existing municipalities for which conditions may change in the future and to new municipalities yet to be formed, the "theoretical possibility" that other municipalities may qualify under the new statute in the future is remote at best. A review of the history of this legislation leads to the conclusion that chapter 390, as its predecessors, clearly provides an impermissible preference to the religious community in response to its desire to provide its handicapped children with special education services in an exclusively sectarian environment. Accordingly, chapter 390, despite its facial neutrality, violates the second, "endorsement", prong of the test set forth in Lemon v Kurtzman (403 US 602) as its primary effect endorses the sect's religious beliefs.

 

232 of 992 DOCUMENTS


St. Joseph's Hosp. Health Ctr. v. Department of Health


0642.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


247 A.D.2d 136; 677 N.Y.S.2d 194; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8429



July 8, 1998, Decided  
July 8, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospitals filed an action against defendant state department of health (DOH) seeking a judgment declaring a portion of the regulation promulgated by the DOH regarding Medicaid hospital reimbursement for bad debts and costs of charity care to be unconstitutional and contrary to N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 2807-a(8)(e), 2808-c(4)(3). The Supreme Court of Onondaga County (New York) granted summary judgment in favor of the hospitals.


OVERVIEW: The DOH challenged the summary judgment. The court held that the regulation was valid and enforceable. The court determined that the maintenance of effort provisions in the regulation were constitutional and valid. The court decided that the reallocation effected by the maintenance of effort regulation did not constitute retroactive ratemaking and the regulation did not operate retroactively. The court also held that the regulation was consistent with the statutory mandate and the DOH did not exceed its authority in authorizing a reallocation based upon a percentage change in need, rather than an actual change in need. The court deferred to the interpretation of the DOH and determined that the interpretations made were consistent with legislative intent and the policy underlying the regulation. The court concluded that the DOH made the requisite annual reviews and definitive findings as required. Finally, the court held that the determination of DOH to reallocate bad debt and cost of charity care funds exceeded the DOH's powers and was arbitrary and capricious.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the permanent injunction and all prior orders of restraint and granted judgment in favor of the DOH declaring that the maintenance of effort regulations were valid. The court vacated the order that the state had to reimburse the hospitals and granted summary judgment in favor of the DOH on the remaining issues with the exception of the reallocation of funds, which were ordered to be recalculated and redistributed.


HEADNOTES

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation

1. A portion of the regulation promulgated by respondent Department of Health (DOH) of the State of New York regarding Medicaid hospital reimbursement for bad debts and the costs of charity care (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]; [p] [7]) is neither unconstitutional nor contrary to the enabling statutes (Public Health Law § 2807-a [8] [e]; § 2808-c [4] [e]); moreover, DOH's implementation of that regulation is not arbitrary and capricious, or contrary to law.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Retroactive Rate-Making

2. A maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, is neither unconstitutional nor invalid on the ground that it retroactively establishes Medicaid reimbursement rates in violation of Public Health Law § 2807 (7). The initial BDCC allocation, which is part of the reimbursement rate for inpatient services of each general hospital for the rate years 1983 and 1984, was established prior to each rate year based upon the amount of the hospital's BDCC need as indicated by each hospital's financial and statistical data for base year 1981. The reallocation scheme implemented by the MOE regulation does not set the reimbursement rate; it is an adjustment of the rate based upon the effort of each hospital in maintaining its level of inpatient services provided to BDCC patients during the rate year. Adjustments to an established rate that are contemplated by the statutory reimbursement scheme do not constitute impermissible retroactive rate-making. Moreover, the MOE regulation did not operate retroactively: it was promulgated in March 1984, and because each hospital is not required to submit its financial and statistical data regarding the cost of providing inpatient services until the first of May of the year following the rate year, DOH could not begin the process of conducting an annual review to determine whether a reallocation is warranted for rate year 1983 until after May 1, 1994; thus, no step in the reallocation process was undertaken until well after promulgation of the 1984 MOE regulation.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - "Need"

3. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, it cannot be said that reallocating regional BDCC pool funds based on "need" is contrary to statutory mandates that the funds be reallocated based on "a change in the proportional amounts of [BDCC] provided" (Public Health Law § 2807-a [8] [e]; § 2808-c [4] [e]), since there is no substantive difference between the terms "need" and "provided". The statutory reimbursement methodologies are neither clear nor unambiguous insofar as they appear to require definitive findings based on proportional changes in BDCC services provided and it cannot be said that the regulation is invalid because it requires findings based on changes in BDCC need. Because the object of the annual review is BDCC "need" and the definitive findings must be based upon the annual review, the meaning of the word "provided" is unclear; BDCC services "provided" is not the subject of the annual review and could not be the basis of the definitive finding mandated by the statutes. Rather, the statutory language should be construed to require definitive findings based upon proportional changes in BDCC need provided.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Agency Interpretation

4. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, it cannot be said that the Commissioner of Health exceeded his statutory authority in authorizing a reallocation based upon a percentage change in BDCC need, rather than an actual change in need, where the MOE regulations were adopted by the Council on Health Care Financing and were approved by the Commissioner. Based on the involvement of the Council in the development of the statutory methodologies and the knowledge of the Council and DOH of the methodology involved in the operational practices of allocating and reallocating BDCC pool funds, courts should defer to their interpretation of the statute since they are the agencies responsible for development and administration of the statute, and their interpretation is not unreasonable or irrational.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Intrasector Reallocations

5. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, it cannot be said that the MOE regulations are invalid insofar as they permit intrasector reallocation, or that the enabling statutes permit only reallocation of BDCC funds between the public and private sector hospitals, since neither statutory methodology uses the term "between" or "intersector reallocation", and neither may be read to limit reallocations to intersector changes in BDCC need; rather, the statutes simply require a reallocation of regional BDCC pool funds within each region if there has been a proportional change in BDCC need provided by major public hospitals and private hospitals. Thus, with respect to intersector and intrasector reallocation, the statutes are ambiguous. Accordingly, courts should defer to the interpretation of the Council on Health Care Financing and Commissioner of Health, since their interpretation, as reflected in the MOE regulations, is consistent with legislative intent and the policy underlying the concept of maintenance of effort. Reallocating BDCC pool funds only between sectors would not accomplish that purpose and would not discourage private hospitals from shifting the BDCC burden to other private hospitals. In those regions without a major public hospital, there would be no reallocation at all; hospitals in those regions, therefore, would be treated differently from hospitals in regions that included a major public hospital. Thus, the decision to include intrasector reallocation is not irrational and should be upheld.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Annual Review

6. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, it cannot be said that because DOH never conducted an annual review nor made definitive findings with respect to the rate years 1983 through 1987, the reallocation determinations made by DOH in 1994 with respect to those rate years are invalid and arbitrary and capricious, since DOH made the requisite annual reviews and definitive findings contemplated by the statutes and regulations. There is no requirement that the reviews be conducted annually; rather, the annual review requirement is satisfied by conducting a separate review for each rate year once adequate data essential to such a review becomes available. Further, the statutes do not specify the form or manner in which definitive findings must be made. DOH disseminated data in 1994 to each hospital showing proportional changes for each rate year in the amount of BDCC provided by that hospital and other hospitals in the region as well as proportional changes between the public and private hospital sectors; although not labeled "definitive findings", such data constitute the requisite definitive findings. Although plaintiffs maintain that the failure of DOH to respond to Freedom of Information Law requests and discovery demands for production of definitive findings establishes that no such findings exist, the failure of DOH to respond is attributable to its position that there is no discrete document entitled "definitive findings" and does not constitute a concession that such findings do not exist.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Delay

7. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals, it cannot be said that because DOH delayed implementation of MOE reallocation for 10 years, the reallocation of BDCC funds for rate years 1983 through 1987 should be permanently enjoined, since the statutory methodologies and the MOE regulations contain no time limit for the implementation of MOE reallocations: the timing of implementation of that process is within the agency's discretion, and the mere passage of time is insufficient to warrant judicial intervention in the administrative process. Plaintiffs have shown no prejudice stemming from the delay and may still challenge DOH's interpretation of the methodologies as reflected in the 1984 MOE regulation notwithstanding that documents relating to the adoption of that regulation either are in long-term storage or have been destroyed: the request for those documents was made in 1994; plaintiffs could have mounted a challenge to the 1984 MOE regulation in 1984 but did not do so, and there is no proof that the documentation was unavailable in 1984 or for a reasonable period thereafter. Also it cannot be said that potential witnesses are unavailable to testify: although several former financial officers have left their positions, there is no proof that those witnesses are unavailable or that the testimony of the one deceased former financial officer is critical to a challenge to the MOE reallocation. Moreover, the assertion by some hospitals that they have destroyed source documents relevant to data submitted to DOH fails to demonstrate that they are unable to contest DOH's calculations.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Annual Review

8. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, DOH, in reallocating BDCC pool funds, improperly reimbursed certain hospitals more than 100% of their BDCC need, in violation of the 1984 MOE regulation. That error, however, can be remedied by recouping the amount of overpayment and revising the reallocation made to other hospitals. The fact that several hospitals have terminated operations is inherent in the reallocation process, and plaintiffs have not shown that the exclusion of the defunct institutions from the reallocation process will result in a shortfall in the funds to be redistributed to general hospitals who have maintained their BDCC effort. Further, plaintiffs have not shown that they will be prejudiced by having to provide additional moneys to eliminate the shortfall, or that there is a significant difference between the amount to be recouped from and the amount to be redistributed to defunct hospitals. Plaintiffs have failed to establish that, to the extent that DOH was required as a practical matter to rely upon some data that was erroneous or unaudited, such data will produce an unreasonable reallocation or that the entire process is so fundamentally flawed as to be arbitrary and capricious.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - Due Process

9. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, there is no merit to the contention that plaintiffs have a due process right to the finality of the initial BDCC distributions that they received, since there is no protected property interest in those Medicaid payments that may be adjusted at a later time, either as the result of an audit or a statutory adjustment to the rate. Also without merit is the contention that plaintiffs have been denied procedural due process: DOH notified each hospital in January 1994 of the data to be utilized in the MOE reallocation computations and requested each hospital to inform it of any errors in that data; some hospitals did report errors and corrections were made. Administrative review is not necessary when the methodology itself or the promulgation of a regulation has been challenged, and the availability of CPLR article 78 review to challenge the actual reallocation satisfies due process requirements.

Health - Medicaid Reimbursement Payments - Hospital Reimbursement for Bad Debts and Charity Care - Validity of Regulation - Maintenance of Effort - State-Wide Pool Funds for Bad Debts and Charity Care

10. In a challenge to a maintenance of effort (MOE) regulation (10 NYCRR 86-1.11 [g] [7]), which is designed to encourage general hospitals to maintain their efforts at providing care to those unable or unwilling to pay for care and to discourage hospitals from shifting the financial burdens associated with bad debt and charity care (BDCC) to other hospitals by authorizing the State Department of Health (DOH) to reallocate regional pool funds if it finds that there has been a change in the proportional amount of BDCC need provided by the hospitals, DOH improperly reallocated, pursuant to a revised statutory methodology, State-wide BDCC pool funds for the rate years 1986 and 1987 in the same manner that it reallocated regional BDCC pool funds. Where the statute is unambiguous (see, Public Health Law § 2807-a [8] [e]; [16] [a]; [24] [a]), it is inappropriate to defer to the agency's interpretation; moreover, the interpretation of the agency is contrary to its own regulations, which do not authorize such a reallocation. Even if it is assumed that reallocation of State-wide BDCC pool funds is consistent with the policy underlying the MOE statute and that the failure of the Legislature to authorize the reallocation of State-wide BDCC pool funds was an oversight, if any unsought consequences result, the Legislature is best suited to evaluate and resolve them. Thus, because the MOE statute and regulations do not authorize reallocation of State-wide BDCC pool funds, the determination of DOH to reallocate those funds exceeded the agency's powers, and was arbitrary and capricious. However, neither the improper reallocation of State-wide BDCC pool funds nor the improper distribution to a hospital of more than 100% of its BDCC need warrants the issuance of an injunction permanently restraining implementation of the MOE reallocation scheme, since those improprieties may be corrected by a remittal to the agency for a proper recalculation.

 

233 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Rudder v. Pataki


80230


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


246 A.D.2d 183; 675 N.Y.S.2d 653; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7989



July 2, 1998, Decided  
July 2, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a coalition director and various organizations, appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, the Governor and the Director of Regulatory Reform (Director), and dismissed the coalition director and the organizations' complaint.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 405.28(d) required each hospital in the state, except rural or non-urban hospitals, to have an organized social work department under the direction of a qualified medical social worker. The Director disapproved a proposed amendment to § 405.28(d) that sought to require social work department directors to hold master's degrees in social work. The coalition director and the organizations filed an action against the Governor and the Director, challenging the constitutionality of the Governor's executive order that created the Director's position. On appeal, the court held that the coalition director and the organizations lacked standing to bring their action. The coalition director claimed standing as a citizen taxpayer under N.Y. State Fin. Law § 123-b, but she failed to sufficiently specify any alleged improper spending by the Director's office. The organizations failed to allege any specific harm other than generalized statements that the existing version of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 405.28(d) somehow diminished patient care. Therefore, any anticipated harm to the organizations was remote and highly speculative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court in favor of the Governor and the Director.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Citizen-Taxpayer - Challenge to Executive Order Implementing Regulatory Oversight

1. In an action challenging the constitutionality of an Executive Order establishing the position of Director of Regulatory Reform, which would review rules and regulations proposed by State agencies and then submit an evaluation of the proposal to a committee which may then either authorize publication, disapproval or revision of the proposal, the individual plaintiff lacks standing as a citizen-taxpayer under State Finance Law § 123-b where, other than general references to improper spending by defendant Office of Regulatory Reform, plaintiff has failed to designate with any specificity either the amount of funds to be expended or the manner in which the expenditure will occur, since such specificity is required in order to maintain standing under the statute.

Parties - Standing - Organizational Standing - Challenge to Executive Order Implementing Regulatory Oversight

2. In an action challenging the constitutionality of an Executive Order establishing the position of Director of Regulatory Reform, which would review rules and regulations proposed by State agencies and then submit an evaluation of the proposal to a committee which may then either authorize publication, disapproval or revision of the proposal, the organizational plaintiffs, a potpourri of agencies involving social workers, nursing home advocates and other advocates for the aged and disabled, as well as the Women's City Club of New York and the League of Women Voters, lack standing where their immediate challenge arises out of the Director's disapproval of a regulation which would have required hospitals to have an organized social work department under the direction of a medical social worker who would be required to have a Master's degree in social work from an accepted educational program. Although the present regulations provide that a qualified medical social worker would have to be replaced by a person holding a Master's degree in social work, there is a lack of any specific harm to any of the plaintiff organizations other than generalized statements that continuing with the present rule would somehow diminish the care presently available or have a deleterious effect on patients. From a reading of plaintiffs' brief, it is clear that the main contention of plaintiffs is that the executive order vests the Governor's Office of Regulatory Reform with a "virtually limitless" veto power in the rule-making process, a concern which is more appropriately addressed by the Legislature. Since no final determination was made in the challenged proceeding and any anticipated harm to the plaintiffs is remote and highly speculative, plaintiffs have failed to sustain their burden of establishing standing in this matter.

Meadow Farm Realty Corp. v. Pekich


97-02869


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


251 A.D.2d 634; 676 N.Y.S.2d 203; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7866


March 26, 1998, Argued  
June 29, 1998, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is reversed, on the law, with costs, the motion by the defendant Peter A. Pekich, Jr., is denied, the plaintiff's cross motion is granted, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Nassau County, for the entry of an appropriate judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner brought an action against defendants, county and purchaser, seeking to invalidate a deed. The purchaser filed a motion to dismiss, and the landowner brought a motion for summary judgment. The Supreme Court of Nassau County (New York) granted the purchaser's motion to dismiss. The landowner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowner's property was sold in a tax sale. The landowner did not learn of the sale until it planned to sell the property. At that time, the landowner's attorney discovered that the purchaser was the purported owner of record. The trial court dismissed the landowner's complaint on the ground that it was barred by the statute of limitations. Upon review, the court held that there was no basis upon which to dismiss the action because the landowner did not have timely notice. The court held that the landowner was deprived of due process solely due to the county's failure to provide it with constitutionally adequate notice of the tax lien sale.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the purchaser's motion to dismiss the landowner's action to invalidate the deed. The court granted the landowner's motion for summary judgment.

Relin v. Connell


0745.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


251 A.D.2d 1041; 674 N.Y.S.2d 192; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7112



June 10, 1998, Decided  
June 10, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action to criminally prosecute defendant for a capital crime, the state commenced an original N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding and sought to prohibit the enforcement of an order of the Monroe County Court (New York), which had found N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 220.10(5)(e), 220.30(3)(b)(viii), and 220.60(2)(a) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The state argued that the provisions of New York's death penalty statute governing kinds of pleas, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10(5)(e), those governing pleas to part of an indictment or covering other indictments N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.30(3)(b)(viii), and those governing the change of a plea, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.60(2)(a), did not violate defendant's right to a jury trial and therefore were constitutional. The court agreed and held that the sections did not grant defendant the unilateral right to plead guilty and thereby avoid the death penalty, and therefore, they did not needlessly encourage guilty pleas in violation of defendant's right to demand a jury trial. Accordingly, the court granted the portion of the petition that sought an order converting the petition into an action for a declaratory judgment and entered judgment declaring the contested provisions constitutional.


OUTCOME: The state's petition to prohibit the enforcement of the order declaring the contested provisions of the criminal code unconstitutional was granted in part, and the petition was converted into an action for declaratory judgment. Judgment was then granted in favor of the state and the contested provisions were declared constitutional.

Lichtenstein v. Emerson


1448


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


251 A.D.2d 64; 674 N.Y.S.2d 298; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6529



June 9, 1998, Decided  
June 9, 1998, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) that granted defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint by reason, inter alia, of the circumstance that plaintiff's attorney was not, when he commenced the action on plaintiff's behalf, authorized to practice in New York state.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's attorney was found not to have satisfied the condition placed upon the practice of law under N.Y. Jud. Law § 470, by non-New York residents, that they maintain an office for the transaction of law business within the state. The trial court then granted defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint based on this non-compliance. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court decision. The court found that the evidence amply supported the trial court's factual determination. The court found the assertion by plaintiff that his attorney transacted legal business from a small room located in the basement of a restaurant and bar reachable only by passing through a kitchen and down a flight of stairs was most improbable. In addition, the court found that plaintiff's attorney had not reported any New York income for the past five years, had no employees in the state, his name was not posted anywhere on the premises, there was no indication that any of the employees of the restaurant/bar had ever been instructed to accept legal papers, and the attorney had listed a Washington address as his office. The court also held that the § 470 office requirement was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the trial court that granted defendant's motion to dismiss plaintiff's complaint by reason, inter alia, of the circumstance that plaintiff's attorney was not, when he commenced the action on plaintiff's behalf, authorized to practice in New York state.

Cerro v. Town of Kingsbury


81260


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


250 A.D.2d 978; 672 N.Y.S.2d 953; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5691

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As corrected March 22, 1999.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Washington County (New York) entered judgment for respondent town and related parties (town) and granted the town's motions to dismiss in a combined proceeding commenced by petitioner landowner pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78. The landowner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The gravamen of the landowner's complaint and petition was a denial in 1992 by the town of his application to rezone his property to permit him to build a senior citizen mobile home park. The landowner never initiated any challenge to this denial until after the town discovered in 1995 that certain 1986 and 1987 amendments to its zoning laws were never filed with the secretary of state as required by law. To remedy this defect, the state legislature enacted special laws validating the amendments conditioned on the town's compliance with the State Environmental Quality Review Act. The court affirmed and found that to the extent that the landowner challenged the propriety of the town's denial of his rezoning application, the proceeding was clearly time-barred as it was not commenced within four months of the determination. The landowner's claim that the denial of his application constituted a violation of his civil rights was also untimely because it was not asserted within three years of its accrual. The court rejected the landowner's challenges to the town's seeking curative legislation as to the amendments that were never filed with the secretary of state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the landowner's claims were either time-barred or legally deficient.

People v. Adinolfi


96-06278


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


250 A.D.2d 695; 672 N.Y.S.2d 432; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5489


March 30, 1998, Submitted  
May 11, 1998, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court, Suffolk County (New York) that convicting him, upon a guilty plea, of murder in the second degree, burglary in the third degree, attempted burglary in the third degree, and reckless endangerment in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Upon leaving the scene of a burglary, defendant disregarded flashing lights of a responding patrol car and led the police on a high-speed car chase during which an officer was killed. Defendant was subsequently convicted, upon a guilty plea, of murder in the second degree, burglary in the third degree, attempted burglary in the third degree, and reckless endangerment in the first degree. The predicate offense for defendant's felony murder conviction was burglary in the third degree. Prior to pleading guilty, defendant sought dismissal of the felony murder count on the ground that the felony murder statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad because it did not state the specific degrees of burglary that might form the predicate felony. The trial court denied the motion. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that, by enumerating the degrees of some crimes and not others, the legislature made clear that all degrees of burglary could serve as predicate offenses. Moreover, the manner in which defendant attempted to evade the police in his immediate flight from this burglary made it the very type of dangerous felony that the legislature intended to serve as a predicate felony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment convicting defendant, upon a guilty plea, of murder in the second degree, burglary in the third degree, attempted burglary in the third degree, and reckless endangerment in the first degree.

May 11, 1998, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order and judgment is modified by deleting therefrom the provision granting the defendants' respective cross motions for summary judgment and dismissing the complaints and substituting therefor a provision denying the cross motions; as so modified, the order and judgment is affirmed, with one bill of costs to the plaintiff, and the complaints are reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff gas supplier sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which, in consolidated actions for a judgment declaring that certain portions of two town codes were unconstitutional, denied the gas supplier's motion for summary judgment and granted defendant towns' cross motions for summary judgment and dismissed the complaints.


OVERVIEW: The court found, in the gas supplier's action to have certain portions of the town codes declared unconstitutional, that the towns failed to submit evidentiary proof demonstrating that compliance with the respective town codes did not present an "obstacle" to compliance with the federal statute. They further failed to establish that the fees charged by them for compliance with the local regulations were fair. Thus, the towns failed to make the required prima facie showing of their entitlement to summary judgment. Therefore, the supreme court should have denied their respective cross motions for summary judgment. Accordingly, the court modified the judgment by denying the towns' motions, and affirmed the denial of the gas supplier's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the supreme court by deleting the provision of the order granting the towns' motions for summary judgment, denying the motions, and reinstating the complaints. The court affirmed the judgment as modified, in the gas supplier's action seeking a judgment that portions of the town codes were unconstitutional.

Miriam Osborn Mem. Home Ass'n v. Chassin


96-08850


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


240 A.D.2d 143; 669 N.Y.S.2d 613; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2175


September 23, 1997, Argued  
March 2, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended March 5, 1998.
Appeal dismissed by Osborn Mem'l Home Ass'n v. Chassin, 92 N.Y.2d 919, 703 N.E.2d 271, 1998 N.Y. LEXIS 2875, 680 N.Y.S.2d 459 (1998)
Affirmed in part and modified in part by, Remitted by Miriam Osborn Mem. Home Ass'n v. Chassin, 2003 N.Y. LEXIS 1315 (N.Y., June 5, 2003)



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is modified by (1) deleting the provision thereof granting so much of the defendants' motion which was for summary judgment dismissing those branches of the complaint which were for a judgment declaring that Public Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii) is unconstitutional as applied to the plaintiff and to enjoin the defendants from enforcing Public Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii), and substituting therefor a provision denying those branches of the defendants' motion, and (2) deleting the provision thereof denying so much of the plaintiff's [***2] cross motion which was for summary judgment on those branches of the complaint which were for a judgment declaring that Public Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii) is unconstitutional as applied to the plaintiff and to enjoin the defendants from enforcing Public Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii), and substituting therefor a provision granting those branches of the plaintiff's cross motion; as so modified, the order is affirmed, with costs to the appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nursing home sought declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent appellee state from collection of assessments under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d (1990). The Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York) dismissed the complaint and partially granted the state's summary judgment enforcement motion. The nursing home appealed.


OVERVIEW: The nursing home occupied a unique position as an eleemosynary institution because it was neither a Medicaid participant nor one of a limited number of other specially created institutions. It objected to statutory assessments under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d (1990) on constitutional equal protection grounds because it would not benefit from them inasmuch as they were intended to shore up the Medicaid structure. The trial court dismissed the nursing home's complaint and entered an order that required statutory reporting. On appeal the court modified the trial court order and, as modified, affirmed it. The court held that the 0.6 percent assessment of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d (2)(b)(I) (1990), in contrast to the 1.2 percent assessment of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807-d(2)(b)(ii) (1992), was a valid assessment that bore a rational relationship to the legitimate state interest of raising revenues to reduce the budget and Medicaid deficit because the 0.6 percent assessment applied to all nursing facilities, except those exempted, and it was not reimbursable. The 1.2 percent assessment was arbitrary, inconsistent with the statutory purpose, and unequal as applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the 0.6 assessment, but found that the 1.2 assessment could not be applied.


HEADNOTES

Health - Health-Related Facilities - Constitutionality of Statute Imposing Assessment on Gross Receipts of Certain Health-Related Facilities - Equal Protection of Laws Public Health Law § 2807-d (2) (b) (ii), which, in 1992, imposed an additional assessment on residential health care facilities (RHCFs) of 1.2% of gross receipts over and above the initial 1990 assessment of 0.6% in order to deal with budget gaps caused by Medicaid expenses, excepting RHCFs that are financed entirely by charitable contributions or income derived from them and facilities dedicated solely to the care of police, firefighters, volunteer firefighters and emergency service personnel, is unconstitutional as applied to plaintiff, a non-Medicaid, not-for-profit RHCF that does not fit within any of the statutory exemptions. While the initial nonreimbursable 0.6% across-the-board assessment was a valid, constitutionally imposed assessment that bore a rational relationship to the legitimate State interest of raising revenues to reduce the budget and Medicaid deficit, the 1992 amendment deprived plaintiff of equal protection by declaring the 1.2% assessment to be a reimbursable cost in determining a facility's Medicaid rate, thereby leaving plaintiff as the only not-for-profit nursing home in the State that does not get either an exemption or Medicaid reimbursement for the assessments. To meet equal protection criteria, there must be general uniformity of treatment of those similarly situated, the classifications for disparity must be reasonable, and the taxes or assessments imposed must be uniform within the class. The added 1.2% is a palpably arbitrary assessment, inconsonant with the statutory purpose, and grossly unequal when visited exclusively upon plaintiff. By singling out plaintiff to pay the full, unreimbursable 1.2% assessment, the State denied plaintiff equal protection under the Federal and State Constitutions.

Royal Serv., L. L. C. v. Village of Monticello, Inc.


78740


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff car service and intervenor taxi services appealed a decision from the Supreme Court in Sullivan County (New York), which dismissed the car service's action for a declaratory judgment. The car service asserted that the Village ordinance was unconstitutional and the trial court erred in dismissing its cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The car service filed its application for four licenses from the Village, which was denied because the Village had already issued the maximum number of licenses it was allowed pursuant to Monticello, N.Y., Village Code art 1, § 163-2. The court denied the car service's request, dismissed the complaint without addressing the constitutionality of the Village Code, and denied the proposed intervenors' motion as academic. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the car service was not entitled to injunctive relief because it pursued its intended course despite knowledge that licensure and registration were required prior to the operation of the business.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court dismissing the car service's motion for a declaratory judgment seeking to enjoin a city ordinance as unconstitutional.

Pure Air & Water, Inc. v. Davidsen


80150


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


246 A.D.2d 786; 668 N.Y.S.2d 248; 1998 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 294



January 15, 1998, Decided  
January 15, 1998, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner non-profit corporation sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the non-profit corporation's application to review an advisory opinion of respondent Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets (commissioner) that respondent farm had acceptable storage and application procedures for manure.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Right to Farm Law, codified at N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 308, the farm requested that the commissioner issue an opinion regarding the soundness of the farm's manure management program, which would operate to bar any private nuisance action brought by a private party. The non-profit corporation contended that the statute violated the Due Process Clause because it did not provide for notice and a hearing. On appeal, the court held that the non-profit corporation's claim of deprivation of procedural due process necessarily failed where the non-profit corporation identified its property interest as the right to have a court determine the merits of a private nuisance suit. Such a right did not constitute a due process protected interest because the non-profit corporation did not have a vested interest in any rule of the common law. The court further held that the commissioner's determination was not arbitrary or capricious, as it was made in accordance with his responsibilities and fully supported by facts in the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's dismissal of the non-profit corporation's application for review of the commissioner's opinion.

Hynes v. Tomei


97-07253


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


237 A.D.2d 52; 666 N.Y.S.2d 687; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13300


November 26, 1997, Argued  
December 22, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the proceeding is converted into an action for a judgment declaring that CPL 220.10(5)(e), CPL 220.30(3)(b)(vii), and CPL 220.60(2)(a) are constitutional; and it is further, ADJUDGED that CPL 220.10(5)(e), CPL 220.30(3)(b)(vii), and CPL 220.60(2)(a) are declared to be constitutional; and it is further, ORDERED that the motion by the respondent Michael Shane Hale, inter alia, to grant the petition in part and deny it in part, or alternatively, to dismiss the proceeding, is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed an order of the Supreme Court (New York) declaring N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 220.10(5)(e), 220.30(3)(b)(vii), and 220.60(2)(a) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, charged with first-degree murder, argued that plea bargain provisions, which enabled a defendant charged with first-degree murder to avoid any possibility of a death sentence by entering into an agreement with the prosecution, penalized his right to a jury trial, by exposing him to the risk of death only when he exercised that right. The trial judge held that the plea bargain provisions were unconstitutional. The State moved to reargue the judge's order. Defendant and the State reached a plea bargain agreement. The trial judge denied the State's motion to reargue the order and advised defendant and the State that he would not accept their plea bargain agreement unless the State first withdrew the notice of intent to seek the death penalty. On appeal, the court held the plea bargain statutes were constitutional and that a plea bargain was not invalid merely because it was induced by fear of receiving the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The order declaring plea bargain provisions unconstitutional was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer - Prohibition - When Remedy Available - Constitutionality of Statute - Conversion to Declaratory Judgment Action The People's petition pursuant to CPLR article 78 seeking to prohibit respondent Justice from enforcing so much of his orders in a capital murder case as declared unconstitutional the plea bargain provisions of New York's death penalty statute, which enable a defendant charged with the capital crime of murder in the first degree to avoid any possibility of a death sentence by entering into a plea bargain agreement with the People (CPL 220.10 [5] [e]; 220.30 [3] [b] [vii]; 220.60 [2] [a]), must be denied. Because of its extraordinary nature, prohibition is available only where there is a clear legal right, and then only when a court, in cases where judicial authority is challenged, acts or threatens to act without jurisdiction or in excess of its authorized powers. Furthermore, prohibition is not a proper method to determine the constitutionality of a statute. However, the People's alternative request that the proceeding be converted into an action for a declaratory judgment (see, CPLR 103) to declare the challenged plea bargain provisions to be constitutional and valid in all respects is granted. The remedy of a declaratory judgment is available in cases where a constitutional question is involved or the legality or meaning of a statute is in question and no question of fact is involved. Further, a declaratory judgment may be used to attack a criminal court's interlocutory ruling when the ruling has an obvious effect extending far beyond the matter pending before it so that it is likely that the issue will arise again with the same result in other cases.

Crimes - Murder - Constitutionality of Plea Bargain Provisions of Death Penalty Statute The plea bargain provisions of New York's death penalty statute, which enable a defendant charged with the capital crime of murder in the first degree to avoid any possibility of a death sentence by entering into a plea bargain agreement with the People (CPL 220.10 [5] [e]; 220.30 [3] [b] [vii]; 220.60 [2] [a]), do not unconstitutionally penalize a capital defendant's right to a jury trial by exposing such defendant to the risk of death only when exercising that right. Respondent Justice therefore erred in declaring the plea bargain provisions to be unconstitutional in reliance upon the decision of the United States Supreme Court in United States v Jackson (390 US 570). In contrast to the Federal Kidnaping Act found unconstitutional in Jackson, the death penalty statute in New York provides for a bifurcated trial where the defendant's guilt, and the sentence to be imposed, are determined separately by the jury. At the guilt phase, the defendant retains all of his or her constitutional protections, e.g., he or she is not compelled to take the witness stand, and the People must prove guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. At the sentencing phase, the defendant has the opportunity to present "any evidence", including his or her own testimony, "relevant to any mitigating factor" set forth in the statute (CPL 400.27 [6], [9]). Moreover, the plea bargain provisions herein prohibit a capital defendant from pleading guilty, as of right, to murder in the first degree; rather, they merely grant the People the discretion to enter into a plea bargain agreement with the capital defendant so as to remove the case from the capital punishment track. Furthermore, the Supreme Court in decisions subsequent to Jackson has reaffirmed the permissibility of plea bargaining in order to avoid a possible death penalty. Lastly, any inquiry into whether the State Constitution provides enhanced protection in this case is better left to the Court of Appeals, which, in any event, has given its unequivocal approval to the practice of plea bargaining.

Becher v. Becher


97-03205


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


245 A.D.2d 408; 667 N.Y.S.2d 50; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13078


November 14, 1997, Argued  
December 15, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and the husband's motion is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which denied a motion to declare N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236(B)(5)(h), (6)(d) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The husband moved for a judgment declaring N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236(B)(5)(h), (6)(d) unconstitutional. These statutes provided that, where appropriate, a court shall consider the effect of a barrier to remarriage when determining equitable distribution and maintenance. Here, the husband had refused to deliver respondent wife a Jewish religious divorce. In response to the husband's motion the wife waived all of her rights under § 236(B)(5)(h), (6)(d). The wife requested that the trial court dismiss the husband's motion as moot. The trial court found that the motion was not moot, but it denied the motion to declare the statutes unconstitutional. The court reversed. It ruled that the trial court should have found the underlying controversy had been rendered moot and that the judicial determination sought would constitute the rendering of an advisory opinion. Because the wife waived her rights to have the husband's failure to deliver a Jewish divorce considered on the issues of equitable distribution and maintenance, there was no dispute between the parties which had to be resolved.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision denying the husband's motion to find the statutes unconstitutional, finding that the husband's motion should have been dismissed as moot.
