R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Co. v. City of New York Dep't of Fin.


61564


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


237 A.D.2d 6; 667 N.Y.S.2d 4; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12755



December 9, 1997, Decided  
December 9, 1997, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted respondent company's motion for summary judgment, declared N.Y. Comp. Codes R. &. Regs. tit. 11, § 603 to be unconstitutional, declared appellant's tax notice determination void, and permanently enjoined appellant from enforcing the notice against respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged a lower court judgment that granted respondent company's motion for summary judgment, declared N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, § 603 to be unconstitutional, voided the tax notice determination, and permanently enjoined appellant from enforcing the tax notice against respondent. Section 603 disallowed a depreciation deduction for a corporation's property placed in service out of the city, while allowing such a deduction for property located within the city. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because it found that the taxing ordinance was not even-handed. The court determined that the ordinance was facially discriminatory and that appellant failed to meet its burden of demonstrating that there was no other method to advance a legitimate local interest unrelated to economic protectionism. The court concluded that the ordinance had the practical effect of discriminating against taxpayers doing business in the city that had commercial property outside the city.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court judgment that granted respondent company's motion for summary judgment and declared appellant city's taxing ordinance to be unconstitutional because the ordinance was facially discriminatory and appellant failed to demonstrate that there was no other method to advance a legitimate local interest.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Commerce Clause - New York City Business Tax Favoring In-State Property Holders The corporate taxing provision of the Administrative Code of the City of New York tax that disallows an accelerated depreciation deduction otherwise allowed under Internal Revenue Code (26 USC) § 168 for a corporation's property "placed in service" outside of New York, while allowing such a deduction for property located within New York (Administrative Code of City of NY § 11-602 [8]), confers preferential tax treatment upon local businesses, concomitantly discriminating against out-of-State property holders, so as to violate the Commerce Clause (US Const, art I, § 8, cl [3]). Where, as here, a tax statute that burdens out-of-State businesses more than it burdens local businesses is facially discriminatory and not an "even-handed" regulation, the taxing authority has the burden of demonstrating that there is no other method to advance a legitimate local interest. In this case, the City has failed to articulate what constitutionally legitimate local interest is being served. No matter how the differential depreciation is explained, it impermissibly imposes on a business's interstate commercial decisions. The unequal treatment of corporate taxpayers results solely from the situs of their activities and provides a commercial advantage to local businesses. The practical effect of the tax scheme is to discriminate against taxpayers doing business in New York City who have commercial property located outside of New York such as plaintiff, a New Jersey corporation with its principal place of business in North Carolina engaged in the manufacture and sale of cigarettes in interstate commerce. The City's argument that there is a distinction, for Commerce Clause purposes, between overt differences in tax rates, and a mere disallowance of a mode of deduction for out-of-State property, elevates form over substance and lacks merit.

 

250 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Board of Coop. Educ. Servs. v. State


79352


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


236 A.D.2d 84; 664 N.Y.S.2d 149; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11473



November 13, 1997, Decided  
November 13, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name of this Case has been Corrected by the Court January 6, 1998.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Supreme Court (New York), which, in three declaratory judgment actions, granted defendants' cross motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaints challenging N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 803.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the claim that § 803 violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7 lacked merit. Section 7 recognized membership in a state pension or retirement system as a contractual relationship and prohibited the diminution or impairment of pension benefits. The court found that § 803 did not diminish any member's benefits. Rather, it augmented benefits for eligible members by allowing for retroactive membership.


OUTCOME: The order granting summary judgment for defendants was affirmed, without costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Equal Protection of Laws - Retroactive Pension Benefits - Disproportionate Financial Burden Placed upon First Employers Retirement and Social Security Law § 803 (L 1993, ch 437), which, inter alia, permits current members of a public retirement system, who did not join when they first became eligible because they were unaware of their right to do so, to obtain retroactive membership to the date when they first became eligible, does not unconstitutionally place a disproportionate financial burden upon first employers. Plaintiffs' due process and equal protection claims have been rendered moot by subsequent amendments (L 1995, ch 683; L 1997, ch 606), which spread out or "socialize" the costs of retroactive membership among all of the participating employers in the affected retirement systems.

Constitutional Law - Impairment of Pension Benefits - Retroactive Pension Benefits Retirement and Social Security Law § 803 (L 1993, ch 437), which, inter alia, permits current members of a public retirement system, who did not join when they first became eligible because they were unaware of their right to do so, to obtain retroactive membership to the date when they first became eligible, does not unconstitutionally impair pension benefits in violation of NY Constitution, article V, § 7. Rather, section 803 augments benefits for eligible members and does not diminish any member's benefits.

Constitutional Law - Gift of Public Funds - Retroactive Pension Benefits Retirement and Social Security Law § 803 (L 1993, ch 437), which, inter alia, permits current members of a public retirement system, who did not join when they first became eligible because they were unaware of their right to do so, to obtain retroactive membership to the date when they first became eligible, does not violate the constitutional prohibition against gifts of public funds (NY Const, art VIII, § 1) to private individuals. Section 803 does not entail the payment of extra compensation to employees. It merely extends retroactive membership and presents the possibility of increased pension benefits to current public employees who have been in continuous public service and always eligible to join the retirement system but for the fact they were not informed of that right.

Constitutional Law - Restrictions on Indebtedness of Local Subdivisions - Retroactive Pension Benefits Retirement and Social Security Law § 803 (L 1993, ch 437), which, inter alia, permits current members of a public retirement system, who did not join when they first became eligible because they were unaware of their right to do so, to obtain retroactive membership to the date when they first became eligible, is not violative of the constitutional prohibition against a local political subdivision contracting for another's indebtedness (NY Const, art VIII, § 2) since this prohibition only applies to indebtedness voluntarily assumed and payable at a stipulated time in the future in accordance with stipulated terms. Although section 803 permits the public employer to amortize retroactive membership costs over a period of 5 or 10 years, the indebtedness is not voluntarily assumed; rather, it is imposed on first employers, like a judgment, for past breach of duty. In any event, NY Constitution, article VII, § 8 specifically excludes from the article VIII prohibitions any increases in pension benefits resulting from the extension of retroactive membership rights under section 803.

 

251 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. La Fontaine


60802


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


235 A.D.2d 93; 664 N.Y.S.2d 587; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11046



November 6, 1997, Decided  
November 6, 1997, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which court denied defendant's motion to suppress narcotics found in a New York apartment by New Jersey police officers after finding that a federal arrest warrant validated extraterritorial arrest; the supreme court convicted defendant of criminal possession of controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: New Jersey police officers had arrest warrants for defendant issued by State and Federal courts in New Jersey. The officers went to an apartment in New York City to execute the warrants, knocked on the door, and identified themselves as police. Defendant moved to suppress narcotics found in the apartment on the grounds that officers did not have authority to make arrests in New York. The court held that the extraterritorial arrest was lawful. The court stated that although police officers had no police power outside of their geographical jurisdiction, they retained all their powers as private citizens, including the power to arrest. The court found that, even if their authority to act as private persons was negated by identifying themselves as police, suppression was not warranted because there was no constitutional right to be arrested by a particular law enforcement officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of criminal possession of a controlled substance, holding that New Jersey police officers retained all their powers as private citizens while outside their geographical jurisdiction, including the power to arrest.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Arrest - Extraterritorial Arrest by Out-of-State Police Officers - Citizen's Arrest

Defendant's extraterritorial arrest in New York, by New Jersey detectives armed with arrest warrants issued by both State and Federal courts in New Jersey charging him with conspiracy to commit murder and aggravated assault, was lawful where two of the officers knocked on the door of defendant's apartment, identified themselves as "police" and an officer stationed on the fire escape then apprehended defendant as he attempted to flee by climbing out of the window. Although the New Jersey officers had no official authority to arrest defendant or to execute the warrants in New York (see, CPL 140.10 [3]; 120.70 [1]; Fed Rules Crim Pro, rule 4 [d] [1]) and were not otherwise in close pursuit of defendant (see, CPL 140.55), the officers nonetheless made a valid citizen's arrest (CPL 570.34) since the officers were not acting under the "color of authority" to obtain evidence not available to private citizens. Other than identifying themselves as "police", the New Jersey officers did nothing that could not have been done by any private person. While the officers did act in their law enforcement capacity in obtaining the warrants, and in obtaining the cooperation of the New York City police earlier on the day of the arrest, they gained no evidence solely by virtue of their official status. They simply made a warrantless arrest of a fleeing felon on a fire escape and then escorted him back through the window into his apartment where the subject plastic bags of cocaine, in plain view on the refrigerator, were lawfully seized. Under these circumstances, defendant's arrest was not the "functional equivalent" of an unlawful warrantless arrest inside his home since defendant's flight was not precipitated by any official coercion. Defendant's return back into the apartment was necessitated by the fact that he was shirtless and the need to assist his crying two-year-old daughter, whom he had abandoned, unattended in the apartment in his haste to escape. Moreover, even if the officers' authority to act as private persons was negated by their act of identifying themselves as "police", their technical violation of procedural statutes conferring the power to arrest did not implicate any constitutionally protected right such as would require suppression. Given the existence of the State and Federal warrants authorizing defendant's arrest, there is no viable claim that defendant's arrest lacked probable cause. Therefore, the Fourth Amendment's protection against unreasonable searches and seizures, and the exclusionary rule remedy, are not applicable. Accordingly, defendant's conviction of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree should be affirmed.

 

252 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Bryant v. Bryant


76832


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


235 A.D.2d 116; 663 N.Y.S.2d 401; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10331



October 23, 1997, Decided  
October 23, 1997, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: The order is modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as awarded petitioner $ 115 per week in child support and $ 100,000 in additional child support; matter remitted to the Family Court of Schenectady County for further proceedings not inconsistent with this court's decision; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In petitioner wife and respondent husband's divorce proceedings, the wife filed an application to determine the husband's child support obligations. The parties cross appealed from the order of the Family Court, Schenectady County (New York), which modified the hearing examiner's ordered but still granted the wife's application in a proceeding pursuant to Family Court Act art. 4.


OVERVIEW: The husband had inherited a substantial sum, of which the trial court ordered $ 100,000. The court held that the husband's inheritance was not income as contemplated by Family Court Act §§ 413(1)(e) or 413(1)(b)(5), that an award of support from the inheritance did not duplicate the basic child support obligation under Family Court Act § 413(1)(c), that such an award was permissible under Family Court Act § 413(1)(f), that the husband failed to show that Family Court Act § 413(1)(e) was unconstitutional, but that the amount of the award from the inheritance was inappropriate, because such was a significant portion of the inheritance, the lump sum distribution required liquidation of substantial assets therefrom, and one of the children was living with the husband at the time of review. The valuation figure was erroneous due to the trial court's failure to consider tax consequences of the lump sum award and evidence of growth in the assets since receipt. The trial court should have considered the cost of the children's insurance in determining the basic support obligation, and the order to provide insurance for the wife was erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order on the law, without costs, by reversing so much as awarded the wife $ 115 per week in child support and $ 100,000 in additional child support and in all other respects affirmed said order. The court remitted the matter to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the court's decision.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Support - Consideration of Inheritance in Making Additional Child Support Award

1. In a proceeding to determine respondent's support obligations for his two minor children, Family Court was authorized to award the children a share of respondent's inheritance from his father as additional child support pursuant to Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e), which provides that the court may allocate a proportion of a gift or inheritance received by a parent to child support to be "paid in a manner determined by the court". Respondent's argument that the moneys generated by his inheritance should be imputed to him as income for purposes of calculating his basic child support obligation under Family Court Act § 413 (1) (c) is not only contrary to the plain language of Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e), which treats inheritances as "non-recurring payments from extraordinary sources not otherwise considered as income", but is also inconsistent with its underlying purpose and objective which is to ensure an appropriate and consistent level of support for children. Nor is there any merit to respondent's assertion that an award of additional child support pursuant to Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e) duplicates his basic child support obligation under Family Court Act § 413 (1) (c) since Family Court Act § 413 (1) (f) expressly contemplates and permits an award of additional support beyond the noncustodial parent's basic child support obligation. Lastly, respondent has failed to establish beyond a reasonable doubt that Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e) is unconstitutional.

Parent and Child - Support - Consideration of Inheritance in Making Additional Child Support Award - Propriety of Lump-Sum Distribution - Consideration of Health Insurance Premiums in Determining Basic Child Support Obligation

2. In a proceeding to determine respondent's support obligations for his two minor children, Family Court's $ 100,000 lump-sum award as additional child support pursuant to Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e) based upon respondent's inheritance from his father was inappropriate under the circumstances, especially since the lump-sum distribution would require respondent to liquidate substantial assets. Although Family Court properly charged respondent with the increase in the value of respondent's inheritance between the date of his father's death and the date of distribution, the court's finding that respondent's actual proceeds from the estate amounted to at least $ 400,000 lacks support in the record as a whole. Moreover, the parties' son is presently residing with respondent and, to the extent that this child already enjoys an enhanced standard of living as a result of respondent's inheritance, it would be inappropriate to award him additional monetary support. In addition, Family Court erred in failing to consider the tax consequences of a lump-sum award. Furthermore, Family Court also erred in ordering respondent to maintain health insurance for petitioner and in failing to consider whether the additional expense incurred by respondent in providing health and dental insurance for the children warranted deviation from his $ 115 weekly basic child support obligation imposed by the statute. Health insurance premiums are not proper add-ons to the basic child support obligation. Family Court should have taken this additional expense into consideration in determining whether respondent's basic child support obligation was fair and just and, having failed to do so, the court's award of child support was fundamentally flawed. Accordingly, the matter must be remitted to Family Court for a new hearing as to child support.

Parent and Child - Support - Consideration of Inheritance in Making Additional Child Support Award

3. In a proceeding to determine respondent's support obligations for his two minor children where Family Court's initial support order, including a $ 100,000 lump-sum award as additional child support pursuant to Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e) based upon respondent's inheritance from his father was set aside as fundamentally flawed, Family Court, in determining whether an award of additional child support is warranted on remittal, must take into consideration a number of relevant factors including, inter alia, the parties' combined parental income, their pro rata basic support obligation, whether such an award would reduce respondent's income below the poverty level or self-support reserve, the actual needs of the children and, ultimately, whether such an award would be unfair or unjust in light of the basic support obligation already imposed (see, Family Ct Act § 413 [1] [c], [d], [f]). The mere fact that respondent inherited a sizeable sum of money does not, standing alone, provide an adequate basis for an award of additional child support. Should Family Court determine that an additional award of support pursuant to Family Court Act § 413 (1) (e) is warranted here and, further, that a lump-sum payment is appropriate under the circumstances, the court should give careful thought to the impact that such an award would have upon respondent and consider whether, in light of whatever assets may be held by respondent, the award can be fashioned in such a manner as to avoid invading the principal. Any outright grant of funds to petitioner, such as the $ 25,000 previously awarded by Family Court as additional child support, would be looked upon with disfavor by the Appellate Division.

People v. English


1139


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


242 A.D.2d 940; 662 N.Y.S.2d 890; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10540



September 30, 1997, Decided  
September 30, 1997, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York), which entered judgment on the jury's verdict that found defendant guilty of violation of N.Y. Nav. Law §45.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was conviction of violations of various provisions of N.Y. Nav. Law § 45. The court held that: (1) the trial court properly refused to instruct the jury that it had to find recklessness in order to determine that defendant was guilty of violating N.Y. Nav. Law §45(1) because the standard for determining a violation was ordinary negligence, which was measured by the conduct of a reasonably prudent person; (2) the trial court properly refused to charge the jury that defendant must have "knowingly" failed to report the boating accident because the instruction to the jury that the State was required to prove that defendant was consciously aware that he had been involved in an accident with another vessel causing loss of life, personal injury, or property damage was all that was required; (3) N.Y. Nav. Law §45(1) was not so vague and indefinite as to render it unconstitutional; and (4) the evidence was legally sufficient to support the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

Schultz v. City of Port Jervis


96-07974


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


242 A.D.2d 699; 662 N.Y.S.2d 591; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9205


June 19, 1997, Submitted  
September 29, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is modified, on the law and as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, by deleting the provision thereof which declared Local Law, 1995, No. 8 of the City of Port Jervis to be valid and constitutional, and substituting therefor a provision dismissing the complaint; as so modified the order is affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action seeking a judgment declaring City of Port Jervis, N.Y., Local Law No. 8 (1995) to be unconstitutionally vague, plaintiff builders appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Orange County (New York), which denied their motion for summary judgment declaring the law to be unconstitutionally vague and declared the law to be valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The builders sought a declaratory judgment seeking a determination that City of Port Jervis, N.Y., Local Law No. 8 (1995) was unconstitutionally vague. The trial court denied their motion for summary judgment and declared the law valid and constitutional. On appeal, the court found that it was premature to attempt to determine the constitutionality of the challenged ordinance because the builders' challenge was unripe for adjudication. The court reasoned that, while the enactment was far from a model of clarity and the building inspector was imbued with broad discretion, there was no indication in the record supporting the builders' apparent contention that the building inspector would necessarily exercise his discretion in an arbitrary and capricious manner. The court held that it appeared that any fears the builders had of running afoul of the ordinance in question were wholly speculative and thus their complaint alleged only a hypothetical future controversy which was not ripe for adjudication. Accordingly, the court modified the trial court's order by deleting the portion thereof that declared the law valid and substituted a provision dismissing the complaint.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court denying the builders' motion for summary judgment declaring the local law to be unconstitutionally vague was modified by deleting the portion of the order declaring the law to be valid and constitutional, and substituting a provision therefor that dismissed the builders' complaint.

Mental Hygiene Legal Servs. ex rel. Aliza K. v. Ford


59231


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT

September 11, 1997, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a habeas corpus proceeding consolidated with a declaratory judgment action initiated by respondent claimant who had been civilly committed in a nonsecure hospital, appellant hospital administrator sought review of an order and judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which permanently stayed the claimant's transfer to a secure facility.


OVERVIEW: The claimant was civilly committed in a nonsecure hospital because her delusion that her former lover still loved her and wanted to reunite with her. After the claimant was committed, her behavior became assaultive and combative when she perceived that hospital personnel were trying to interfere with such a reunion. The hospital requested the claimant's transfer to a secure psychiatric facility, pursuant to 14 N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 14, part 57, which set forth the administrative procedure governing transfers, including a provision for judicial review. The trial court permanently stayed the transfer. The court modified the order to declare that because the transfer implicated a liberty interest and was based on security concerns, narrowly connected to the patient's delusion, rather than a general medical diagnosis that the patient was a danger to herself or to others, due process required prior judicial review of the transfer. The case was remanded for determination of whether the hospital proved by clear and convincing evidence that the transfer was necessary.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order and remanded the case for a new hearing to consider whether the hospital's evidentiary burden set forth herein had been satisfied.

Schulz v. State


78235


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT

July 24, 1997, Decided  
July 24, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The orders are affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owners and respondents county and state appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted a motion by certain respondents to dismiss the petition for failure to state a cause of action against them, and an order which partially granted the property owners' application to annul a determination of the county accepting a supplemental final environmental impact statement for a proposed sewer project.

The court also affirmed the dismissal of all claims against the state on the ground that the state had no involvement in the county's sewer project.

Schulz v. New York State Exec.


78929


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


233 A.D.2d 43; 660 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7479



July 17, 1997, Decided  
July 17, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voters sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 and an action for declaratory judgment against appellee state, held that the Clean Water/Clean Air Bond Act (Bond Act) of 1996, 1996 N.Y. Laws 412, was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The voters filed a combined N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that the Bond Act violated NY Const. art. VII, § 11, art. III, § 16. The state filed a motion to dismiss and the voters filed a motion for summary judgment. The supreme court denied the voters' summary judgment motion and determined that the voters lacked standing and that the Bond Act was constitutional. The individuals voters the portion of the order that declared the Bond Act constitutional, and the court of appeals transferred the appeal to the court. The court held that the voters lacked citizen-taxpayer standing. However, the court held that voters had standing in actions or proceedings predicated on an alleged violation of any of the fundamental requirements of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11. The court concluded that the voters were permitted to intelligently evaluate and appraise the wisdom of incurring the subject indebtedness for the single over-all purpose of benefitting the state's environment and thus, the purpose underlying the single work or purpose provision of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11 was fulfilled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the Bond Act was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Taxpayer's Action Challenging Constitutionality of Environmental Bond Act Petitioners lack standing as citizen-taxpayers under State Finance Law § 123-b (1) to challenge the constitutionality of the "Clean Water/Clean Air Bond Act of 1996" (L 1996, ch 412), which authorized the State to incur indebtedness not exceeding $ 1.75 billion for the purpose of preserving the environment by means of safe drinking water projects, clean water projects, solid waste projects, environmental restoration projects and air quality projects (see, L 1996, ch 413). Since State Finance Law § 123-b (1) specifically excepts from its application "the authorization, sale, execution or delivery of a bond issue or notes issued in anticipation thereof by the state or any agency, instrumentality or subdivision thereof or by any public corporation or public benefit corporation", a taxpayer has neither common-law nor statutory standing to challenge the issuance of State bonds or bond anticipation notes. Petitioners' challenge to the constitutionality of State Finance Law § 123-b (1), raised for the first time in their reply brief, has not been properly raised on appeal and lacks merit in any event. In addition, petitioners lack constitutional voter standing with regard to so much of the proceeding as is predicated on NY Constitution, article III, § 16, as that provision is not "linked to any voting rights".

Parties - Standing - Voter Standing to Challenge Environmental Bond Act as Violative of "Single Work or Purpose" Constitutional Debt-Incurring Provision Petitioners have separate and independent voter standing to challenge the "Clean Water/Clean Air Bond Act of 1996" (L 1996, ch 412), which authorized the State to incur indebtedness not exceeding $ 1.75 billion for the purpose of preserving the environment by means of safe drinking water projects, clean water projects, solid waste projects, environmental restoration projects and air quality projects (see, L 1996, ch 413), as violative of NY Constitution, article VII, § 11, which provides, inter alia, that "no debt shall be hereafter contracted by or in behalf of the state, unless such debt shall be authorized by law, for some single work or purpose, to be distinctly specified therein". An important purpose underlying this constitutional provision was to permit the people of the State to intelligently evaluate and appraise the single purpose of any proposed long-term bonded indebtedness to be incurred by the State and to prohibit the Legislature from lumping several purposes into one bond authorization and thereby obtain voter approval that perhaps could not otherwise be obtained if the relative merit and strength of each purpose were separately evaluated and appraised. Voter standing to sue in an action or proceeding predicated, as here, upon an alleged violation of any of the fundamental requirements of NY Constitution, article VII, § 11 is therefore necessary to ensure that the State government does not ignore constitutional dictates concerning public financing laws, especially since a citizen-taxpayer lacks standing under State Finance Law § 123-b (1) to challenge the issuance of State bonds or bond anticipation notes.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Challenge to Environmental Bond Act as Violative of "Single Work or Purpose" Constitutional Debt-Incurring Provision The "Clean Water/Clean Air Bond Act of 1996" (L 1996, ch 412), which authorized the State to incur indebtedness not exceeding $ 1.75 billion for the purpose of "preserving, enhancing, restoring, and improving" the quality of the State's "environment" by means of safe drinking water projects, clean water projects, solid waste projects, environmental restoration projects and air quality projects (see, L 1996, ch 413), is not violative of NY Constitution, article VII, § 11, which provides, inter alia, that "no debt shall be hereafter contracted by or in behalf of the state, unless such debt shall be authorized by law, for some single work or purpose, to be distinctly specified therein". The broad environmental projects authorized by the bond act are necessarily and naturally related to one another such that they constitute a single purpose within the meaning of the constitutional provision. As such, the voters were permitted to "intelligently evaluate and appraise" the wisdom of incurring the subject indebtedness for the single over-all purpose of benefitting the State's environment and, thus, the purpose underlying the "single work or purpose" provision of NY Constitution, article VII, § 11 was fulfilled.

 

258 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gamez


70166, 75586


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


241 A.D.2d 693; 660 N.Y.S.2d 196; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7437



July 17, 1997, Decided  
July 17, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from a judgment of the County Court of Ulster County (New York), upon a verdict convicting defendant of two counts of the crime of conspiracy in the second degree, and from an order denying defendant's motion to vacate the judgment of conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for two counts of conspiracy in the second degree. The indictment came after an investigation which included a series of police-monitored and recorded telephone conversations between defendant and an associate of his. Although a bench warrant was promptly issued for defendant's arrest, he was not returned to New York until over a decade later, having been a fugitive from justice. Upon his arrival, defendant's case was scheduled and thereafter tried. Upon his conviction, defendant was sentenced to two concurrent prison terms. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed, holding that defendant's lack of speedy trial was because of his own absence; that defendant had failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel; and defendant had failed to preserve appealable issues at trial.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. The time period within which plaintiff was required to be ready for trial was excluded because of defendant's status as a fugitive from justice.

 

259 of 992 DOCUMENTS


CONRAIL v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


75520


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


231 A.D.2d 140; 660 N.Y.S.2d 459; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7445



July 17, 1997, Decided  
July 17, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: Partially converted to an action for declaratory judgment, without costs, it is declared that Tax Law § 301-a has not been shown to be unconstitutional, remainder of petition dismissed and determination confirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner railroad system owner and operator sought review of the decision of respondent Tax Appeal Tribunal (tribunal), which sustained a deficiency of a petroleum business tax imposed under N.Y. Tax Law art. 13-A.


OVERVIEW: The owner purchased petroleum fuel from out-of-state suppliers, brought the fuel into New York, and stored it until it was used in locomotives that traveled both in and outside of New York. Pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 301-a(c)(1), the owner filed monthly returns and reduced its taxable diesel fuel gallons for "allocation of fuel used outside of the state," which reflected his view that only imported fuel actually consumed by locomotives in New York was taxable. State tax authorities conducted an audit of the owner's business and issued a notice of deficiency, which was sustained by an administrative law judge (ALJ). The court affirmed the tribunal's affirmance of the ALJ's determination because § 301-a(c)(1) did not violate the commerce clause. "Use" was broadly defined and included the situation when an owner withdrew the imported fuel from storage in New York and dispensed it by fueling it into locomotives. The tax did not discriminate against interstate commerce, as the "taxable event" was the withdrawal from storage of the fuel. Double taxation did not result because the fuel was not taxed if it was merely transported through the state or consumed within the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tribunal's affirmance of an ALJ's determination, which sustained a deficiency of a petroleum tax imposed under an amendment to the state's tax law.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Petroleum Gross Receipts Tax - Failure to Raise Constitutional Claim before Administrative Law Judge - Conversion of Constitutional Challenge into Declaratory Judgment Action A CPLR article 78 proceeding seeking to annul a determination of the Tax Appeals Tribunal sustaining a deficiency of a petroleum business tax on the ground that the taxing statute (Tax Law § 301-a) facially discriminates against interstate commerce in violation of the Commerce Clause of the US Constitution should not be dismissed merely because petitioner, an interstate railway operator, first raised the constitutional claim before the Tribunal. It is irrelevant that petitioner did not raise the constitutional claim before the Administrative Law Judge since the Tribunal correctly declined to rule on the constitutional issue based on the fact that it had no jurisdiction to consider whether a statute is unconstitutional on its face. In addition, since an article 78 proceeding is not the proper vehicle to attack the constitutionality of Tax Law § 301-a, that portion of the instant proceeding seeking such relief will be converted into a declaratory judgment action (see, CPLR 103 [c]).

Taxation - Petroleum Gross Receipts Tax - Commerce Clause - Constitutionality of Imposing Tax on Withdrawal of Fuel from Storage for Use in Interstate Railway Operations Tax Law § 301-a, which imposes a monthly petroleum business tax on petitioner, an interstate railway operator, for imported diesel motor fuel withdrawn from storage tanks in New York and used to power locomotives in petitioner's interstate operations, does not facially discriminate against interstate commerce in violation of the Commerce Clause of the US Constitution since the "taxable event" triggering the tax is the "withdrawal from storage" of the fuel and not consumption. The fuel, upon being unloaded and stored in New York, ceases to be a subject of transportation in interstate commerce and loses its immunity as such from State taxation. The fact that the fuel is later withdrawn from storage for use in interstate transportation does not affect the power of the State to tax it all before the interstate transportation commences. The distinction between storage and consumption minimizes the potential for double taxation since the fuel could not be taxed in States through which it is merely transported nor by the State in which it is merely consumed. A fair result is achieved by such a tax because a State in which preloading storage facilities are maintained is likely to provide substantial services to those facilities, including police protection and the maintenance of public access roads. Accordingly, the determination of the Tax Appeals Tribunal sustaining a deficiency of a petroleum business tax imposed upon petitioner should be confirmed.

 

260 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Ozanam Hall of Queens Nursing Home v. State


78793


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


241 A.D.2d 670; 661 N.Y.S.2d 54; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7334



July 10, 1997, Decided  
July 10, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nursing home sought review of an order of the Court of Claims (New York), which, inter alia, dismissed a portion of the nursing home's claim against respondent State of New York, which assessed late payment charges on the nursing home under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2087-d(6), (8), for lack of subject matter jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The state withheld a certain sum from medicaid reimbursement funds due the nursing home as unpaid assessments under N.Y. Pub Health Law § 2807-d. The nursing home sought to recover the withheld sum and, in addition, a judgment prohibiting the state from appropriating future funds due to it based upon its failure to pay assessments under § 2807-d. The state asserted affirmative defenses arguing, inter alia, that the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction. The nursing home sought to dismiss the affirmative defenses and the state contended that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to review the constitutionality of § 2087-d. The trial court dismissed so much of the nursing home's claim that sought a declaration of § 2807-d's unconstitutionality as applied to the nursing home and to prohibit appropriation of funds in the future. The court held that although in determining claims for money damages against the state, the trial court could apply equitable considerations and could grant some sort of incidental equitable relief, its primary jurisdiction was limited to actions seeking money damages against the state in appropriation, contract, or tort cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing a portion of the nursing home's claim against the state.

 

261 of 992 DOCUMENTS


New York Auto. Ins. Plan v. Muhl


61137


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


240 A.D.2d 303; 660 N.Y.S.2d 111; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6737



June 24, 1997, Decided  
June 24, 1997, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner New York Automobile Insurance Plan appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), denying the Plan's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 application challenging a directive of respondent Superintendent of Insurance of the State of New York, amending a section of the Plan, permitting an insurer member to deduct from a current deposit received with an insurance application any balance owed to that insurer on a prior policy.


OVERVIEW: The Superintendent's directive that a specific section of the Plan should be amended to provide that an insurer could not offset against an unearned premium balance on a cancelled policy when the insurance premium was advanced under a finance agreement was rationally based on N.Y. Ins. Law § 3428(d) and N.Y. Banking Law § 576(1)(f). Section 576(1)(f) and N.Y. Ins. Law § 3428(d) mandated return of unearned premiums to the bank or premium finance company upon cancellation of a financed insurance contract. Although the statutes were in existence when the subject Plan section was adopted, it was not until about four years later that the rule's susceptibility to an interpretation inconsistent with the clear mandates of the statutes was brought to the Superintendent's attention, making it incumbent upon him to clarify the rule to ensure compliance therewith. The Plan's claim that the directive unconstitutionally impaired obligations under existing contracts between its participating insureds and insurers was improperly raised for the first time on appeal and was without merit. The directive did not impair extant insurance contracts that had always been subject to those statutes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied the Plan's application challenging the Superintendent's directive, which amended a section of the Plan.

 

262 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Tamagni v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


76339


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


230 A.D.2d 417; 659 N.Y.S.2d 515; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6712



June 19, 1997, Decided  
June 19, 1997, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 13, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.s. LEXIS 6508.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to challenge the constitutionality of the definition of residency, N.Y. Tax Law § 605(b)(1)(B) and New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 11-1705(b), and the order of respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal, which sustained a tax assessment and rejected the taxpayers' claims that their susceptibility to income tax in two states violated the Commerce Clause and N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 3.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers filed nonresident income tax returns for New York State and New York City during certain years. They were domiciliaries of New Jersey and owned a cooperative apartment in New York City. One taxpayer worked as an investment banker in New York City. The Department of Taxation and Finance issued a notice of deficiency to the taxpayers, based upon the department's determination that the taxpayers were residents of New York City and New York State pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 11-1705(b) and N.Y. Tax Law § 605(b)(1)(B) because they had maintained a permanent place of abode and spent more than 183 days in the state and city during certain years. The court affirmed the assessment and rejected the taxpayers' constitutional challenge because: (1) the Commerce Clause was not implicated because commuting from one state to work in another and maintaining a residence and spending time in a non-domiciliary state did not produce the requisite effect on or necessarily involve interstate commerce; (2) the income tax did not burden the taxpayers' right of interstate travel; and (3) the tax on income generated by intangibles did not offend the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tribunal's determination that the taxpayers owed New York State and New York City income taxes for certain tax years, partially converted the proceeding to an action for declaratory judgment, and declared that the definitions of residency were not shown to be unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Actions - Conversion of One Form of Action to Another - Challenge to Constitutionality of Statute A CPLR article 78 proceeding is not the proper vehicle for challenging the constitutionality of Tax Law § 605 (b) (1) (B), which deems individuals who are not domiciled in New York but who nevertheless maintain a "permanent place of abode" and spend more than 183 days in New York to be "statutory residents" for personal income tax purposes. Accordingly, that portion of this matter seeking such relief is converted into an action for declaratory judgment (see, CPLR 103 [c]).

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Taxation of Nondomiciliary Residents - No Commerce Clause Violation Tax Law § 605 (b) (1) (B), which deems individuals who are not domiciled in New York but who nevertheless maintain a "permanent place of abode" and spend more than 183 days in New York to be "statutory residents" for personal income tax purposes (see also, Administrative Code of City of NY § 11-1705 [b]), thereby potentially subjecting nondomiciliary residents such as petitioners to taxation in two States, is not violative of the Commerce Clause of the US Constitution (US Const, art I, § 8, cl [3]). The Commerce Clause is not implicated since, unlike the business of transporting individuals between States, the practice of commuting from one State to work in another does not produce the requisite effect on commerce. In addition, maintaining a residence and spending time in New York, purchasing utilities, acquiring items of service, utilizing banking facilities and frequenting restaurants and retail establishments, does not necessarily involve interstate commerce. Furthermore, the taxing statute does not implicate the constitutional right to interstate travel.

Taxation - Personal Income Tax - Taxation of Nondomiciliary Residents - No Violation of State Constitution Tax Law § 605 (b) (1) (B), which deems individuals who are not domiciled in New York but who nevertheless maintain a "permanent place of abode" and spend more than 183 days in New York to be "statutory residents" for personal income tax purposes, thereby potentially subjecting nondomiciliary residents such as petitioners to taxation in two States, is not violative of NY Constitution, article XVI, § 3, which provides that the situs of intangibles for purposes of taxation is "located at the domicile of the owner", since that constitutional provision does not preclude New York from applying its personal income tax to income from intangibles owned by a nondomiciliary resident. Furthermore, petitioners were not taxed on the ownership of intangibles located in New York nor were their intangibles taxed on an ad valorem basis. Rather, they were taxed based on the income generated by those intangibles, which does not offend the State Constitution.

Friedenburg v. New York State Dep't of Envtl. Conservation


96-01681


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


240 A.D.2d 407; 658 N.Y.S.2d 643; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5768


February 4, 1997, Argued  
June 2, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised June 6, 1997.
Subsequent appeal at Friedenburg v. New York State Dep't of Envtl. Conservation, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12447 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Nov. 24, 2003)



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the order is modified by deleting the provision thereof which denied that branch of the separate motion which was to dismiss the cause of action asserted in the amended petition to annul the determination and by substituting therefor a provision granting that branch of the separate motion which was to dismiss the cause of action to annul the determination and denying the separate motion [***2] to dismiss in all other respects; as so modified, the order is affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Department of Environmental Conservation (department), sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, New York, which denied the department's motion to dismiss respondent property owners' complaint on the ground that the cause of action was barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: The department denied the property owners' request for a permit and variances under the Tidal Wetlands Act, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 25, to construct a residence on property located near a bay. Thus, the property owners commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 asserting that the denial of their application by the department constituted a taking of their property without compensation. The original petition seeking a determination that a taking had occurred expressly conceded the validity of the department's denial of the permit and related variances. The original claim therefore did not give notice of the later, amended claim and thus waived that claim. Accordingly, the court found on appeal that the supreme court improperly held that the relation back doctrine was applicable. In denying the department's motion to dismiss the complaint, the court found that the supreme court should have considered the merits of the arguments the department raised in the original motion to dismiss. In addressing those arguments, the court found that dismissal of the property owners' claims that a taking occurred was not warranted, as those claims stated valid causes of action.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the supreme court by granting a branch of the department's motion to dismiss the cause of action to annul the determination of the department, and as so modified, affirmed the judgment.

 

264 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jeffrey


0815


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


239 A.D.2d 953; 659 N.Y.S.2d 602; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6397



May 30, 1997, Decided  
May 30, 1997, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed three judgments of the Niagara County Court (New York), which convicted him upon his plea of guilty of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the fourth degree, attempted criminal possession of stolen property in the third degree, and criminal sale of a controlled substance in the fifth degree and sentenced him as a second felony offender to an aggregate term of incarceration of 11 to 22 years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty and was convicted of several crimes. Defendant was sentenced as a second felony offender to an aggregate term of incarceration of 11 to 22 years. Defendant contended that his plea was not knowingly and voluntarily entered and that the sentence was unduly harsh or severe and constituted cruel and unusual punishment. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of the trial court. The court found that defendant had failed to preserve for the court's review his contention that his plea was not knowingly and voluntarily entered. The court held that, in any event, the record established that defendant had ample time to confer with counsel before accepting the offer and stated during his plea colloquy that he was voluntarily pleading guilty and had not been threatened or forced to do so. The court also found that the record showed that the trial court made appropriate inquiry to assure defendant's intelligent waiver of the defense of agency. The court held that the sentence imposed on defendant was not so unduly harsh or severe or that it constituted cruel or unusual punishment, as the sentencing statute had not been unconstitutionally applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court, which convicted defendant upon his plea of guilty of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the fourth degree, attempted criminal possession of stolen property in the third degree, and criminal sale of a controlled substance in the fifth degree and sentenced him as a second felony offender to an aggregate term of incarceration of 11 to 22 years.

 

265 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Jarrett


0645


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


230 A.D.2d 513; 660 N.Y.S.2d 916; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6282



May 30, 1997, Decided  
May 30, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed without opinion by In re Jarrett, 90 N.Y.2d 935, 686 N.E.2d 1367, 1997 N.Y. LEXIS 3171, 664 N.Y.S.2d 272 (1997)
Writ of certiorari denied Johnson v. Doe, 524 U.S. 918, 118 S. Ct. 2301, 141 L. Ed. 2d 161, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 3770, 66 U.S.L.W. 3782 (1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant birth parents sought review of the decision of the Family Court, Niagara County, New York, which concluded that the best interest of the child born to the birth mother were served by his adoption. The birth parents did not challenge the best interests finding, but instead challenged the court's jurisdiction and raised constitutional challenges. The court had determined on prior appeal that the birth mother's consent was valid.


OVERVIEW: Birth parents lived in Pennsylvania. The child was placed with respondent adoptive parents in New York shortly after birth, pursuant to the Interstate Compact on the Placement of Children. In addition to contesting the adoption, the birth parents had brought a custody proceeding in a Pennsylvania state court and also an action in federal district court seeking custody. The court held that the family court had jurisdiction under the Interstate Compact because the birth mother, as the sending agency, had transferred jurisdiction by executing valid consent documents. N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 374-a(1), art. V(a). The court had already determined that the birth father was entitled only to notice and an opportunity to participate at a best interests hearing. The court held that the New York adoption law did not violate the birth father's due process and equal protection rights. There was a rational basis for treating him differently from the birth mother because he had never accepted responsibility for the child. The court held that adoption based upon a best interests of the child standard did not violate the birth parents' constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the family court in favor of the adoptive parents.


HEADNOTES

Courts - Family Court - Subject Matter Jurisdiction over Private Placement Adoption Governed by Interstate Compact on Placement of Children Niagara County Family Court at all times possessed subject matter jurisdiction under the Interstate Compact on the Placement of Children (Social Services Law § 374-a) over private placement adoption proceedings involving a child who had been placed in the custody of the New York adoptive parents by the birth mother the day after he was born in Pennsylvania (see, Domestic Relations Law § 115-a), notwithstanding the Pennsylvania Interstate Compact agency's purported rescission of its initial approval of the adoptive child's placement for adoption in New York. Approval for the child's removal to New York was given by both the Pennsylvania and New York Interstate Compact agencies after they had been supplied with the requisite information, including the identity of the nonresident unwed birth father. By executing valid consent documents, the birth mother, as the "sending agency" under the Interstate Compact, lawfully transferred both the child and jurisdiction to New York. Under these circumstances, the child's custody should be decided by the courts of New York. Pennsylvania, having authorized the removal of the child to New York, may not retroactively rescind its approval for the child's placement in New York. Furthermore, the State Constitution does not limit Family Court's subject matter jurisdiction only to those adoptions that involve children born in New York.

Adoption - Private Placement Adoption - Interstate Compact on Placement of Children - Constitutional Rights of Unwed "Notice" Father The constitutional rights of the nonresident unwed birth father were not violated by the adoption of his child by the New York adoptive parents after a best interests hearing (Domestic Relations Law § 115-a) where the birth mother, as the "sending agency" under the Interstate Compact on the Placement of Children (Social Services Law § 374-a), executed valid consent documents lawfully transferring both the child and jurisdiction from Pennsylvania to New York and both the Pennsylvania and New York Interstate Compact agencies authorized the child's removal to New York after they had been supplied with the requisite information, including the identity of the birth father. The birth father has failed to meet his burden of proving the adoption statute's unconstitutionality, either facially or as applied, beyond a reasonable doubt. The birth father's due process rights as a "notice" father (see, Domestic Relations Law § 111-a) were satisfied by affording him an opportunity to participate in the best interests hearing. The birth father did not have a constitutionally protected liberty interest in retaining custody of his child that would allow him to veto the adoption due to his failure to promptly assume parental responsibility prior to and following the birth of his child. There is no constitutional infirmity in judging the interest of a birth father in being a parent based on his conduct in the six months preceding his child's placement for adoption. During the period of the birth mother's pregnancy, the birth father was arrested for assaulting her; during that same period he was married to another woman and was living with a 17-year-old girl, whom he impregnated, and he has yet to pay for the birth mother's pregnancy or birth-related expenses. In addition, the New York adoption statute is not violative of due process merely because it does not require a finding that the birth father is unfit as a parent, nor is the principle of equal protection offended by relying on the birth father's prebirth conduct in determining his rights. Given the differences between the responsibilities of the birth mother and birth father and the purpose of New York's law, which is to promote early stability and to provide safe and secure homes for children, any difference in the treatment of a birth father and birth mother found in the Domestic Relations Law is rationally related to achieving that purpose and, therefore, any differential treatment does not violate equal protection.

Adoption - Private Placement Adoption - Interstate Compact on Placement of Children - Constitutional Rights of Unwed Birth Parents The constitutional rights of the nonresident birth mother were not violated by the adoption of her child by the New York adoptive parents after a best interests hearing (Domestic Relations Law § 111-a) where the birth mother, as the "sending agency" under the Interstate Compact on the Placement of Children (Social Services Law § 374-a), voluntarily executed valid consent documents lawfully transferring both the child and jurisdiction from Pennsylvania to New York and both the Pennsylvania and New York Interstate Compact agencies authorized the child's removal to New York after they had been supplied with the requisite information. The birth mother set the adoption proceedings in motion and has not been unconstitutionally deprived of the custody of her child based on her status as an unwed mother. The fact that the birth mother lost the presumption of custody by voluntarily placing the child for adoption does not offend constitutional principles nor does utilization of the best interests standard violate her constitutional right to equal protection. Furthermore, the birth mother's arguments that adoptive parents start out in a superior position and that birth parents are presumed to be ambivalent toward a child who has been surrendered for adoption are meritless, inasmuch as the adoption statute specifically provides that there shall be no presumption that any particular disposition is in a child's best interests (see, Domestic Relations Law § 115-b [6] [d] [v]). Nor is there any merit to her contention that the statute encourages the taking of a child from its parent merely because of the parents' poverty. The respective financial abilities of the parties, as well as the stability of the current custody arrangement, were proper concerns of the Family Court in reaching a best interests determination. Furthermore, Family Court did not limit the birth mother's opportunity to present witnesses or to fully develop the record. Family Court's best interests determination and its order granting the petition of the adoptive parents is fully supported by the record which demonstrated the unstable and irresponsible lifestyle of the birth parents as compared with adoptive parents' "stable, happy and successful marriage".

 

266 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cleveland


0326


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


238 A.D.2d 897; 660 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4685



April 25, 1997, Decided  
April 25, 1997, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Cayuga County Court (New York), which, upon a guilty plea, convicted defendant of aggravated unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle in the first degree, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 511(3), and driving while ability impaired, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed a conviction entered on a guilty plea for aggravated unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle in the first degree, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 511(3) and driving while ability impaired, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(1). Defendant claimed that § 511(3) was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant did not dispute that his license was suspended for driving while impaired, but alleged that he was unaware that his license was still suspended for failure to pay the suspension termination fee. The court found the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 503(2)(j) required the license to be reinstated by paying a fee or the license was not restored. Therefore, defendant knew or should have known the license was still suspended. When § 511(3) was read in conjunction with § 503(2)(j), the statute was clear that failure to reinstate meant the license was still suspended as encompassed by § 511(3).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the statute was not unconstitutionally vague.

Trustees of Union College v. Members of the Schenectady City Council


77552


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


230 A.D.2d 17; 656 N.Y.S.2d 425; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3733



April 10, 1997, Decided  
April 10, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged an order of the Supreme Court, Schenectady County (New York), which granted plaintiff college's motion for summary judgment and made a declaration in favor of the college in its declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that the city's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: The city challenged an order of the trial court, which granted the college's motion for summary judgment and made a declaration in its favor in the college's declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that a city's code was unconstitutional on its face. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that by excluding educational uses from the class of special permit uses in the district, the city effectively deprived the college of any opportunity to demonstrate that its proposed educational use was consistent with and in furtherance of the public good. The court was of the view that the college met its burden of establishing, beyond a reasonable doubt, that Schenectady City, N.Y., Code § 264-8, which excluded educational uses from the class of special permit uses in the district and, in so doing, deprived the college of the opportunity to demonstrate that the educational use of its property did not pose a threat to the public welfare, was not designed to accomplish or advance a legitimate public purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court granting summary judgment in favor of the college and making a declaration in the college's favor that the city's code was unconstitutional on its face.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Special Use Permit - Unconstitutionality of Ordinance Excluding Educational Uses in Historic District Plaintiff educational institution, which, prior to defendant City's adoption of an ordinance amending its zoning provisions to exclude educational uses from the class of special permit uses in a Single-Family Historic District (District), had been allowed to use its properties for educational purposes as a special use within the District, has met its burden of establishing, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the zoning ordinance is unconstitutional on its face due to the failure to provide any mechanism through which the legitimate interests of the parties may be balanced against one another, especially in view of the "preferred status" enjoyed by educational institutions with respect to the application of municipal zoning ordinances. While defendants may exclude plaintiff's educational use of its property from the District upon a proper showing that plaintiff's use is detrimental to the public health, safety, welfare or morals, defendants cannot short circuit the deliberative process by adopting an ordinance that results in the wholesale exclusion of educational uses within the District in the first instance. By excluding educational uses from the class of special permit uses in the District, defendants effectively have deprived plaintiff of any opportunity to demonstrate that its proposed educational use is consistent with and in furtherance of the public good. Furthermore, the availability of avenues of review for plaintiff such as a variance application, even if viable, do not cure the facial invalidity in the ordinance. Moreover, neither the variance process, which requires plaintiff to demonstrate practical difficulties or unnecessary hardships, nor the zoning amendment process is a constitutionally permissible substitute for the necessary balancing of the parties' competing interests, which most appropriately is undertaken in the context of reviewing an application for a special use permit. Defendants' remaining contentions, including their assertions that the controversy is not ripe for judicial review, lack merit.

People v. Kinch


76665


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


237 A.D.2d 830; 655 N.Y.S.2d 191; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2746



March 20, 1997, Decided  
March 20, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed from a judgment from the County Court of St. Lawrence County (New York), which convicted defendant, upon his guilty plea, of the crime of grand larceny in the fourth degree. On appeal, defendant primarily contended that his guilty plea was not properly entered, and that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 410.91 was unconstitutional because it deprived him of equal protection of the law.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a judgment convicting criminal defendant, upon his guilty plea, of the crime of grand larceny in the fourth degree. Although defendant's contentions addressed to the voluntariness of his plea were not preserved for consideration, the transcript of the plea allocution established that defendant's plea of guilty was knowingly and voluntarily entered. Contrary to defendant's assertion, there was no requirement that a defendant personally recite the facts underlying his or her crime. The court was not persuaded that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 410.91, which created the sentencing option of parole supervision for eligible individuals with a history of controlled substance dependence, denied defendant equal protection of the law. The court reasoned that, in light of the current nationwide drug crisis and the resulting criminal conduct, it could not be seriously argued that legislation targeting drug-dependent individuals and providing for intensive drug treatment as an alternative to incarceration did not bear a reasonable relationship to a legitimate legislative objective. The court thus held that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 410.91 was constitutional as applied to defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of fourth degree grand larceny was affirmed.

People v. Mink


76409


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


237 A.D.2d 664; 655 N.Y.S.2d 115; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2250



March 6, 1997, Decided  
March 6, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State indicted defendant on one count of cruelty to animals and one count of possession of fighting animals in violation of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law §§ 353 and 351(3)(b). A jury in the County Court of Columbia County (New York) convicted defendant of the possession charge. Defendant received a 10-day jail sentence and a five thousand dollar fine. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: An informant, accompanied by an officer, visited defendant's property and found cages housing birds that had their wattles and crests cut and their chest and leg area shaved, suggesting that they were used for fighting. The court held that the warrant issued after the visit was proper because it was based on sworn affidavits of the officer and the informant. The court further held that the warrant described the premises to be searched with sufficient precision. In affirming, the court held that the federal statute, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2156, did not preempt state law because the statute expressly did not preclude the state of origination from enacting laws prohibiting the keeping, raising, or using of animals for fighting purposes. The court held that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 351 was not unconstitutionally vague because it specifically prohibited the owning, possessing, or keeping of any animal under circumstances evincing an intent that such animal engage in animal fighting. The court held that such language was sufficiently definite to provide a person of ordinary intelligence with fair notice of any prohibited conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment entered on a jury verdict that convicted defendant of one count of possession of fighting animals.

Manocherian v. Lenox Hill Hosp.


59002 & M-5819


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


229 A.D.2d 197; 654 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1291



February 18, 1997, Decided  
February 18, 1997, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners challenged the order and judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which gave the owners possession of eight apartments but ordered the owners to offer renewal leases to the nurse subtenants of seven other apartments. Appellee hospital lessee challenged the order and judgment of the trial court that awarded possession of the apartments to the owners.


OVERVIEW: The owners successfully challenged a law that allowed the hospital lessee to sublease apartments subject to New York City's Rent Stabilization Law (RSL), New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code of the City of New York, tit. 26, ch. 4, without the consent of the owners and to be deemed a residential tenant. The owners then sought to evict the nurse subtenants and to regain possession of the apartments. The lower court gave the owners possession of eight apartments but ordered that renewal terms be offered to the nurse tenants of the other seven units. Both the owners and the hospital lessee appealed. The court noted that the declaration of unconstitutionality of the law favoring the hospital lessee's tenancy effectively removed that provision from consideration as if it had never been passed. The court then observed that, under the plain language of the RSL, the hospital lessee as the prime tenant had a right to a renewal lease but that the subtenants had no such right.


OUTCOME: The order and judgment of the trial court was modified, on the law, to the extent of granting the owners possession of each of the seven apartments for which possession was not granted. Except as modified, the order and judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Motions and Orders - Reargument or Renewal - Raising Issue for First Time on Reargument

In an action by plaintiffs landlords seeking, inter alia, the termination of defendant hospital's rent-stabilized tenancies in 15 apartments occupied by defendant's nurses as month-to-month subtenants on the ground that defendant was not a primary resident of any of the apartments and was thus not entitled to a renewal lease under the Rent Stabilization Law, it was not an abuse of discretion for the motion court to have heard defendant's motion for reargument and clarification, which was brought prior to the entry of an order and judgment on the court's initial decision, and which first raised the issue of whether the subtenants were entitled to renewal leases in their own names. A motion court's decision whether to grant or deny a motion for reargument is firmly within its discretion and should only be disturbed where such discretion has been abused.

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Renewal Leases for Not-for-Profit Hospital Which Subleases Rent-Stabilized Apartments to Employees

In an action by plaintiffs landlords seeking, inter alia, the termination of defendant hospital's tenancies in 15 rent-stabilized apartments occupied by defendant's nurses as month-to-month subtenants on the ground that defendant was not a primary resident of any of the apartments and was thus not entitled to a renewal lease under the Rent Stabilization Law (RSL), Supreme Court, in determining that defendant was not entitled to renewal leases but that the individual subtenants occupying the apartments had separate rights to renewal leases in their own names based upon the holding in Matter of Cale Dev. Co. v Conciliation & Appeals Bd. (94 AD2d 229, affd 61 NY2d 976) that a corporate entity could be a residential tenant, incorrectly interpreted the decision of the Court of Appeals in prior litigation between the parties (see, 84 NY2d 385) declaring Laws of 1984 (ch 940), which amended the Omnibus Housing Act (L 1983, ch 403) and the RSL to allow a not-for-profit hospital, such as defendant herein, renting a housing unit for residential use to be deemed a tenant and to sublet housing leased for its employees without the landlord's consent, to be an unconstitutional regulatory taking of property. The decision of the Court of Appeals limits Cale's grant of lease renewal rights regarding corporate-leased housing units to instances where the lease in question specifically names the individual intended occupant and no perpetual tenancy is possible. Consequently, where, as here, the leases did not name the intended occupant and created the possibility of a tenancy in perpetuity, the leases cannot be renewed. Absent chapter 940's insulation from subletting restrictions and nonprimary residence claims, the controlling law clearly and unambiguously states that the subtenant nurses are not entitled to renewal leases in their own names (Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-504 [a] [1]; § 26-511 [c] [12] [d], [g]). Moreover, the award of these stabilized tenancies to the subtenant nurses would be a windfall since they never appeared in the action, despite being named as parties, and never had any expectation of the RSL's protection.
