Dorst v. Pataki


76699


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


228 A.D.2d 4; 654 N.Y.S.2d 198; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1130



February 6, 1997, Decided  
February 6, 1997, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmates challenged the order of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which declared that N.Y. Correct Law § 851, as amended by 1995 N.Y. Laws ch. 3, § 29, was a constitutional delegation of legislative authority to respondent, the governor. Section 851 delegated to the governor the authority to exclude or limit the participation of otherwise eligible inmates in temporary release programs.


OVERVIEW: The inmates, who were either eligible for temporary release or had been approved for work release, all became ineligible by reason of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 5.5, an executive order. While the case was pending, 1995 N.Y. Laws ch. 3, § 29 amended N.Y. Correct. Law § 851(2) to provide that the governor, pursuant to an executive order, was entitled to exclude or limit the participation of any class of otherwise eligible inmates from participation in a temporary release program. Although the trial court declared N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 5.5 unconstitutional because it imposed restrictions upon the temporary release program that were inconsistent with the eligibility requirements under N.Y. Correct. Law § 851, it declared 1995 N.Y. Laws ch. 3, § 29, as it amended N.Y. Correct. Law § 851, constitutional. The inmates appealed only the finding that 1995 N.Y. Laws ch. 3, § 29 was constitutional. The court affirmed and held that § 29 was a constitutional delegation of legislative authority to the governor because the safeguards and standards were as applicable to the governor as they were to the Commissioner of Correctional Services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Separation of Powers - Statute Authorizing Governor to Regulate Inmate's Participation in Temporary Work Release Program A 1995 amendment to the Correction Law granting the Governor the power, by Executive Order, to "exclude or limit the participation of any class of otherwise eligible inmates in a temporary release program" (Correction Law § 851 [2], as amended by L 1995, ch 3, § 29; see, Executive Order No. 5 [9 NYCRR 5.5]) is a constitutional delegation of legislative authority to the Executive that does not violate the separation of powers doctrine. The Legislature has not given the Governor the unbridled authority to issue Executive Orders excluding any or all inmates otherwise eligible for work release. Rather, the general safeguards and standards contained in the statutory scheme requiring that determinations regarding temporary release programs be "guided by consideration for the safety of the community and the welfare of the inmate" (Correction Law § 852 [1]) sufficiently circumscribe the Governor's power in this area so as to make the legislative delegation of authority constitutional.

People v. Bundy


57711


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


235 A.D.2d 334; 654 N.Y.S.2d 108; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 600



January 28, 1997, Decided  
January 28, 1997, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, New York County (New York), inter alia, convicted defendant of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first, second, third and fourth degrees; criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree; and criminal use of drug paraphernalia in the second degree, and sentenced her to concurrent terms of 15 years to life on the first degree drug possession count; and three years to life for second degree drug possession.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first, second, third, and fourth degrees; criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree; and criminal use of drug paraphernalia in the second degree. The trial court sentenced defendant to concurrent terms of 15 years to life on the first-degree drug possession count; three years to life on the second-degree drug possession count, one to three years on the remaining drug possession counts and the weapon counts; and one year on the drug paraphernalia count. On review, the court affirmed the convictions and the sentences. The court held that the evidence was legally sufficient under the drug factory presumption, N.Y. Penal Law § 220.25(2), to prove defendant constructively possessed all the contraband, and the jury's verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. The jury reached the only reasonable conclusion based on the evidence, i.e., that defendant constructively possessed the contraband found inside and just outside the apartment as one of the participants in the activities in the narcotics factory in the apartment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the circumstances established the operation of a narcotics factory for purposes of the drug factory presumption statute and the inference that defendant, as well as those found in the apartment, were engaged in the illicit enterprise, was irresistible and, therefore, entitled the jury to find defendant guilty as it did of the various charges.

Miller v. DeBuono


95-10349


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


235 A.D.2d 480; 652 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 396


November 19, 1996, Argued  
January 21, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION: ADJUDGED that the petition is granted to the extent that the penalty imposed pursuant to 10 NYCRR 415.4(b)(1)(ii)(b) is annulled, on the law, without costs or disbursements, the determination is otherwise confirmed, and the proceeding is otherwise dismissed on the merits; and it is further,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nurse's aide requested review of a determination of respondent Commissioner of Health of the State of New York (commissioner) that reversed a decision of an administrative law judge and sustained a charge of patient abuse against the nurse's aide pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 415.4(b)(1)(ii)(b).


OVERVIEW: The commissioner reversed a decision of an administrative law judge and sustained a charge of patient abuse against the nurse's aide pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 415.4(b)(1)(ii)(b). The nurse's aide appealed. Upon review, the court noted that substantial evidence supported the determination that the nurse's aide was guilty of patient abuse. However, neither § 415.4(b)(1)(ii)(b), nor the federal regulation upon which it was based, 42 C.F.R. § 483.13(c)(1)(ii)(B), evidenced an intention that they be retroactively applied. Accordingly, it was error for the commissioner to apply the regulations retroactively to the nurse's aid. The petition for review was therefore granted, and so much of the penalty imposed as prohibited the nurse's aid from continued or future employment pursuant to the statute was annulled.


OUTCOME: The nurse's aid's petition seeking review of a decision of the commissioner that reversed a decision of an administrative law judge and sustained a charge of patient abuse against the nurse's aide was granted to the extent that the penalty imposed was annulled.

Village of Port Chester v. City of Rye


96-00228, 96-06491


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


234 A.D.2d 453; 651 N.Y.S.2d 146; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13090


November 18, 1996, Argued  
December 16, 1996, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of Rye sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which declared a City of Rye traffic ordinance unconstitutional in an action brought by appellee Village of Port Chester. The traffic ordinance applied to a street that was outside the Village boundaries and the City argued that the Village lacked standing to challenge the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The City of Rye adopted an ordinance prohibiting large trucks from making a left turn onto a City of Rye street. The intersection was wholly within the City's limits and outside the village's boundary. The Village, alleging that the ordinance deprived heavy trucks from one possible means of access to the Village, filed an action challenging the ordinance. The City sought summary judgment, which the trial court denied. The trial court further held that the traffic ordinance was unconstitutional, and the City appealed. The court stated that governmental entities, being artificial creatures of statute, had neither an inherent nor a common-law right to sue, and that a right to sue, if it existed at law, was to be derived from relevant enabling legislation or other concrete statutory predicate. The court found that there was no express statutory authority that would allow the Village to challenge the City's traffic ordinance on a street wholly within the City's limits. The court found further that the Village could point to no relevant enabling legislation or other statutory predicate from which such authority could be inferred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment that declared the City's traffic ordinance unconstitutional, holding that there was no statutory authority for the Village's challenge to the ordinance.

T.D. v. New York State Office of Mental Health


58554


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


228 A.D.2d 95; 650 N.Y.S.2d 173; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12293



December 5, 1996, Decided  
December 5, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant New York State Office of Mental Health (OMH) challenged a decision of the Supreme Court (New York), which granted plaintiff involuntarily hospitalized patients' motion for summary judgment, and held that the regulations promulgated by OMH, codified at N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 14, § 527.10, were beyond OMH's authority and were invalid and unenforceable.


OVERVIEW: The regulations at issue provided for prescreening of potential subjects regarding participation in more than minimal risk nontherapeutic and possibly therapeutic experiments. The regulations also contained provisions for obtaining consent to the research, which involved the administration of approved and experimental antipsychotic and psychotropic drugs that were capable of causing harmful or fatal side effects, and involved highly invasive painful testing procedures with essentially no benefit to the subjects. The regulations were promulgated beyond OMH's authority, because such authority was exclusively granted to another agency. Furthermore, under the regulations, it was possible that an otherwise capable person may have been found to be incapable, and the determination of capacity was not subject to review at the patient's request. The court held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 14, §§ 527.10: 14, 527.10 (e) (2) (ii) and (ix), 527.10 (e) (2) (viii), 527.10 (e) (3) (iii) and (v), 527.10 (e) (2) (iii) and (iv), and 527.10 (e) (3) (i) failed to provide for adequate notice and review procedures and therefore violated both the state and federal Due Process Clauses.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to include that other sections of the regulations at issue violated the Due Process Clause of both the state and federal constitutions, and otherwise affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Mental Health - Human Subject Research - Validity of Regulations Promulgated by Office of Mental Health Regulations promulgated by defendant New York State Office of Mental Health (OMH) (14 NYCRR 527.10), which set out procedures to be followed for the participation of adults and children, who are patients or residents of OMH operated and licensed facilities deemed incapable of giving consent, in "more than minimal risk" nontherapeutic research experiments involving psychiatric medications that may cause harmful permanent or fatal side-effects, are invalid and unenforceable as being beyond the authority of the Commissioner of OMH. While OMH has broad jurisdiction to fulfill its general responsibility to foster research in order to prevent, diagnose and treat mental illness (see, Mental Hygiene Law § 7.01), it has not been given any specific authority to regulate human subject research involving mental illness and its power to regulate human subject research cannot be implied from the absence of a specific statutory prohibition. Rather, the Department of Health has the exclusive authority to promulgate rules governing human subject research activities conducted at OMH facilities pursuant to article 24-A of the Public Health Law, which requires the consent of the Commissioner of Health for the conduct of non-Federally regulated research involving minors, incompetents and mentally disabled persons (Public Health Law § 2444 [2]). Furthermore, the non-Federally funded research being conducted at OMH facilities fails to comply with Federal reporting requirements (see, 28 CFR 46.103 [b] [5]) and is therefore subject to Public Health Law article 24-A.

Mental Health - Human Subject Research - Validity of Regulations Promulgated by Office of Mental Health - Common-Law Privacy and Due Process Rights of Potential Subjects Regulations promulgated by defendant New York State Office of Mental Health (OMH) (14 NYCRR 527.10), which set out procedures to be followed for the participation of adults and children, who are patients or residents of OMH operated and licensed facilities deemed incapable of giving consent, in "more than minimal risk" nontherapeutic research experiments involving psychiatric medications that may cause harmful permanent or fatal side-effects, do not adequately safeguard and therefore violate the common-law privacy and due process rights of potential subjects. In order to be sustained as constitutional and otherwise viable, the challenged regulations must, at the very least, contain appropriate and specific provisions for notice to the potential subject that his or her capacity is being evaluated and for appropriate administrative and judicial review of a determination regarding capacity. However, the regulations do not identify or set out specific or even minimum qualifications for the individual or individuals who initially assess a potential subject's capacity, and do not contain a specific protocol for how the assessment is to be carried out. Further, there are no provisions requiring any notice to the patient that his or her capacity to provide or withhold consent for a particular study is being questioned. Consequently, there is no provision for review of a determination of lack of capacity at the patient's request so as to satisfy due process requirements. Moreover, once a patient is deemed incapable (14 NYCRR 527.10 [d] [6]), his or her ability to have an objection to participation or continued participation honored is severely, and in some instances impermissibly curtailed by provisions allowing for override of the objection without notice to the patient or patient's representative based upon the finding of a psychiatrist that the intervention or procedure involved holds out a prospect of some undefined "direct benefit" (see, 14 NYCRR 527.10 [e] [2] [viii]) and for surrogate consent by individuals other than legal guardians and/or individuals designated pursuant to a durable power of attorney (see, 14 NYCRR 527.10 [e] [2] [iv]). The substantive and procedural safeguards applicable to surrogate decision-making in life-sustaining treatment settings are also applicable to nontherapeutic greater than minimal risk experimentation. In addition, the provisions of the regulations that allow for consent to be obtained on behalf of minors for participation in such experimentation from the minor's parent or legal guardian, or, where no parent or guardian is available, from an adult family member involved in making treatment decisions for the child (14 NYCRR 527.10 [e] [3] [i]) are also unacceptable. To the extent that the regulations appear to authorize a determination that a particular child or population of children is abused or neglected, or that, in a given situation, the requirement of parental consent may be harmful to the minor, they conflict with the long-established procedures for the protection of children established in the Social Services Law and the Family Court Act.

People v. Abrams


58731


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


232 A.D.2d 240; 649 N.Y.S.2d 5; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10104



October 15, 1996, Decided  
October 15, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) convicting him of sodomy and sexual abuse. Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence and the admission of the testimony of an emergency room doctor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for sodomy in the first degree and sexual abuse in the first degree. Court stated that, viewing the evidence in a light most favorable to the state and giving them the benefit of every reasonable inference, the evidence was legally sufficient to support every element of the crimes charged, including the element of forcible compulsion. Court held that testimony of the emergency room doctor that the complainant's behavior after the attack was common among victims of a sexual assault was properly admitted into evidence despite the fact that the doctor was never qualified as an expert. Finally, court held that because counsel failed to object to testimony concerning the rape trauma syndrome, or the court's charges on forcible compulsion, those challenges had not been preserved for appellate review. Finding no errors, court affirmed convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, finding that evidence was sufficient to support conviction, and testimony of emergency room doctor was properly admitted even though he was not certified as expert.

Community Sch. Bd. Nine v. Crew


58455, 58456, 58457


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


224 A.D.2d 8; 648 N.Y.S.2d 81; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10094



October 10, 1996, Decided  
October 10, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the chancellor of the board of education of the city of New York (chancellor), appealed orders of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which granted petitions in separate cases by respondents, school board members of two different school districts, to vacate the chancellor's orders suspending them from office.


OVERVIEW: The chancellor issued administrative orders suspending several school board members in two school districts after an investigation aired on television which charged the school boards with improprieties. In two separate suits, school board members sought to vacate the orders, and the chancellor filed cross-motions to dismiss on the ground that the school board members had not exhausted their administrative remedies. In both suits the trial courts vacated the order of suspension and denied the chancellor's cross-motion. On appeal, the court held that the trial courts erred in concluding that the school board members were not required to exhaust their administrative remedies prior to initiating court proceedings. The court pointed out that administrative remedies existed pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-1(2) and related regulations. There were exceptions that could be made to the rule, in the trial court's discretion; however, the court held that questions regarding the operation of the public school system were matters for the professional judgment of educators and government officials.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the trial courts.


HEADNOTES

Schools - Administrative Personnel - Removal or Suspension of Community School Board Member by Chancellor - Exhaustion of Administrative Appeal Procedure CPLR article 78 proceedings challenging the removal and suspension of members of a Community School Board by the Chancellor of the Board of Education of the City of New York for misfeasance in office must be dismissed because of petitioners' failure to exhaust their administrative remedies prior to initiating court proceedings. Judicial intervention in the administration of the public school system should be limited to exceptional circumstances involving "gross violations of defined public policy". The remedies sought by petitioners exist within the administrative review process, and that process has routinely entertained claims by Community School Boards that a Chancellor has wrongfully removed or suspended them from office. Supreme Court's assumption of jurisdiction on the ground that the Chancellor's actions exceeded his statutory grant of power pursuant to Education Law § 2590-l undermines the administrative review provisions of the Education Law. By virtue of establishing an extensive administrative appeal procedure, the Legislature clearly intended that these controversies be addressed within that arena.

People v. Cosselmon


1206

Almor Assocs. v. Town of Skaneateles


1144


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


231 A.D.2d 863; 647 N.Y.S.2d 316; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14256



September 27, 1996, Decided  
September 27, 1996, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York) that denied its motion to dismiss the second amended complaint of the respondent corporation.


OVERVIEW: The corporation filed suit against the town regarding the town's zoning regulations. The lower court denied the town's motion to dismiss the corporation's second amended complaint but the court reversed. The court found that the first five causes of action were time-barred by the six-year statute of limitations contained within N.Y. C.P. L.R. 213(1). The court found that the corporation's remaining claim was based on the town's alleged agreement whereby the corporation would terminate the litigation in exchange for rezoning of some commercial property. The court found this claim should also have been dismissed because the town could not contract away or in any manner limit or impair the discretionary authority of future legislative bodies in an area relating to governmental or legislative functions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court that denied the town's motion to dismiss the second amended complaint of the corporation.

In re Donald MM


74913


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


231 A.D.2d 810; 647 N.Y.S.2d 312; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9377



September 19, 1996, Decided  
September 19, 1996, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent appealed an order of the Family Court of Tioga County (New York), which placed him in petitioner's custody after he was adjudicated to be in violation of the terms of his conditional discharge.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was adjudicated a juvenile delinquent and given a conditional discharge for a period of 12 months. Petitioner alleged that respondent had violated the terms of his discharge. The family court adjudicated respondent to be in violation of the terms of his conditional discharge; accordingly, it placed him in petitioner's custody. Respondent sought review. On appeal, the court held that the family court had properly denied respondent's motion to dismiss the petition as untimely. Specifically, the court held that the petition, through N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 360.2(4), operated to toll the period of conditional discharge even though that statute only specifically mentions the tolling of a probationary period. The court also determined that the family court's decision to remove respondent from his mother's home was rational and supported by the record. It therefore affirmed the order of the family court.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner's request was timely because statute operated to toll the period of respondent's conditional discharge. Finding sufficient evidence in the record to support the family court's decision to place respondent in petitioner's custody, the court affirmed the underlying order.

City of New York v. Patrolmen's Benevolent Ass'n


58412


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


231 A.D.2d 422; 647 N.Y.S.2d 728; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9199



September 12, 1996, Decided  
September 12, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police union sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss the complaint of respondent city, granted the city's cross-motion for summary judgment, declared that the Board of Collective Bargaining retained exclusive jurisdiction over collective bargaining impasses, and declared 1996 N.Y. Laws 13 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The lower court declared that 1996 N.Y. Laws 13 was unconstitutional because it violated N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2), the home rule provision. The court affirmed and found that contrary to the police union's argument, the statute was not a special rule applicable only to the city, while leaving intact the right of other cities to opt out of the New York State Public Employment Relations Board procedures and create their own procedures. The court found a home rule provisions was essential to the constitutional validity of the statute because it did not serve a substantial state concern and that there was no logical rationale to deem such matters to be of state interest merely because they pertained to police officers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that denied the police union's motion to dismiss the complaint of the city, granted the city's cross-motion for summary judgment, declared that the Board of Collective Bargaining retained exclusive jurisdiction over collective bargaining impasses, and declared 1996 N.Y. Laws 13 unconstitutional.

 

282 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Grumet v. Cuomo


73803


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


225 A.D.2d 4; 647 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8713



August 26, 1996, Decided  
August 26, 1996, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended September 6, 1996.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is reversed, on the law, without costs, summary judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs and it is declared that the Laws of 1994 (ch 241) is unconstitutional as violative of the Establishment Clause of the 1st Amendment of the U.S. Constitution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted summary judgment dismissing the complaint to defendant board of education, in plaintiffs' action challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Educ. Law § 1504(3).


OVERVIEW: A religious village voted to establish its own school district for its special needs children, pursuant to the recently enacted N.Y. Educ. Law § 1504(3). Plaintiffs filed an action challenging the constitutionality of the statute. The board of education filed a motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint, which was granted by the trial court upon a finding that the statute was constitutional as a matter of law. Plaintiffs sought review and the court reversed and, instead, granted summary judgment to plaintiffs, holding that the statute was unconstitutional as a violation of the Establishment Clause of U.S. Const. amend. I. The court concluded that § 1504(3) had simply resurrected a prior law, which had been deemed unconstitutional, by achieving the same result through indirect means. The court noted that while the law was facially neutral, the specific demographics and criteria made it applicable only to the one village in the entire state, which finding was further buttressed by the purpose of its enactment. The court held that the law was not of uniform state-wide application for all special education needs, but merely to provide a separate facility for the village.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had granted summary judgment to the board of education and dismissed the complaint. The court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs and declared the law unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Establishment of Religion -- Validity of Statute Permitting Creation of School District Solely within Municipality to Meet Required Educational Needs

Laws of 1994 (ch 241), which permits a municipality to organize a union free school district consisting of the entire territory of such municipality when the educational interests of the community so require if certain additional requirements are fulfilled, enacted to enable the religious enclave of Satmar Hassidim of the Village of Kiryas Joel to establish a public school to provide special services for their handicapped children outside of the existing public school district, is unconstitutional as violative of the First Amendment's Establishment Clause since the legislation was crafted to permit only the Village of Kiryas Joel to qualify for its special treatment and it is thus a mere subterfuge that does not constitute a generally applicable law setting forth neutral criteria under which a municipality may have a school district of its own. The current law was enacted only 11 days after the United States Supreme Court invalidated the prior legislative attempt to create a separate public school district coterminous with the Village and it brings about the same result as the unconstitutional prior law, the creation of a special school district for the Village of Kiryas Joel and no other municipality in the State. Despite the facial neutrality of the current law's complex series of demographic qualifiers, the demographic criteria selected by the Legislature fulfill no existing educational policy or purpose and limit the range of eligible municipalities to one. Accordingly, plaintiffs are granted summary judgment declaring the challenged law to be unconstitutional.

 

283 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Pandozy v. Murphy


75253


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


222 A.D.2d 145; 646 N.Y.S.2d 400; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7983



July 25, 1996, Decided  
July 25, 1996, Entered



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant stepmother and estate of father of convicted juvenile offender appealed from so much of an order of the Supreme Court, Clinton County (New York), which denied their motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint of plaintiff Commissioner of the Clinton County Department of Social Services (department) seeking reimbursement from them for the expenses incurred by the department as a result of the juvenile's confinement.


OVERVIEW: The department brought an action against defendants seeking reimbursement for the expenses incurred by it as a result of the juvenile's confinement for his conviction for murder in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 30.00(2). Defendants moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint, urging that N.Y. Exec. Law § 528 was unconstitutional where the primary placement was to protect society, rather than to serve the interests of the child. The supreme court denied the motion and they appealed. The court affirmed the order, holding that § 528 did not violate the principles of equal protection, for it merely bestowed upon the supreme court the authority to insist that the parents of an incarcerated minor continue to meet their preexisting duty to support their child, just as they would have been bound to had he not been convicted pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act. § 413(1)(a). The court held that § 528 did not require that defendants defray the entire cost of the juvenile's detention. Only those who were able to pay a part of the cost of maintenance of such child could be directed to do so, and then only in such amounts as the supreme court decided was fair and equitable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the supreme court, which denied defendants' motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint of the department, which sought reimbursement from them for the expenses incurred by the department as a result of the juvenile's confinement.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Support - Liability of Parents for Support of Incarcerated Juvenile - Constitutionality of Reimbursement Statute Executive Law § 528, which provides that the persons responsible for the support of a juvenile committed to the custody of the Division for Youth may be required to pay a part of the maintenance costs incurred as a result of the child's placement, in such amounts "as the court may decide to be fair and equitable", does not violate the principles of equal protection when interpreted to bestow upon the court the authority to insist only that the parents of an incarcerated minor continue to meet their preexisting duty to support their child (Family Ct Act § 413 [1] [a]; § 416), just as they would be bound to had the child not been convicted. Since it would be constitutionally unacceptable to assess a juvenile offender's parents for the additional costs associated with maintaining the security of an institution for the protection of the public, such a charge could not be sustained as "fair and equitable" under the statute. The existence of the duty of support provides a rational basis for imposing an obligation upon the parents of an incarcerated juvenile that is not shared by the parents or other relatives of an imprisoned adult, or by other members of society. Accordingly, the Department of Social Services, which, pursuant to Executive Law § 529, paid one half of the cost of the care, maintenance and supervision for defendant's minor son while he was incarcerated in Division facilities, may seek reimbursement from defendant for the expenses it incurred as a result of the juvenile's confinement, limited to only those amounts attributable to items for which defendant would otherwise have been obligated, namely, those things that are properly classified as elements of support, including food, clothing, shelter and the like (see, Family Ct Act § 416).

Allied-Signal Inc. v. Tax Appeals Tribunal of Dep't of Taxation & Fin.


75023


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


229 A.D.2d 759; 645 N.Y.S.2d 895; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7854



July 18, 1996, Decided  
July 18, 1996, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 15, 1996.



DISPOSITION: The determination is confirmed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought review pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 of a determination by respondent Tax Appeals Tribunal (tribunal) (New York), which sustained respondent tax department's corporation franchise tax assessments imposed under N.Y. Tax Law art. 9-A.


OVERVIEW: After an audit, the tax department issued two notices of deficiency and assessed a penalty. The taxpayer filed a petition challenging the tax assessment. The tribunal denied the taxpayer's challenge and sustained the notice of deficiency. On further appeal, the court affirmed the tribunal's determination. The court found that the tax commissioner correctly asserted that the computation of the investment allocation percentage pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 210(3)(b) was based on the New York activities of the issuers of the stock in which the taxpayer invested, and was not a tax on the investment activities of the taxpayer. The tribunal properly found that the tax was imposed on investment income. It was unnecessary to locate a unitary business relationship to validly impose the tax because the corporations generating the income taxed each had their own connection with the taxing jurisdiction. The taxpayer failed to present proof that the dividends and capital gains were attributable in larger part to factors present in other states but not present in New York. The court concluded that the tribunal properly determined that the tax was not imposed on extraterritorial values.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tribunal's determination, which sustained the tax department's corporation franchise tax assessments imposed on the taxpayer, and dismissed the taxpayer's petition.

Altamore v. Barrios-Paoli


57327


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


229 A.D.2d 333; 645 N.Y.S.2d 472; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7842



July 18, 1996, Decided  
July 18, 1996, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-city residents challenged the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed their N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition for lack of standing. The petition sought to enjoin respondent city personnel director (director) from using a city residency credit with regard to an eligibles list for appointment to city firefighter and to extend and use another eligibles list in which the non-city residents were candidates.


OVERVIEW: The non-city residents passed an examination for appointment to the position of city firefighter and were placed on an eligibles list. The director instituted a residency credit in which city residents received additional credits on the written test. The director let the list that the non-city residents were on expire so that he could begin appointing candidates from a second list in which the city residents had the advantage. On appeal, the court reversed the dismissal of the petition. The non-city residents had passed an examination for the position of firefighter and were placed on the eligibles list for appointment. The non-city residents complained that the director was unlawfully appointing favored candidates from another later list instead of giving them priority. Consequently, the non-city residents were directly and substantially affected by the issue being litigated and had standing. Although the non-city residents did not join the candidates from the second list who were city residents, when the proceeding was commenced, the list of eligibles for the second list had not yet been promulgated and the second list candidates were, thus, not necessary parties at that time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's dismissal of the non-city residents' petition, denied the director's motion to dismiss the petition, and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings. The court directed the director to serve an answer to the petition within 20 days after the date of the court's order. Upon remand, assuming that conditions changed, the trial court could make an order regarding joinder as might be required.

Prodell v. State


75689


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


222 A.D.2d 178; 645 N.Y.S.2d 589; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7865



July 18, 1996, Decided  
July 18, 1996, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4623.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a decision by the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted plaintiff taxpayers' motion for summary judgment and declared unconstitutional Suffolk County, N.Y., Tax Act art. I, § 3 (1983).


OVERVIEW: The lower court had declared the Suffolk County Tax Act (Tax Act) unconstitutional. The court held that the Tax Act had not been shown to be unconstitutional. The court first looked to the Tax Act's stated purpose, which was to prevent school district taxpayers from reaping unwarranted windfalls. The legislature had specifically found that due to their high value, nuclear power facilities paid substantial taxes. As such, they created tax havens resulting in lower taxes in the school districts that contained them. Therefore, if it was determined that a nuclear power facility was overassessed and entitled to a refund, the legislature concluded that the other school districts should not be required to pay the refund. The court was of the view that in distinguishing nuclear power facilities from other types of property uses the legislature could rationally have assumed that if such facilities turned out to be overassessed, any tax refund would impose a greater tax windfall for school districts with such facilities if they did not have to pay the entire refund themselves. Thus, the court held that the Tax Act was rationally related to achieving a legitimate state purpose.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by reversing so much as granted the taxpayers' motion for summary judgment declaring the Tax Act 1983 Amendment was unconstitutional. The court granted the state's cross motion for summary judgment. The court declared that the Tax Act 1983 Amendment had not been shown to be unconstitutional. The court affirmed the judgment as modified.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Taxpayer Action In an action challenging the constitutionality of a law providing that upon any assessment reductions of nuclear power electrical generating facilities, the school districts in which such facilities were located would be responsible for the school tax refunds, the concerns of the school district's taxpayers over the impact of said law on them are sufficient to gain them standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute. Although the standing issue was never raised by defendants in their cross motions and was not addressed by Supreme Court, defendants did allege as affirmative defenses in their answers that plaintiff taxpayers lacked standing; however, those defenses related solely to plaintiff taxpayers and not the school district. Therefore, the issue of the district's standing is not properly before the court.

Constitutional Law - Equal Protection of Laws - School Tax Refunds - Nuclear Power Facilities Suffolk County Tax Act, article I, § 3 as amended by the Laws of 1983 (ch 1018), which provided that upon any assessment reductions of nuclear power electrical generating facilities, the school districts in which such facilities were located, rather than the towns, would be responsible for the school tax refunds, has not been shown to be unconstitutional where plaintiffs have specifically conceded that the Legislature has a legitimate interest in eliminating tax windfalls created by certain highly valued facilities paying substantial amounts of tax, since the classification and the goal are not "wholly disconnected". Inasmuch as nuclear power facilities created "tax havens" resulting in lower taxes in the school districts which contained them, the Legislature could rationally conclude that should a nuclear power facility ultimately be determined to be overassessed and entitled to a refund, other school districts should not be required to pay the refund, given that they did not share in the benefit of the initial overassessment. The fact that there was only one school district actually affected by the law is not fatal, since the statute did not, by its terms, single out or limit itself to only one district. Although plaintiffs attribute an improper intent since only one district was affected by the statute, where there are legitimate purposes to justify the classification whether they are the purposes which actually motivate the Legislature or not, the legislation will still be deemed to have a rational basis. Moreover, merely because the statute applied regardless of the amount determined to be owed as a refund, it cannot be said to violate equal protection principles: the Legislature was warranted in assuming that any refund owed would be substantial, and it was not required to resort to close distinctions or to maintain a precise, scientific uniformity with reference to composition, use or value.

Henry v. Milonas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


229 A.D.2d 921; 645 N.Y.S.2d 227; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8947



July 12, 1996, Decided  
July 12, 1996, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, judges for the County, Family, and Surrogate's Courts, appealed the decision of the Supreme Court, Ontario County (New York), which denied their motion for summary judgment and granted the motion of defendant, the administrative board of the state judicial conference. At issue was whether the disparity between the salaries of judges in different locations violated the judges' rights to equal protection under state and federal law.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Jud. Law § 221(d)-(f) discriminated between the salaries paid to judges for the county, family, and surrogate courts in one county and the salaries paid to judges who sat in the same courts in another county. The judges who received less money filed an action for declaratory judgment and argued that the disparity violated their rights to equal protection under U.S. Const. XIV amend. and N.Y. Const., art. I, § 11. The judges' action named the administrative board of the state judicial conference and related officials as defendants. On cross motions for summary judgment, the supreme court granted the board's motion and dismissed the action. On appeal, the court examined the Judiciary Law and found that differences in salary levels were not based on the distinct duties of the judges at county-level courts; instead, they were based entirely on geography. Because this distinction lacked a rational basis, the court reversed and granted the judges' motion for summary judgment. Because the judges were the prevailing parties under the federal civil rights statute, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, the court remitted the action for a determination of attorney fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision to grant summary judgment to the administrative board of the state judicial conference; instead, the court found that the pertinent provisions of the New York Judiciary Law were unconstitutional and granted summary judgment to the judges. The court remanded for a determination of reasonable attorney fees to be awarded to the judges.

Time Square Books v. City of Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


223 A.D.2d 270; 645 N.Y.S.2d 951; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9026



July 12, 1996, Decided  
July 12, 1996, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant book and video store owners sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), in favor of appellee city, which denied the owners' motion for a preliminary injunction against enforcement of the city's open video viewing booth requirement.


OVERVIEW: The city amended its municipal code to require that video viewing booths be open, ostensibly to prevent the spread of AIDS. Book and video store owners challenged the amendment on grounds of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution. The trial court denied the owners' motion for a preliminary injunction, and the court reversed. The court held that the sexually explicit but nonobscene entertainment displayed in booths at the owners' establishments was a protected form of expression under the First Amendment. The court also held that, under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8, the protection afforded by the guarantees of free press and speech in the New York were often broader than the minimum required by the First Amendment. Accordingly, the court concluded that, relying upon the broad degree of protection afforded free expression by the State constitution, the owners had made a prima facie showing of their right to relief sufficient to warrant the issuance of a preliminary injunction. The court further held that the owners demonstrated a likelihood of success at trial, and infringement of their constitutionally guaranteed right of free expression constituted irreparable injury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and granted the book and video store owners' motion for a preliminary injunction barring enforcement of the municipal code amendment during pendency of the action.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Freedom of Speech - Validity of Open Booth Requirement for Private Viewing of Adult Entertainment - Preliminary Injunction Plaintiffs, the owners of adult entertainment centers which offer enclosed booths for the private screening of sexually explicit movies, have made a prima facie showing pursuant to CPLR 6301 of their right to preliminarily enjoin enforcement of that part of a municipal ordinance requiring that only open booths be used for the private viewing of adult entertainment since, in the absence of proof that the open booth requirement is no broader than necessary to accomplish the legitimate governmental objective of preventing the transmission of AIDS and other sexually transmitted diseases, plaintiffs are likely to succeed on the merits of their challenge to the open booth requirement under the State Constitution's broad guarantee of free expression (NY Const, art I, § 8). Nothing in the record supports defendants' position that prohibiting the private viewing of motion pictures or adult entertainment in closed booths is necessary to serve their legitimate governmental objective. Defendants have failed to sufficiently demonstrate that "high-risk sexual contact" has occurred in closed booths in plaintiffs' establishments and thereby threatened the public health or that plaintiffs were aware of or condoned sexual activity on their premises. Defendants have also failed to demonstrate that less restrictive alternatives, including enforcement of the newly enacted requirements of solid walls and one person per booth, would be any less effective in meeting their objective than opening the booths to public view. Furthermore, plaintiffs satisfied their burden of demonstrating that they will suffer irreparable injury from the infringement of their constitutionally guaranteed right of free expression if the preliminary injunction is not granted and that a balancing of the equities weighs in their favor in view of the evidence that they will sustain a significant loss of business if the open booth requirement is enforced.

 

289 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Astoria Fed. S&L Ass'n v. State


94-08656


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


222 A.D.2d 36; 644 N.Y.S.2d 926; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7700


March 4, 1996, Argued  
July 8, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4600. As Amended September 20, 1996.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff savings and loan appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which denied the savings and loan's motion for summary judgment and granted the cross-motions of defendants, the State and City, for summary judgment. The trial court declared that interest earned on bonds issued by the New York City Municipal Water Finance Authority (Authority) was not exempt from State and City franchise tax.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the declaration and orders of the trial court and held that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1045-u(3), as originally enacted, did not exempt bonds issued by the Authority and the interest they generated from inclusion in the savings and loan's total net income for the purpose of calculating the savings and loan's City and State franchise tax obligations. The court stated that to rule otherwise would render § 1045-u (3) discriminatory and unconstitutional under 31 U.S.C.S. § 3124(a), because the interest from the State's public authority bonds would be exempted from income used to determine franchise taxes, while the interest from comparable federal obligations would not receive a similar benefit. The court also noted that both the State and City franchise tax statutes expressly required that bond interest be included in any calculation of a corporation's entire net income. Accordingly, if the savings and loan's reading of N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1045-u(3), were to be adopted, the provision in question would conflict with N.Y. Tax Law § 1453 and New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 11, § 602 et seq. (2000).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the City and the State.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Franchise Tax on Business Corporations - Exemptions - Bonds Issued by New York City Municipal Water Finance Authority Public Authorities Law § 1045-u (3), as originally enacted, did not exempt the bonds issued by the New York City Municipal Water Finance Authority and the interest they generate from inclusion in a corporate bondholder's entire net income for the purpose of calculating the corporation's New York State and New York City franchise tax obligations, since it is well established that interest paid on State revenue bonds which is exempt from direct taxation is not exempt from indirect taxation, and a franchise tax is an indirect tax. Courts have traditionally ruled that such interest must be included in the measure of a corporation's total net income for purposes of calculating the corporation's franchise tax obligation, even when the governing statute provides that the income from a municipal bond, for example, shall at all times be free from taxation, except for estate and gift taxes and taxes on transfers. Moreover, since 1933, the Legislature has not passed a single enabling statute whereby a public authority has been empowered to issue a revenue bond whose interest would be exempt from inclusion in the measure of a corporate holder's income for the purpose of computing its franchise taxes. Although the statute was inartfully drafted and ambiguous, plaintiff has failed to carry its heavy burden of showing its entitlement to the claimed exemption: given that ambiguity, the court may look to the legislative history to aid in its interpretation and that history gives no indication that the bond interest would be excludable from a corporation's entire net income. Additionally, the bonds were not marketed on the basis of the unusual tax advantage asserted by plaintiff, and plaintiff's construction would conflict with other taxing statutes, would render the statutory scheme violative of Federal law, because it would result in discriminatory treatment of similar Federal obligations, and would conflict with the Tax Commissioner's interpretation, which given his expertise, should be deferred to by the courts.

Giuliani v. Hevesi


58369, 58370, 58371


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


228 A.D.2d 348; 644 N.Y.S.2d 265; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7334



June 25, 1996, Decided  
June 25, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, mayor and council, sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed their action against appellee Comptroller of the City of New York. Appellant environmental group sought review of the judgment of the same court which dismissed its action against appellee New York City Municipal Water Finance Authority (Authority). The appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: A mayor proposed to finance the sale of the city water system to the New York City Water Board by bonds issued by the Authority. When the Comptroller refused to recommend the issuance of the bonds, the mayor and council sought declaratory and injunctive relief to compel the recommendation. An environmental group filed an action to prevent the sale. The court held that the there was no statutory support for the Authority to issue the proposed bonds, so the Comptroller lawfully refused to act under N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1045-o. The court held that the financing plan was unlawful because it was an unconstitutional tax. The rates paid became general revenue, so non-city rate payers did not receive a benefit from the rate they paid. The court held that the proposal was invalid under the New York Environmental Quality Review Act because no environmental impact study was performed and because it caused additional debt which might have eliminated or delayed necessary water and sewer projects. The court held that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1045-h(1) was inconsistent with the New York City Charter provisions authorizing the sale, so state law prevailed.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the mayor and council's complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief was affirmed. The dismissal of the environmental group's claim that the plan was invalid was reversed, but the dismissal of the remainder of its claims was affirmed.

Hudson View II Assocs. v. Gooden


55689N


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


222 A.D.2d 163; 644 N.Y.S.2d 512; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7346



June 25, 1996, Decided  
June 25, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff limited partnerships sued defendants, general partners and managing agents for breach of contract and conversion. Defendants counterclaimed. The Supreme Court (New York), denied plaintiffs' motion to strike a jury demand and sua sponte determined that the jury would serve in an advisory capacity as to equitable claims. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sued defendants for breach of contract and conversion, and sought to have defendants removed as general partners and managing agents. Defendants asserted counterclaims for the recover of the reasonable commercial value of services performed and reimbursement for expenditures. Upon defendants' demand for a trial by jury, plaintiffs moved to strike the demand on the ground that defendants had no right to a jury trial on their equitable defenses. Plaintiffs also claimed that by asserting those equitable defenses defendants waived their right to a jury on plaintiffs' legal claims. The court affirmed the order holding that (1) considering the main thrust of plaintiffs' action and the relief sought, there was no constitutional impediment to finding defendants equitable defenses must be tried in accordance with N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 4101 (2) the mere assertion by defendants of equitable defendants could not, without concomitant assertions of equitable counter claims, result in a waiver of the right to a jury, and (3) since defendants' counterclaims were not equitable, by asserting their counterclaim defendants had not waived their right to a jury trial on plaintiffs' legal claims.


OUTCOME: The order, which denied plaintiffs' motion to strike a jury demand and sua sponte determined that the jury would serve in an advisory capacity as to equitable claims, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Trial - Right to Jury Trial - Waiver - Assertion of Equitable Defenses and Counterclaims In an action by plaintiffs, limited partnerships that own two housing projects, to, inter alia, recover damages arising out of the alleged mismanagement of the buildings by defendants, the mere assertion by defendants of equitable defenses to which they are not entitled to a jury trial pursuant to CPLR 4101 is not alone sufficient to constitute a waiver of defendants' right to a jury trial on plaintiffs' legal claims. Similarly, defendants' additional assertion of counterclaims for the recovery of the "reasonable commercial value" of their services as well as reimbursement for expenditures made for plaintiffs' benefit does not constitute a waiver of their right to a jury trial on plaintiffs' legal claims since such counterclaims seeking money damages only are predicated on principles of contract and/or quasi-contract and are thus "legal", not "equitable" within the contemplation of CPLR 4101, notwithstanding that the rationale underlying the counterclaims is fairness and equitable principles in a general, rather than legal, sense. Furthermore, since plaintiffs' equitable claims, including the cause of action seeking to remove defendants as general partners and managing agents of the buildings based on their alleged breach of fiduciary duty, as well as defendants' equitable defenses are so intertwined with the legal claims as to make one trial of all the causes of action appropriate, Supreme Court properly determined that the jury would serve in an advisory capacity as to those issues of fact which relate solely to the parties' equitable claims.

 

292 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Imperial Gardens v. Town of Wallkill


94-09278


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


228 A.D.2d 562; 644 N.Y.S.2d 528; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7215



June 17, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] This Opinion Substituted on Grant of Rehearing for Vacated Opinion of February 26, 1996, Previously Reported at: 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1876.



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is modified by deleting therefrom the provision awarding the plaintiffs the principal sum of $ 775,923 as reimbursement for the water and sewer connection fees and traffic impact fees that the plaintiffs paid to the defendant and substituting therefor a provision awarding the plaintiffs $ 252,000, as reimbursement for the water and sewer connection fees that the plaintiffs paid to the defendant under protest; as so modified, the judgment is affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements, and the matter is remitted to the Supreme Court, Orange County, for a trial on the issue of the plaintiffs' payment of traffic impact fees to the defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent corporation brought a motion for reargument of an appeal from a judgment in favor of the corporation as to water and sewer connection and traffic impact fees paid to appellant town under duress.


OVERVIEW: The corporation brought an action seeking reimbursement for water and sewer connection fees that it paid under protest. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the corporation, the town appealed, and the corporation brought a motion for reargument of the appeal. The court affirmed the trial court, holding that the corporation was entitled to reimbursement because it paid the fees involuntarily as indicated by the inscription "under protest" on the checks tendered to the town. The court held that the corporation was not entitled to reimbursement of the remaining water and sewer connection fees paid because they had not established that the payments that were not made under protest were tendered under duress. The existence of numerous issues of fact regarding the circumstances surrounding the corporation's payment of traffic impact fees to the town precluded an award of summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment, substituted a modified judgment in favor of the corporation as to checks paid to the town under protest, and affirmed the judgment as modified. The court denied the corporation's motion for summary judgment as to the traffic impact fees and remitted the case for trial.

Idant Lab. v. Department of Health


57063


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


227 A.D.2d 339; 643 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6151



May 30, 1996, Decided  
May 30, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner laboratory, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, sought review of a cease and desist order issued by respondent, Department of Health of the City of New York, which prohibited the laboratory from analyzing semen samples and denied the laboratory's application for a clinical laboratory permit.


OVERVIEW: After the citation of the laboratory for the violations that prompted the city department to issue its cease and desist order, the public health law was amended to confer exclusive authority to license and regulate clinical laboratories on the New York State Department of Health. The court found merit to the laboratory's' contentions that the state had preempted the field, and that the specific regulation on which the city department based its requirement for a certificate of qualification was unconstitutionally vague. However, the court held that it was dispositive to its review that the statutory basis of the city department's authority in that area had been abolished by legislative enactment. In the absence of any statutory basis to act in respect to the regulation of a tissue bank, the authority of the city department was neither expressly conferred nor necessarily inferable. Accordingly, the order of the city department was annulled.


OUTCOME: The cease and desist order that prohibited the laboratory from analyzing semen samples and denied the laboratory's application for a clinical laboratory permit was annulled.

 

294 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Marine Midland Bank, N.A. v. United Mo. Bank, N.A.


56252


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


223 A.D.2d 119; 643 N.Y.S.2d 528; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6035



May 28, 1996, Decided  
May 28, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, assignee of a promissory note and security agreement executed by decedent, appealed from a judgment of Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied the assignee's summary judgment motion on its suit to recover on defaulted note and security agreement and granted defendant executor's cross motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The note required decedent, a Kansas resident, to make payments to the note holder at its New York headquarters. It also provided that New York law would govern an action to enforce the note. The security agreement provided that decedent consented to the exercise of in personam jurisdiction in New York. After decedent's death, the executor commenced probate proceedings in Kansas. Under the Kansas Probate Code, creditors who failed to file a claim with the probate court within four months from the date of a notice to creditors were barred from asserting their claims by the Kansas nonclaim statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-2239(1). The trial court dismissed the assignee's complaint on the ground the Kansas probate statute barred its claim. The court held that New York law applied in this action and that, because the executor had failed to establish any viable defense, summary judgment should have been awarded to the assignee. Choice of law provisions were valid and enforceable in New York. Further, the U.C.C. provided that where, as here, a transaction bore a reasonable relation to New York, the parties could agree that New York law would govern their rights and duties (U.C.C. § 1-105).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court, reinstated the complaint and granted the assignee's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Conflict of Laws - Law Governing Contract Actions New York law is applicable in an action against a deceased Kansas debtor's estate seeking recovery on a debt which accrued in New York under an agreement providing that New York law would govern in any action for its enforcement. Supreme Court erred in holding that the Kansas statute governing claims against a decedent's estate, which is not subject to any tolling provisions and which bars all claims that have not been "exhibit[ed]" within four months, regardless of their nature or whether they have accrued, is a substantive rule of law, rather than a purely procedural Statute of Limitations and, therefore, is here controlling. Choice of law provisions such as those contained in the documents involved in this transaction, including the promissory note of which enforcement is sought, are valid and enforceable in this State. Moreover, the UCC provides that as long as a transaction bears a reasonable relation to this State, the parties may agree that New York law shall govern their rights and duties (UCC 1-105). Here, the transaction bears a reasonable relationship to New York, and no viable reason has been presented to warrant excluding the transaction from the choice of law provision agreed to by the parties. Clearly, the relevant public policy considerations support the upholding of the New York choice of law provision agreed to by the parties with respect to enforcement of the transaction in issue. Finally, decedent consented to personal jurisdiction in New York and to service by certified mail, and New York courts are not divested of subject matter jurisdiction by the Kansas statute which grants the Kansas probate court exclusive jurisdiction over all controversies involving Kansas estates. Comity does not require or suggest that the courts of this State should surrender their interest in adjudicating disputes which, like this one, have significant contacts with the commerce of this State. Since plaintiff has established that it is the holder of the note sued upon, and defendant has failed to establish that it has any viable defense, summary judgment should be awarded in plaintiff's favor.

Lemoine v. New York City Transit Auth.


95-05927


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C. P. L. However, the court concluded that the trial court correctly concluded that the determination of the Commission was not in violation of the Constitution or of the laws of this State and that it did not act illegally, unconstitutionally, or in excess of its jurisdiction and the determination is therefore beyond judicial review. Therefore, the court affirmed the decision.


OUTCOME: The trial court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied the employee's petition to review the denial of back pay during the period of the employee's suspension.

 

296 of 992 DOCUMENTS


McCall v. State


75506


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


219 A.D.2d 136; 640 N.Y.S.2d 347; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3468



April 4, 1996, Decided  
April 4, 1996, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state officials sought review of a decision of the lower court (New York), which, in consolidated declaratory judgment actions, granted appellee state officials' motions for summary judgment declaring Laws of 1995 (ch. 119, §§ 13, 16) unconstitutional and the supplemental retirement allowances provided for pursuant to Laws of 1995 (ch. 119, §§ 1-11) be payable on September 1, 1995.


OVERVIEW: The legislature passed a law which granted state and municipal employers a credit, to be assessed against the assets of the supplemental reserve fund (SRF), equal to the amount of the additional contributions they would otherwise have had to make pursuant to the comptroller's restoration plan, L 1995, ch. 119, § 13. The legislation also increased the supplemental retirement allowances paid to those retirees formerly receiving them and provided such allowances for the first time to those who retired between 1983 and 1989. Section 16 of the legislation conditioned the commencement date of the benefit increase on the existence, and outcome, of litigation challenging the credit mechanism, L 1995, ch. 119, § 16. The lower court found §§ 13, 16 unconstitutional. The court held § 13 intruded on the comptroller's freedom to manage the funds in the manner he considered to be most fiscally appropriate and such an infringement was constitutionally impermissible. The court found § 16 was simply a device whereby the legislature made unmistakably clear its intention with respect to how the remainder of the statute was to be treated if the credit provision was found to be illegal.


OUTCOME: The court declared unconstitutional that portion of the statute conditioning the payment of benefits on the commencement or existence of litigation but upheld the remainder of the section and ordered that the supplemental allowances be paid, in accordance with the express terms of the statute as modified, as of September 1996.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service - Retirement and Pension Benefits - Supplemental Retirement Allowances - Invalid Impairment of Pension Benefits Laws of 1995 (ch 119, § 13), which, in response to the judicial invalidation of the Legislature's attempt in 1990 to reduce public employer contributions to the Common Retirement Fund (CRF) by mandating a new funding method for the CRF, inter alia, grants the State and municipal employers a credit, to be assessed against the assets of the supplemental reserve fund (SRF), equal to the amount of additional pension contributions they would otherwise have had to make pursuant to plaintiff State Comptroller's plan to restore funds of the CRF to the level dictated by the original funding method, is unconstitutional as violative of the Impairment Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art V, § 7). Regardless of whether the SRF is actually considered a separate fund, or merely a portion of the constitutionally protected pension accumulation fund, it is indisputably an asset of the retirement system. The Comptroller is vested with the power to hold, manage and invest the assets contained in the pension funds for the benefit of the members and beneficiaries of the retirement systems, and for the protection of the taxpayers. It is implicit in the Constitution's assurance that pension benefits shall not be impaired, that the funds from which those benefits are drawn are to be protected, and that pension beneficiaries are entitled to the independent judgment of the Comptroller in managing the system's assets. Since 1970, employees becoming members of the retirement system have had a right to rely on the Comptroller's independent judgment in the management of the assets comprising the SRF. Section 13 directly infringes upon this contractual right, for it intrudes upon the Comptroller's freedom to manage the funds in the manner he considers to be most fiscally appropriate. In addition, section 16 of the legislation, which provides that the increased supplemental retirement benefits bestowed by other provisions of the legislation be delayed in the event litigation is initiated challenging section 13, is likewise unconstitutional to the extent that the payment of benefits is conditioned solely on the existence or commencement of litigation. However, the component of section 16 which prescribes September 1996 as an alternate effective date if section 13 is declared unconstitutional is valid and, therefore, the supplemental allowances must be paid as of that date.

Alston v. Transport Workers Union


56765


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


225 A.D.2d 424; 639 N.Y.S.2d 359; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2753



March 21, 1996, Decided  
March 21, 1996, ENTERED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted defendant labor union's motion to dismiss the employee's complaint as barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: The employee alleged that the labor union breached its duty of fair representation when it failed to properly represent him in connection with various disciplinary proceedings that eventually led to his dismissal. The supreme court granted the labor union's motion to dismiss the complaint as barred by the statute of limitations. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the employee's cause of action, based on the date it accrued, was immediately barred under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217(2)(1) upon its effective date. While the court recognized that the legislature intended N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217 to have retrospective effect, it held that it would have been unconstitutional to apply the four month limitation period to the employee's claim. The court noted that the only restriction upon the legislature, in the enactment of statutes of limitation, was that a reasonable time was allowed for suits upon causes of action theretofore existing. If a statute of limitations deprived a party of a reasonable time within which suit may be brought, the court held that it violated the constitutional provision that no person should have been deprived of property without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the supreme court, which granted the labor union's motion to dismiss the employee's claim that the labor union breached its duty of fair representation.

Sand Hill Assocs. v. Legislature of Suffolk


95-04006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


225 A.D.2d 681; 640 N.Y.S.2d 128; 1996 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2637


February 2, 1996, Argued  
March 18, 1996, Decided



DISPOSITION: ORDERED that the judgment is affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Suffolk County Legislature (county legislature) appealed a decision from the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York) that granted plaintiff property owners' motion for summary judgment in the property owners' action challenging a resolution adopted by the legislature. The resolution authorized the collection of a fee for each late property tax payment to offset advertising costs.


OVERVIEW: The county legislature adopted a resolution that authorized the county treasurer to collect an application fee for each late or delinquent real property tax payment to offset the cost of advertising the sale of land for unpaid taxes. The property owners failed to timely pay their real property taxes, and the tax redemption statements for each parcel included the newly enacted fee, which the property owners paid under protest. In the property owners' action challenging the resolution, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the property owners. In affirming the summary judgment in favor of the property owners, the court found that the purpose of the Suffolk County Tax Act § 13-a(3), 1920 N.Y. Laws ch. 311, was to enumerate all of the penalties and fees that could be imposed on the amount of unpaid taxes. The state legislature did not intend to authorize the recovery of the administrative costs of advertising land for sale.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor the property owners in their action challenging the resolution authorizing the collection of a fee for each late property tax payment to offset advertising costs.
