470 Newport Assocs. v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


70100


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


211 A.D.2d 322; 627 N.Y.S.2d 1020; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5629



May 25, 1995, Decided  
May 25, 1995, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer challenged respondent municipal tribunal's decision as to petitioner's tax liability from a specified real estate transaction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayer sought reversal of respondent municipal tribunal's decision refusing to grant petitioner's request for redetermination of its tax liability. The court affirmed. Petitioner had been assessed tax on a gain from the sale of property, plus a penalty and interest, that it considered excessive. Petitioner noted that other joint owners had been involved in the same sale of property, but their basis was lower than the co-owners (and thus their gain greater) due to the nature of their transaction. The other owners were allowed to use fair market value, whereas petitioner was not since it sold its interest to a housing cooperative. Petitioner contended that this difference constituted an unconstitutional difference that violated its equal protection rights. On appeal, the court rejected this argument, noting that the state's highest court had already rejected a similar equal protection argument.


OUTCOME: Judgment was entered for respondent. Petitioner was liable for the taxes, penalty, and interest assessed by respondent, and its equal protection claim failed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Real Property Transfer Gains Tax - Cooperative Conversion Where petitioner, a limited partnership, acquired an apartment building in 1976 for $ 5,750,000, sold the building to a cooperative housing corporation (CHC) in 1982 for $ 15,773,302.20 with part of the consideration consisting of unsold shares of the CHC valued at $ 6,935,162.57, and then between March 28, 1983 (the effective date of Tax Law art 31-B) and September 1986, sold shares to individual tenant/shareholders valued at $ 4,832,602, respondent Department of Taxation and Finance properly determined that petitioner had a gain on the sale of shares based on the price it initially paid for the property and thus owed a 10% real property transfer tax on the gain (Tax Law § 1441) in the amount of $ 445,581 plus a penalty of $ 72,634 and interest of $ 40,174. The fact that petitioner's "original purchase price" is different from that of its individual partners on the sale of the same stock of the CHC for purposes of the transfer gains tax because of the "exception" for pre-March 29, 1983 transfers, which allows the partners' original purchase price to be "stepped up" to the value of the shares at the time of the transfers (20 NYCRR former 590.35 [f], renum 590.36 [f], eff Nov. 9, 1994), does not violate petitioner's equal protection rights. The challenged regulation advances a legitimate State interest by shielding that portion of the partners' gain which preceded the effective date of Tax Law article 31-B from the gains tax. In addition, allowing petitioner to "step up" its original purchase price of the property to its fair market value at the time of transfer to the CHC would contravene the well-settled principle that a cooperative conversion is a single transfer for gains tax purposes with the gain being measured by the difference between the consideration received upon the sale of the shares and the transferor's cost of acquiring the property prior to its transfer to the CHC.

Taxation - Real Property Transfer Gains Tax - Cooperative Conversion Where petitioner, a limited partnership, acquired an apartment building in 1976 for $ 5,750,000, sold the building to a cooperative housing corporation (CHC) in 1982 for $ 15,773,302.20 with part of the consideration consisting of unsold shares of the CHC valued at $ 6,935,162.57, and then between March 28, 1983 (the effective date of Tax Law art 31-B) and September 1986, sold shares to individual tenant/shareholders valued at $ 4,832,602, respondent Department of Taxation and Finance properly determined that petitioner had a gain on the sale of shares and thus owed a 10% real property transfer tax on the gain (Tax Law § 1441) in the amount of $ 445,581 plus a penalty of $ 72,634 and interest of $ 40,174. The fact that different tax consequences arise from the sale of the CHC shares to tenant/shareholders and the sale of shares of a noncooperative corporation that owns real estate does not violate petitioner's equal protection rights since the Legislature may constitutionally apply a different tax treatment to condominium and cooperative developers than it applies to other types of real estate developers.

Taxation - Real Property Transfer Gains Tax - Cooperative Conversion - Penalty for Untimely Payment of Tax Where petitioner, a limited partnership, acquired an apartment building in 1976 for $ 5,750,000, sold the building to a cooperative housing corporation (CHC) in 1982 for $ 15,773,302.20 with part of the consideration consisting of unsold shares of the CHC valued at $ 6,935,162.57, and then between March 28, 1983 (the effective date of Tax Law art 31-B) and September 1986, sold shares to individual tenant/shareholders valued at $ 4,832,602, respondent Department of Taxation and Finance properly determined that petitioner had a gain on the sale of shares and thus owed a 10% real property transfer tax on the gain (Tax Law § 1441) in the amount of $ 445,581 plus a penalty of $ 72,634 and interest of $ 40,174. Petitioner is not entitled to an abatement of the penalty imposed for its failure to timely pay the transfer gains tax (Tax Law § 1446 [2] [b]). Since petitioner could have readily ascertained its tax liability based upon respondent's 1983 publication stating that, for purposes of the gains tax, the original purchase price used in calculating the gain on the sale of cooperative shares "is the original purchase price ... in the hands of the realty transferor", there is no reason to disturb the determination of the Tax Appeals Tribunal sustaining the penalty imposed.

 

331 of 992 DOCUMENTS


People v. Concert Connection


93-08324


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


211 A.D.2d 310; 629 N.Y.S.2d 254; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5395


January 6, 1995, Argued  
May 22, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised August 28, 1995.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a ticket broker and its president, sought review of an order and judgment of the Supreme Court in Westchester County (New York), which denied their motion to dismiss and granted the petition for a permanent injunction against their continued violation of New York's ticket-scalping statute, N.Y. Arts and Cult. Aff. Law § 25.01 et seq.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court initially held that broker and president were subject to the jurisdiction of the New York courts even though neither one was a New York resident. New York's long-arm statute, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302, permitted its courts to exercise personal jurisdiction over a nondomiciliary who contracted anywhere to supply goods or services in the state. Proof of one transaction in New York was sufficient to invoke jurisdiction, so long as a defendant's activities were purposeful and there was a substantial relationship between the transaction and the claim asserted. The court noted that in the instant case, broker had contracted, via telephone, to resell tickets to New York residents and shipped those tickets into New York. The court then upheld the constitutionality of the ticket-scalping statute, as the regulation of ticket sales to protect the public against fraud was a legitimate government interest and did not offend notions of due process. The court concluded that the imposition of injunctive relief under the valid statute was appropriate, noting that the statute violated neither the Equal Protection Clause nor the Commerce Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment under review.


HEADNOTES

Consumer Protection - "Ticket Scalping" - Personal Jurisdiction over Nonresident Ticket Sellers A violation of New York's "ticket scalping" law (Arts and Cultural Affairs Law § 25.01 et seq.), which prohibits reselling or offering to resell tickets to any place of entertainment in New York for more than the "maximum premium price" permitted except for certain exempt ticket sellers, provides a proper basis for the exercise of personal jurisdiction over nonresidents engaged in the business of reselling entertainment tickets at places of entertainment located in New York in a proceeding commenced by the Attorney-General pursuant to Executive Law § 63 (12) to enjoin "repeated" illegal acts and obtain restitution for eligible consumers where defendants had certain minimum contacts with New York. In this case, when the corporate defendant, a Connecticut corporation, contracted to send tickets to purchasers located in New York for New York events at prices far in excess of the "maximum premium price", it was subjecting itself to personal jurisdiction for any cause of action that arose out of those contracts (see, CPLR 302 [a] [1]). Moreover, defendant purposefully availed itself of business opportunities in New York by advertising in New York newspapers, maintaining two New York telephone numbers, reselling tickets for New York events to New York residents, and actually shipping those tickets into New York. These acts are clearly sufficient to establish the minimum contacts with this State necessary to satisfy constitutional due process requirements as well as to place defendant squarely within the reach of New York's long-arm statute. Furthermore, Supreme Court properly concluded that it had personal jurisdiction over the individual defendant, the president of the corporate defendant. Since the record establishes the existence of sufficient minimum contacts to subject the individual defendant to personal jurisdiction in the New York courts pursuant to CPLR 302 (a) (1), he may not hide behind the corporate entity.

Consumer Protection - "Ticket Scalping" - Constitutionality of "Ticket Scalping" Law - Due Process New York's "ticket scalping" law (Arts and Cultural Affairs Law § 25.01 et seq.), which prohibits reselling or offering to resell tickets to any place of entertainment in New York for more than the "maximum premium price" permitted except for certain exempt ticket sellers, does not violate the Due Process Clause. The regulation of ticket sales to protect the public against fraud, extortion, exorbitant rates and similar abuses is a legitimate governmental interest and does not offend any notions of due process.

Consumer Protection - "Ticket Scalping" - Constitutionality of "Ticket Scalping" Law - Equal Protection New York's "ticket scalping" law (Arts and Cultural Affairs Law § 25.01 et seq.), which prohibits reselling or offering to resell tickets to any place of entertainment in New York for more than the "maximum premium price" permitted except for certain exempt ticket sellers, does not violate the Equal Protection Clause in distinguishing between the resale prices that may be charged by different classes of ticket sellers. The statute treats all ticket brokers alike while exempting certain other classes of ticket sellers from the application of the "maximum premium price" that may be charged such as theater owners and not-for-profit corporations. The statute makes a valid distinction between those who would lawfully recover their expenses and those who would gain substantial profits from the unlawful reselling of tickets. Furthermore, the exemption of not-for-profit organizations is rationally related to the legitimate government interest in the promotion of charitable activities. Thus, so long as the subject entity wholly dedicates the profits realized from the resale of tickets to the purpose for which it was granted "exempt" status, it is acting within the specific exemptions of the statute and in accordance with the legitimate government interest.

Consumer Protection - "Ticket Scalping" - Constitutionality of "Ticket Scalping" Law - Commerce Clause New York's "ticket scalping" law (Arts and Cultural Affairs Law § 25.01 et seq.), which prohibits reselling or offering to resell tickets to any place of entertainment in New York for more than the "maximum premium price" permitted except for certain exempt ticket sellers, is not violative of the Commerce Clause since the statutory scheme regulates evenhandedly and does not seek to regulate activity that occurs wholly outside the State. The regulation of the resale of tickets within the State is a legitimate exercise of the State's power to protect the interests of its populace which clearly does not unduly burden interstate commerce.

Consumer Protection - "Ticket Scalping" - Personal Liability of Corporate Officer under "Ticket Scalping" Law Defendant, the president of a corporation engaged in the business of reselling entertainment tickets at places of entertainment located in New York, may be held personally liable under New York's "ticket scalping" law (Arts and Cultural Affairs Law § 25.01 et seq.), for his actions as an officer of the corporation in a proceeding commenced by the Attorney-General pursuant to Executive Law § 63 (12) to enjoin "repeated" illegal acts and obtain restitution for eligible consumers. The corporate veil may be pierced to hold corporate officers personally liable for tortious conduct or knowing participation in fraudulent acts. Accordingly, since defendant, as president of the corporation, was aware of the corporation's reselling and offering to resell entertainment tickets for more than the "maximum premium price", he can be held personally liable for violating the "ticket scalping" law. To hold otherwise would be an open invitation to individuals to form shell corporations for the purpose of shielding themselves from liability for illegal business transactions.

Nicolai v. Crosson


94-00847


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


214 A.D.2d 714; 626 N.Y.S.2d 210; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4523


March 6, 1995, Argued  
April 24, 1995, Decided

 

333 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Wellspring Zendo, Inc. v. Trippe


72135


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


211 A.D.2d 23; 625 N.Y.S.2d 334; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4201



April 13, 1995, Decided  
April 13, 1995, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nonprofit religious company sought review from a Supreme Court of Westchester County (New York) judgment dismissing its application in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding to declare a zoning amendment enacted by appellees town board and others requiring a 50-foot buffer zone around its property unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After earlier proceedings between a nonprofit religious company, a town board, and others terminated in a settlement between the parties reserving only the nonprofit religious company's right to contest a proposed 50-foot buffer zoning requirement in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, the nonprofit religious company commenced such a proceeding challenging an amendment to the zoning ordinance requiring such a 50-foot buffer zone. The lower court found against the nonprofit religious company and the nonprofit religious company appealed. The court found that (1) the failure to appeal from an interlocutory order did not waive the arguments at issue because of the later appeal from the lower court's judgment, (2) the conduct of the nonprofit religious company's attorneys at a public hearing demonstrated that the only right not waived was that of contesting the size of the buffer zone, (3) all constitutional and other claims against the buffer zone not attacking the size of the buffer zone were thus waived, (4) the zoning ordinance had been a proper use of police powers and furthered legitimate governmental purposes, and (5) therefore the lower court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment against the nonprofit religious company and in favor of the town board.


HEADNOTES

Appeal - Appeal as of Right In a proceeding to declare a zoning amendment unconstitutional, since the proceeding was commenced as a CPLR article 78 proceeding and was not converted to an action, the interlocutory order denying petitioner's motion to strike respondents' answer was not appealable as of right ( CPLR 5701 [b] [1]). In any event, petitioner's appeal from the judgment brings the interlocutory order up for review ( CPLR 5501 [a] [1]), and thus respondents' reliance on petitioner's failure to appeal the interlocutory order is misplaced.

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Creation of 50-Foot Buffer Screening Zone in Residential Districts - No Interference with Free Exercise of Religion - Waiver A zoning amendment enacted by respondent Town, which, inter alia, requires that a 50-foot buffer screening area be maintained with existing vegetation and natural features along all perimeter boundaries of properties used for permitted nonresidential uses in residential districts, furthers the legitimate governmental purposes of preserving the residential character of the neighborhood and preventing noise and disturbance and thus constitutes a valid exercise of the Town's police power. Petitioner, a not-for-profit religious corporation that owns 25 acres in a residential district, may challenge only the size of the buffer zone created by the Town since it waived all constitutional and other legal challenges to the validity of the buffer requirement itself by conceding, following the settlement of its Federal civil rights action against the Town, that it "would not object to a provision of a buffer of some reasonable dimension from the property line". Petitioner has not met its heavy burden of demonstrating that the size of the buffer interferes with the free exercise of religion of its members to engage in a religious practice known as work practice, which involves silent meditation in the course of working in the care and maintenance of petitioner's property. Furthermore, the buffer requirement does not exclude petitioner's religious use of its property, and the fact that respondent's zoning ordinance imposes different buffer requirements in other districts does not affect the reasonableness of the 50-foot requirement in the residential district where petitioner's property is located, especially in view of the evidence concerning disputes between petitioner and one of its neighbors about petitioner's activities near the boundary.

Actions - Conversion of One Form of Action to Another In a proceeding, inter alia, to declare a zoning amendment unconstitutional, since petitioner's challenge focuses on the application of the zoning amendment to its property, there is no need to convert the proceeding to a declaratory judgment action.

Independence Party v. Board of Elections


54533


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


213 A.D.2d 209; 624 N.Y.S.2d 4; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2757



March 9, 1995, Decided

335 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Sun Co. v. City of Syracuse Indus. Dev. Agency


2041/94


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


209 A.D.2d 34; 625 N.Y.S.2d 371; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3748



February 8, 1995, Decided  
February 8, 1995, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 27, petitioner landowners challenged the findings and determination of respondent, City of Syracuse Industrial Development Agency (lead agency), which condemned property owned by the landowners. The agency sought to use the condemned property for the development of a retail shopping center.


OVERVIEW: After signing an environmental assessment form for the development project, the lead agency accepted the draft environmental impact statement (EIS) and the public comment period under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) commenced. The final EIS was filed and the lead agency issued its findings and determination to acquire the landowners' property. The court rejected the landowners' claim that the statutory condemnation scheme was facially unconstitutional. The court concluded that (1) pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 862(2)(b) the project was exempt from the retail establishment prohibition because the project was located in a highly distressed area as defined in N.Y. Gen. Law § 854(18); (2) that the lead agency's acquisition of the landowners' property served a legitimate public purpose; and (3) that the lead agency had control over the ultimate decision on the project and did not contract away its power of eminent domain. However, the lead agency failed to comply with SEQRA because its site-specific SEQRA review resulted in improper segmentation and the lead agency failed to review the cumulative effect of all phases of the redevelopment plan.


OUTCOME: Because the lead agency did not comply with all of the SEQRA requirements, the court annulled the lead agency's determination to condemn the landowners' property. However, the court held that the statute pursuant to which the lead agency was granted eminent domain power was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Industrial Development Agencies - Constitutionality of Statutory Condemnation Scheme - Taking for Public Purpose - Retail Shopping Center

1. The statutory condemnation scheme granting an industrial development agency (IDA) the power to condemn property (General Municipal Law § 858 [4]), pursuant to which respondent IDA proposes to acquire petitioner oil companies' properties for the construction of a retail shopping center by a private developer as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront, is not facially unconstitutional. There is no violation of substantive due process so long as the governmental agency takes the property for a public purpose and provides the owner with just compensation. Furthermore, respondent's agreement with the developer to provide funding for the condemnation is in accord with statutory procedure (see, EDPL 304) and constitutes a certain and adequate source and manner of just compensation.

Municipal Corporations - Industrial Development Agencies - Validity of Condemnation for Construction of Retail Shopping Center

2. Respondent industrial development agency's (IDA) proposed taking of petitioner oil companies' properties pursuant to General Municipal Law § 858 (4) for the development of a retail shopping center by a private developer as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront is not violative of the general statutory prohibition against IDA funding of retail projects (General Municipal Law § 862, as amended by L 1993, ch 356) since the project is located in a "highly distressed area" in view of the area's poverty and unemployment rate (General Municipal Law § 854 [18]) and is thus specifically exempt from the retail establishment prohibition (General Municipal Law § 862 [2] [b]).

Municipal Corporations - Industrial Development Agencies - Validity of Condemnation for Construction of Retail Shopping Center

3. Respondent industrial development agency's proposed taking of petitioner oil companies' properties pursuant to General Municipal Law § 858 (4) for the development of a retail shopping center by a private developer as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront serves the legitimate public purposes of reducing urban physical and economic blight and promoting economic revitalization of the acquisition site and will thus be sustained in a proceeding pursuant to EDPL 207. The fact that the developer will benefit privately from the project does not invalidate the agency's determination since the public purposes for the project are dominant.

Municipal Corporations - Industrial Development Agencies - Validity of Condemnation for Construction of Retail Shopping Center - Failure to Raise Issue of Excess Taking in Condemnation Proceeding

4. Petitioner, whose property was included in respondent industrial development agency's proposed taking of properties pursuant to General Municipal Law § 858 (4) for the development of a retail shopping center by a private developer as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront, cannot challenge the taking of its property as being excess to the shopping center project in a proceeding to review the proposed condemnation pursuant to EDPL 207, since it failed to raise the issue during the condemnation proceedings. Furthermore, the fact that the project would create competition with other existing retail establishments does not invalidate the proposed condemnation as being contrary to the legislative intent to prohibit the use of industrial development funds to "cannibalize" one area of the State at the expense of another since the project is located in a "highly distressed area" (General Municipal Law § 854 [18]) and is thus expressly countenanced by General Municipal Law § 862 (2) (b).

Municipal Corporations - Industrial Development Agencies - Validity of Condemnation for Construction of Retail Shopping Center - "Contracting Away" of Agency's Eminent Domain Power

5. Respondent industrial development agency did not illegally "contract away" its eminent domain power (General Municipal Law § 858 [4]) by entering into an agreement with a private developer under which the developer agreed to provide funding for the construction of a retail shopping center on the site of petitioners' condemned properties as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront. Respondent intended to condemn the properties and merely entered into an agreement with a private developer to ensure that the properties would be developed in the anticipated beneficial manner. While the developer may influence respondent's decision with respect to what properties will be condemned, the agreement clearly establishes that the condemnation decision is respondent's and respondent's alone. The steps articulated in the agreement ensure that respondent will not condemn petitioners' properties without adequate compensatory resources. Furthermore, the language in the agreement suggesting that respondent will condemn the properties upon the developer's final commitment to the redevelopment project does not amount to an impermissible bargaining away of the governmental entity's power of eminent domain.

Environmental Conservation - Environmental Quality Review - Validity of Condemnation by Industrial Development Agency for Construction of Retail Shopping Center - Segmenting of Environmental Review

6. Respondent industrial development agency's determination to condemn petitioner oil companies' properties for the construction of a retail shopping center by a private developer as part of a larger redevelopment plan for the Syracuse lakefront (see, General Municipal Law § 858 [4]) must be annulled pursuant to EDPL 207 (C) (3) due to respondent's failure, as the lead agency for purposes of environmental review, to make its determination and findings in accordance with the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) (ECL art 8). Respondent's site-specific SEQRA review of the shopping center project improperly segmented the environmental review of that project from other "reasonably related" lakefront projects and failed to consider the secondary and cumulative effects of such projects (see, 6 NYCRR 617.2 [gg]; 617.3 [k] [1]). In addition, respondent's SEQRA review failed to account for the acute environmental impacts associated with the relocation and reconstruction of petitioners' oil storage tanks and pipelines (see, 6 NYCRR 617.14 [c]). Furthermore, respondent failed to consider all the environmental ramifications of the shopping center project and failed to analyze reasonable alternatives to the project (ECL 8-0109 [2] [d]; 6 NYCRR 617.14 [f] [5]), particularly petitioners' proposal to consolidate their facilities on a portion of the site. However, respondent's failure to address the economic impacts of the project on the over-all Syracuse economy did not violate SEQRA since those impacts involved purely competitive economic facts, not environmental ones, nor did respondent err in relying only on data it received from its own experts concerning the impact of traffic and air quality since all that is required is that respondent have considered the data it received and given a reasoned response.

 

336 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Gernatt Asphalt Prods. v. Town of Sardinia


2039


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


208 A.D.2d 139; 622 N.Y.S.2d 395; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1929



February 3, 1995, Decided  
February 3, 1995, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 3, 1995.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mining company appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which converted its N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding seeking to annul amendments to respondent town's zoning ordinance to an action for declaratory judgment and declared that the amendments had been validly and constitutionally enacted.


OVERVIEW: A mining company challenged a judgment declaring amendments to the town's zoning laws constitutionally enacted. The amendments would have repealed Town of Sardinia, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance §§ 6.02(A)(5), 7.07 and would have designated as a permissible use mining permitted by a valid permit issued by the Department of Environmental Conservation pursuant to the Mined Land Reclamation Law and a special use permit issued by the town. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and held that the town engaged in impermissible exclusionary zoning. The court found that the town after its executive session at the public hearing on the proposed special use permit amendments, dramatically shifted its focus and, without further public debate or explanation, adopted the Prohibition of Mining Amendments, and thereby completely curtailed a potential source of livelihood for all mine owners. The court also found that there was no evidence that the town provided a comprehensive community plan or determined that the total ban on mining would serve regional needs and requirements.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Notice of Proposed Amendments In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted, since interested parties, such as petitioner mine operator, had neither proper notice of the amendments nor an opportunity to debate them at a public hearing. Whereas the noticed amendments would have generally banned mining in the Town but for permissible uses in certain districts subject to special use permit requirements, the adopted amendments succinctly and completely banned mining throughout the Town. Thus, it was manifestly unfair for the Town to ban all new mining when it had appeared that nonconforming uses could be maintained by special use permits.

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Notice of Proposed Amendments In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted, since the failure to refer a proposed amendment or new ordinance to the pertinent county or regional planning department is a jurisdictional defect, and there is no evidence that the county planning board was ever provided with a copy of the adopted amendments. Because the adopted amendments are substantially different from the proposed amendments, the county board should have had the opportunity to report and make recommendations on the new amendments, and because respondent Town did not afford that opportunity to the planning board, which replied to the submission of the Town's proposed amendments by expressly making "no recommendation", the adopted amendments should be nullified.

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Notice of Proposed Amendments In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted, since there is no evidence that the Town Planning Board, which is required by the local Zoning Ordinance to review and report on proposed amendments, ever reviewed, much less reported on, the adopted amendments completely banning mining in the Town. Given the quick turnabout by the Town Board after its executive session at the public hearing on the proposed amendments, the evidence is that the planning board neither reviewed nor reported on the amendments which were ultimately adopted.

Municipal Corporations - Open Meetings Law - Improper Use of Executive Session In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted. Although the Town Board and certain Town officials went into executive session at the close of the public hearing on the proposed amendments, ostensibly to discuss their likely effect on existing litigation, the Board emerged some 30 minutes later and formally resolved, without any further public involvement, to accept a previously prepared environmental assessment form and a negative SEQRA declaration, as well as to adopt the prohibition of mining amendments, effectively permitting the public to know only the result, viz., that the prohibition of mining amendments would be adopted without further SEQRA review. By depriving the public of the opportunity to debate that adoption, both the letter and the spirit of the Open Meetings Law were violated: the decisions apparently reached at the executive session affected the public and directly related to the possibility of a municipal matter becoming an official enactment.

Parties - Standing - SEQRA Challenge A corporation engaged in mining in respondent Town has standing to seek judicial review of the Town's SEQRA determination that a proposed zoning amendment, which would prohibit mining in respondent Town and relegate currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, would not have a significant impact on the environment. Inasmuch as the adopted amendment bans mining throughout the Town, an activity petitioner has carried on for years, petitioner has demonstrated that the administrative action will in fact have a harmful effect on it; by virtue of its status as the owner of property affected by the amendment, petitioner has demonstrated that its interest is within the zone of interest to be protected by the statute, viz., the environment.

Environmental Conservation - Environmental Quality Review - Failure to Take Hard Look In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have granted petitioner mine owner's SEQRA challenge and annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted. The Town Board failed to identify the relevant areas of environmental concern, take a hard look at them, and make a reasoned elaboration of the basis for its determination.

Mines and Minerals - Mined Land Reclamation Law - Preemption of Local Zoning Ordinance A local Zoning Ordinance which prohibited mining everywhere in respondent Town and relegated currently operating mines to nonconforming uses of land violates the supersession provision of the Mined Land Reclamation Law (see, ECL 23-2703 [2]).

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Comprehensive Plan In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted. The record, which reveals that mining has been a long-standing use in the Town and that the Town's geologist found reason to be wary of mining beneath the Town's water table, does not support the leap of logic taken by the Town from allowing mining as a special permitted use in certain areas, the object of the noticed amendments, to completely disallowing mining throughout the Town. Respondent Town did not clearly articulate its rationale for the total ban on mining; rather, it took action that was tantamount to not considering problems presented and uniform provisions to deal with them, which tend to promote the general community welfare. Accordingly, petitioner mine owner has satisfied its burden of overcoming the strong presumption of validity attaching to the adopted ordinance.

Municipal Corporations - Zoning - Exclusionary Zoning In a proceeding challenging the adoption of zoning amendments prohibiting mining in respondent Town and relegating currently operating mines to nonconforming uses, Supreme Court should have annulled the adopted amendments where the proposed amendments, which were the subject of public notice, were different from the amendments which were ultimately adopted. It appears that the Town engaged in impermissible exclusionary zoning where the Town Board, after an executive session following a public hearing on the noticed amendments, dramatically shifted its focus and, without further public debate or explanation, adopted the ordinance in question, thereby completely curtailing a potential source of livelihood for all mine owners in the Town. There is no evidence that the Town Board provided a comprehensive community plan or determined that the total ban on mining would serve regional needs and requirements.

People v. White


68611


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


211 A.D.2d 982; 621 N.Y.S.2d 728; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 568



January 26, 1995, Decided  
January 26, 1995, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the County Court of Madison County (New York), which convicted defendant of rape in the third degree. Defendant claimed error in admitting DNA test results, denying defendant's request for certain material, allowing fetal remains into evidence, denying defendant's speedy trial motion, denying defendant's request to rule N.Y. Penal Law § 130.25 unconstitutional, and imposing an excessive sentence.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of rape. On appeal, the court reviewed the record, which indicated that there was adequate expert evidence presented that proper laboratory procedures were followed. The court further held that: (1) the period during which the DNA testing was conducted was excludable from the six-month speedy trial period as an adjournment requested by defendant; (2) the State was not required to provide the forensic logbook at a preliminary hearing; (3) the fetal material introduced at trial to establish the chain of custody relating to the admissibility of the DNA evidence was relevant, and any material not used for the DNA test was merely cumulative and was not designed to inflame the passions of the jury; (4) the distinction as to age under N.Y. Penal Law § 130.25 was rational and served a legitimate state interest in the imposition of sanctions based on differences in the maturity of adults and younger persons; (5) the record did not support defendant's contention that the court was biased against or vindictive toward him for refusing a plea offer; and (6) there was no reason to disturb the sentence imposed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

 

339 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Servs. ex rel. Lisa U. v. Steven V.


71579


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


207 A.D.2d 162; 623 N.Y.S.2d 14; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 429



January 19, 1995, Decided  
January 19, 1995, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review of an order by the Family Court, Franklin County (New York), which dismissed her request that respondent father be required to pay the medical expenses incurred by the birth of his daughter. At the time, the parents were not married but living together, and they were eligible for and receiving Medicaid. Medicaid paid the expenses of childbirth.


OVERVIEW: An unwed father was not a "responsible relative" under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 366(3)(a). However, Fam. Ct. Act §§ 514, 545(1)(b) provide that an unwed father may be held accountable for the birth expenses of the mother of his child, which was sufficient to render him a liable "third party" under Medicaid. In contrast, a married father who was not able to contribute at the time the assistance was furnished was not liable under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 101(1). To impose liability on a father who was no more financially able than the mother violated principles of gender neutrality. The court held that: (1) because the father was a qualified Medicaid recipient at the time of the birth, he was not liable for amounts advanced on his daughter's behalf; (2) it was a denial of equal protection of the laws to permit the state to obtain reimbursement from an unwed father when a similarly situated married father was not liable; and (3) the trial court did not abuse its discretion by refusing to order reimbursement by a father who was a Medicaid recipient when his child was born.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.


HEADNOTES

Social Services - Medical Assistance - Liability of Unwed Father for Birth-Related Medical Expenses The State is not entitled to obtain reimbursement from respondent unwed father for either his child's or the mother's birth expenses paid under the Medicaid program where respondent was himself a qualified Medicaid recipient at the time of the birth. Since respondent's liability, as a responsible relative of his child, for birth expenses attributable to the child's medical needs arises from the creation of an implied contract at the time the assistance was furnished (Social Services Law § 366 [3] [a]), reimbursement may only be obtained if respondent was actually able to pay the child's expenses at the time they were incurred. In addition, although an unwed father's liability for the mother's birth-related medical expenses does not similarly arise from the creation of an implied contract at the time the assistance is furnished (see, Family Ct Act § 514, 545 [1] [b]; 42 USC § 1396a [a] [25] [B]), imposing such liability upon an unwed father who was a Medicaid recipient when his child was born would be unconstitutionally discriminatory. Since the mother's receipt of Medicaid benefits to cover the birth constitutes a per se inability to contribute to those expenses for reimbursement purposes, imposing liability upon a father who is no more financially able than the mother would violate principles of gender neutrality. Furthermore, the imposition of liability upon respondent, who was living with the mother before and after the birth and was a contributing member of her household for Medicaid eligibility purposes during that time period, would result in an unacceptable differentiation among fathers, with respect to their liability for birth expenses, on the basis of marital status alone. There is no rational basis for permitting the State to obtain reimbursement from an unwed father when a married father who is in all pertinent respects similarly situated would not be liable due to an inability to contribute to the birth expenses at the time the assistance was furnished.

 

340 of 992 DOCUMENTS



PECORA v CERILLO


93-02285


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


207 A.D.2d 215; 621 N.Y.S.2d 363; 1995 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 134


October 14, 1994, Submitted  
January 9, 1995, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant father appealed an order of the Supreme Court of Westchester County, New York, which denied his motion for summary judgment dismissing plaintiff mother's complaint against him in which she alleged that he breached the parties' modified separation agreement by petitioning the family court for an order compelling the mother's payment of child support.


OVERVIEW: Prior to their divorce, the father and mother entered into a separation agreement which provided that the mother would have custody of the parties' minor children. The parties thereafter modified the agreement to reflect that the father would have custody and that the mother was not obligated to contribute to their support. However, the father ultimately petitioned the family court for an order compelling the mother to pay child support, which she did for two months prior to retaking custody of the children. The mother then brought the instant action against the father alleging that the commencement of the family court proceeding was a breach of the modified separation agreement. The father sought summary judgment on the ground that such a cause of action was impermissible as against public policy, but the trial court denied the motion, finding that there were questions of fact precluding such drastic relief. On appeal, the court reversed and granted the father's motion, holding that such an action violated New York's public policy and that the preclusion of such action was not unconstitutional under the impairment of contracts doctrine of U.S. Const., art I, § 10.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying the father's motion for summary judgment in the mother's action alleging breach of the parties' separation agreement, and granted the father's motion and dismissed the complaint.



HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Support - Action for Damages for Violation of Separation Agreement Where plaintiff mother and defendant father executed a modified separation agreement in which defendant, as part of a joint custody arrangement, obtained exclusive physical custody of the parties' two minor children and agreed to provide for the total support of the children out of his "own funds without contribution from the mother", plaintiff's action to recover damages based upon defendant's alleged breach of the agreement for having subsequently commenced a Family Court proceeding seeking contribution toward child support from plaintiff must be dismissed as violative of New York State's public policy. While the Child Support Standards Act (CSSA) requires that due consideration be given to the terms of a relevant separation agreement (Family Ct Act § 413 [1] [l]), a court is not bound by the terms of a separation agreement in arriving at the relevant support level. Moreover, an inadequate child support provision contained in a separation agreement is voidable and cannot bind the court from remedying the inadequacy. Nor can it bind a parent from seeking to remedy the inadequacy. Furthermore, it is not unconstitutional under the impairment of contracts doctrine (US Const, art I, § 10) to preclude a breach of contract action in this case. A statute that is intended to prevent an economic wrong against the children is not unconstitutional as impairing contract rights.

 

341 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Hull v. Town of Warrensburg


70680


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


207 A.D.2d 37; 620 N.Y.S.2d 570; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13252



December 29, 1994, Decided  
December 29, 1994, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended February 27, 1995.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is modified, on the law, without costs, by declaring that the Sewer Use Ordinance of respondent Town of Warrensburg is valid and has not been shown to be violative of the Municipal Home Rule Law, the NY Constitution or the US Constitution, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court, Warren County (New York), which dismissed his petition brought in a combined proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 and an action for a declaratory judgment, seeking a review of respondent board's assessment of sewer rent charges against the owner's property and a declaration that the board's Sewer Use Ordinance was null and void pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3001.


OVERVIEW: The board assessed sewer rents on the investor's property pursuant to the Sewer Use Ordinance (ordinance). The lower court denied the investor's petition for relief. On appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal of the investor's petition, holding that there was a rational relationship between the sewer rents imposed and the amount of use, and that only a rational basis rather than a mathematical certainty was required to establish constitutionality. Therefore, the court held that underassessments and overassessments did not undermine the constitutionality of the ordinance so long as they were rationally based. Further, the proper standard of review of the rent assessments on the investor's property was whether they were unlawful, arbitrary, and capricious or an abuse of discretion. Because the investor had not challenged the accuracy of the assessments based upon the ordinance, and because the ordinance was constitutional, the assessments were rational and valid. Finally, the court modified the judgment to include a declaration of the ordinance's validity and that it was not violative of the Municipal Home Rule Law or state or federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court held that the board's ordinance was valid and was not violative of the Municipal Home Rule Law, the N.Y. Constitution or the U.S. Constitution. The court affirmed the denial of the investor's petition.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory Judgments - When Remedy Appropriate - Constitutionality of Sewer Use Ordinance In a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action in which petitioner challenges both his sewer assessment for his mixed commercial and residential property and the constitutionality of respondent Town Board's Sewer Use Ordinance (Ordinance), respondent's failure to comply with CPLR 7804 (e) by not filing a certified transcript of the record of proceedings leading to the adoption of the Ordinance did not preclude Supreme Court from dismissing the petition. Where, as here, an administrative action is alleged to be unconstitutional, a declaratory judgment action is the appropriate vehicle for challenge. Since the constitutionality of the Ordinance is appropriately raised in such context, compliance with CPLR 7804 (e) is not required.

Municipal Corporations - Water and Sewer Rates - Constitutionality of Sewer Use Ordinance Respondent Town Board's Sewer Use Ordinance (Ordinance), which imposes sewer rents for all property within the sewer district based upon "user units", defined as the flow from a typical residence of 2.3 persons, is a valid and constitutional exercise of the Board's power. The sewer rents, which are based upon a consideration of the statutory factors listed in General Municipal Law § 451 (1), bear a rational relationship to the services and benefits rendered to property owners. Petitioner's contention that the Ordinance is irrational due to the underassessment of other properties based on the amount of water consumed lacks merit since the law only requires a rational basis for these classifications, rather than a mathematical exactitude. Lastly, since petitioner does not argue that the Board's assessment of 9.5 "user units" for his mixed commercial and residential property is inaccurate based upon the Ordinance and since such Ordinance survives constitutional challenge, any assessment based thereon must be found to be rational (CPLR 7803 [3]).

 

342 of 992 DOCUMENTS


National Ass'n of Indep. Insurers v. State


92-06731


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


207 A.D.2d 191; 620 N.Y.S.2d 448; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13204


March 7, 1994, Argued  
December 27, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State appealed an order from the Supreme Court in Kings County (New York), which, in an action for a judgment declaring N.Y. Tax Law § 341 unconstitutional, granted plaintiff insurers' motion for a preliminary injunction enjoining enforcement or implementation of the statute and denied the State's cross motion for summary judgment declaring the statute constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The State enacted N.Y. Tax Law § 341, which changed the manner in which motor vehicle damage insurance awards were paid. Insurers challenged the constitutionality of the statute, contending that it resulted in double taxation of insurance claimants. The trial court granted the insurers' motion for a preliminary injunction staying implementation of the statute. The trial court also denied the State's motion for summary judgment seeking a declaration that the statute was constitutional, and the State appealed the orders. The court concluded that the insurers failed to overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality that attached to the statute. Accordingly, the court granted the State's cross motion for summary judgment and declared the statute constitutional. Although the court questioned the wisdom of the statute and noted the potential for misuse of the refund provisions, the court held that it was not free to invalidate a statute because it disagreed with its wisdom. Instead, the court's review was limited to determining if any state of facts known or assumed justified the disputed measure. The court held that the statutory scheme was justified.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court granting the insurers' motion for a preliminary injunction was reversed and the insurers' motion for a preliminary injunction was denied. The State's cross motion for summary judgment was granted and the tax statute was declared constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Prepayment of Sales Tax In an action by plaintiff insurance carriers challenging the constitutionality of Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, the State waived the argument that plaintiffs lack standing to raise contentions on behalf of claimants who are not parties to the action by failing to raise it in its answer or in a preanswer motion to dismiss. In any event, plaintiffs, as collectors of the tax and as the parties allegedly faced with overpayments, have a sufficient cognizable stake in the outcome of the action to contest the constitutionality of the statute.

Taxation - Sales and Use Taxes - Prepayment of Sales Tax on Motor Vehicle Damage Awards Supreme Court erred in granting plaintiff insurance carriers a preliminary injunction staying implementation of Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award based upon the higher of the sales tax in the locale where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is to be repaired and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, based on a determination that the statute's provisions violate due process by potentially subjecting claimants to the double payment of sales tax on the same transaction or requiring carriers, on occasion, to overpay the sales tax without the possibility of a refund. Although the statute imposes a tax on the "transfer" of the damage award to or on behalf of claimant, the statute actually provides for the prepayment of the sales tax and does not impose a new transfer tax separate from the sales tax paid when the vehicle is repaired or replaced. Consequently, the statute, on its face and in its intended operation, does not result in double taxation of insurance claimants. Speculation that claimants will be unable to find vendors to accept the vouchers does not demonstrate the statute's unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt. Since the choice of the vendor is in the control of the claimants and their carriers, any disadvantage caused by a vendor's failure to accept the voucher would be self-imposed and therefore should not render the statute constitutionally invalid. Similarly, it is not arbitrary or unreasonable to base the tax rate on the taxing jurisdiction where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is repaired, depending on which is higher. Furthermore, even if a claimant was forced to pay the sales tax twice because no vendor could be found to accept the voucher, the statute should not be interpreted as precluding, on its face, application of the tax refund provision in Tax Law § 1139 (a).

Taxation - Sales and Use Taxes - Prepayment of Sales Tax on Motor Vehicle Damage Awards - Due Process Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award based upon the higher of the sales tax in the locale where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is to be repaired and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, does not violate due process by depriving plaintiff insurance carriers of property without just compensation. The fact that the over-all cost to plaintiffs of complying with the statute's voucher and record-keeping requirements might outweigh the taxes collected does not represent an unconstitutional taking of private property for a public benefit since there is no due process violation where, as here, a taxing statute is enacted to raise revenue and not for any other purpose. Moreover, plaintiffs' due process argument is not persuasive in view of the fact that other businesses, no matter how small, are required to collect sales taxes and to maintain relevant records.

Taxation - Sales and Use Taxes - Prepayment of Sales Tax on Motor Vehicle Damage Awards - Impairment of Contractual Obligations Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award based upon the higher of the sales tax in the locale where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is to be repaired and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, does not unconstitutionally impair plaintiffs' contractual obligations to reimburse claimants for any sales tax paid in violation of the Contract Clause (US Const, art I, § 10) in the event claimants are unable to find motor vehicle vendors willing to accept the vouchers. In general, legislation will not be stricken as unconstitutional, even though it may interfere with existing contracts, if the legislation is addressed to a legitimate end and the measures taken are reasonable and appropriate to that end. The means here used of requiring that the sales tax be remitted by the carrier at the time the damage award is transferred, rather than when the repair is made, is rationally related to the legitimate State purpose of ensuring that the sales tax is efficiently collected on the repair and replacement of motor vehicles. In any event, plaintiffs' claim of harm in this regard is both premature and speculative.

Administrative Law - Rule Making - Explanatory Statements and Technical Instructions Exempt from Rule-Making Procedures Credit voucher specifications issued by the State Tax Department in implementing Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award based upon the higher of the sales tax in the locale where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is to be repaired and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, are merely explanatory statements and technical instructions for meeting statutory requirements and, standing alone, are without legal effect and are thus exempt from rule-making procedures and filing requirements (State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2] [b] [iv]). The statements in the specifications regarding penalties are merely explanatory as the statute provides for the imposition of civil penalties for the failure to furnish a properly completed voucher and stub. Furthermore, rather than prescribing fixed, general principles to be applied by an administrative agency without regard to other facts and circumstances, the specifications indicate that the agency will be flexible in the manner in which the voucher and stub are produced.

Taxation - Sales and Use Taxes - Prepayment of Sales Tax on Motor Vehicle Damage Awards Tax Law § 341, which requires an insurance carrier that issues an award for damage to or theft of a motor vehicle to calculate the "tax component" of the award based upon the higher of the sales tax in the locale where the claimant resides or where the vehicle is to be repaired and to remit that amount directly to the State, with the insurance claimant being issued a credit voucher from the carrier which may then be used in lieu of paying sales tax if the vehicle is repaired or replaced within a year, is constitutional. Plaintiff insurance carriers have failed to overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality that attaches to the taxing statute. Although Supreme Court, in granting plaintiffs a preliminary injunction staying enforcement or implementation of the statute, questioned the wisdom of the statute and noted the potential for misuse of the refund provisions, courts are not free to invalidate a statute because they disagree with its wisdom. The Legislature's concern regarding sales tax evasion in the repair of motor vehicles justifies the statutory scheme.

344 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Girsky v. Touro College


94-05887


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


210 A.D.2d 406; 621 N.Y.S.2d 85; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12845


December 5, 1994, Argued  
December 19, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant law school sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which found in favor of respondent law student in an action brought by the law student challenging his dismissal from the law school for academic deficiency.


OVERVIEW: The law student was dismissed by the law school because his cumulative grade point average fell below 2.000 after he completed four semesters of full time study. Thereafter the student requested to be placed on probation, and after a hearing before the academic policy committee, the request was denied. In the instant action, the law student sought to annul the law school's determination dismissing him from the school. The trial court directed the law school to conduct a further hearing because no record was made at the prior hearing. On appeal, the court found that the decision of the trial court was error. The court held that judicial review of the determinations of educational institutions as to the academic performance of their students was limited to the question of whether the challenged determination was arbitrary and capricious, irrational, made in bad faith, unconstitutional, or contrary to statute. The court found that in the instant case the law student failed to demonstrate that the challenged determination was arbitrary, capricious, or in disregard of the law school's own rules and regulations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the law student in his action challenging his dismissal by the law school.

Thomas S. v. Robin Y.


51466


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


209 A.D.2d 298; 618 N.Y.S.2d 356; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11385



November 17, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father was a sperm donor for respondent mother. The father filed a motion to show cause for an order of filiation and for visitation. The Family Court (New York) found by clear and convincing evidence, based upon blood tests, that the father was the child's biological father. The family court refused to enter an order of filiation and dismissed the proceeding. The father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The father was a sperm donor to the mother and her domestic partner. The child knew of him as her father and, despite residing in California, he had considerable contact with the child at the insistance of her mother. On appeal, the court held that the father was entitled to an order of filiation, as mandated by § 542 of the Family Court Act (Act). Whatever concerns and misgivings the family court and the mother had about visitation, custody, and the child's best interests, those issues were appropriately reserved for a later stage of the proceedings. Without the order of filiation to which the law entitled the father, he lacked standing to seek visitation, pursuant to § 549 of the Act, or to challenge the mother's concept of what might or might not have been in the child's best interests, pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b. Also, the alleged oral understanding between the parties that the father would not assume a parental role towards the child was unenforceable for failure to comply with explicit statutory requirements for surrender of parental rights, pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384 and § 516 of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the father's motion to show case and remanded the case for entry of an order of filiation and remanded the case for a hearing on the issue of visitation.
