People v. Mc Queen


1348


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


209 A.D.2d 995; 620 N.Y.S.2d 195; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12016



November 16, 1994, Filed

 

347 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Campaign for Fiscal Equity v. State


52842, 52843


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


205 A.D.2d 272; 619 N.Y.S.2d 699; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11329



November 15, 1994, Decided  
November 15, 1994, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended November 29, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, organization and city, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County that dismissed their claims that defendant state's allocation of funds perpetuated a disparity in the educational system for failure to state a cause of action under The Education Article, N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1, or the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The claims by plaintiffs, organization and city, that defendant state's allocation of funds perpetuated a disparity in the educational system were dismissed except to the extent that civil rights claims were asserted under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 and Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000. The court affirmed the dismissal, finding that plaintiffs did not state a cause of action under the Education Article, N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1, or the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because the allegations that reduced resources resulted in their failure to meet minimum education standards were rejected in prior cases, and plaintiffs did not demonstrate that circumstances had changed to warrant new scrutiny. The court reversed with respect to the causes upheld by the lower court. It held that reliance on N.Y. Exec. Law § 291(2) was unavailing because the funding allocation scheme itself did not discriminate. Although the court recognized that 34 C.F.R. § 100.3 allowed for a cause of action for disparate impact, it found that such impact did not result from the designation of a lump sum to city schools but rather from the suballocation of the funds.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing claims by plaintiffs, organization and city, that defendant state's allocation of funds perpetuated educational disparity was affirmed because plaintiffs failed to state a cause of action where they did not show changed circumstances to warrant review of prior decisions. The order upholding equal protection claims was reversed because disparate impact was not caused by the mere designation of funds to city schools.


HEADNOTES

Schools - State Aid to School Districts - Constitutionality of Public School Financing System Plaintiffs' challenge to the State's system of allocating funds to local school districts as being violative of the Education Article of the State Constitution (art XI, § 1), based on conclusory allegations that the allocation scheme perpetuates a disparity in the State's educational system, resulting in the failure of certain New York City community school board districts to meet "minimum standards of educational quality and quantity", must be dismissed as a matter of law for failure to state a cause of action since the theory expounded by plaintiffs herein is virtually identical to that advanced, fully tried and ultimately rejected on appeal in Board of Educ. v Nyquist (57 NY2d 27, appeals dismissed 459 US 1139), which held that the Education Article only requires the State to provide "a sound basic education" and that the State's school financing system, although "resulting in significant unevenness in the educational opportunities offered", did not illegally discriminate against large city school districts. Plaintiffs' equal protection claims must likewise be dismissed for failure to state a cause of action since plaintiffs have failed to demonstrate adequately that circumstances have changed or deteriorated in the 12 years since the prior Court of Appeals decision to warrant a new scrutiny of this claim.

Schools - State Aid to School Districts - Constitutionality of Public School Financing System - Civil Rights Violation Plaintiffs' challenge to the State's school financing system as constituting a civil rights violation under article I, § 11 of the State Constitution by reason of discrimination against certain minority students in the affected New York City school district should have been dismissed for failure to state a cause of action. The IAS Court erroneously upheld this cause of action only by reference to Executive Law § 291 (2), which guarantees equal opportunity to education as a civil right. However, that statute merely assures every student an "opportunity to obtain education". There is nothing in the funding allocation scheme which discriminates against any of the individual plaintiffs and thus deprives them of such educational opportunity.

Schools - State Aid to School Districts - Validity of Public School Financing System - Federal Civil Rights Violation Plaintiffs' challenge to the State's school financing system as being violative of title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 (42 USC § 2000d) and the implementing regulations promulgated thereunder (34 CFR 100.3 [b] [2]) should have been dismissed for failure to state a cause of action. The IAS Court erred in finding liability under the applicable regulation, which prohibits a recipient of Federal funds for education programs from utilizing "methods of administration" which result in unjustifiable disparate impact upon minorities, regardless of lack of intention to discriminate. However, the State's role in allocating education funds simply designates a lump sum to the New York City school system as a unitary school district. It is not that function which results in disparate impact on minority racial or ethnic groups; rather, it is the method by which plaintiff Chancellor of the City School District divides and suballocates those funds that may arguably result in the disparate impact complained of here.

Parties - Standing - Standing of Municipal Plaintiffs to Challenge State's Public School Financing System The challenge by the municipal plaintiffs to the State's school financing system on constitutional grounds was properly dismissed by the IAS Court for lack of legal capacity to sue. Units of municipal government, as political subdivisions created by the State, generally lack the capacity to challenge in a lawsuit the constitutionality of State legislative enactments affecting them. In addition, dismissal also rests on the ground of failure to state a cause of action, even in the absence of a cross appeal by defendants in the case brought by the municipal plaintiffs. Consolidation of both cases challenging the funding allocation scheme for decision in the IAS Court and on appeal, and the fact that defendants have noticed a cross appeal in one of the cases, permit the Appellate Division to reach this expanded disposition.

 

348 of 992 DOCUMENTS


In re Francis S.


49420


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


206 A.D.2d 4; 618 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11296



November 15, 1994, Decided  
November 15, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order of the Supreme Court (New York), which, in a recommitment proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(14), denied the state's application seeking the recommitment of respondent, an acquittee by reason of mental disease or defect.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court denied the state's petition to have respondent, an acquittee by reason of mental disease or defect, recommitted pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(14), and the state sought review. The court held that the supreme court erred in finding that respondent was not suffering from a dangerous mental disorder at the time of the hearing merely because respondent did not exhibit dangerous behavior under the structured environment of a psychiatric hospital as respondent had repeatedly violated the order of conditions upon which he gained release and was still mentally ill and a polysubstance abuser given to acts of violence. The court found that even if respondent should have been treated like a civil committee, the evidence at the hearing met the more stringent clear and convincing evidence test. The court held that the failure to serve respondent the recommitment application within the five-year period was not a jurisdictional defect. The court found that the affidavit submitted in support of the recommitment application was sufficient. The denial of the application for recommitment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying the state's application to have respondent, an acquittee by reason of mental disease or defect, recommitted, and granted the application to have respondent confined to a secure psychiatric institution, holding that there was ample evidence that respondent suffered from a dangerous mental disorder.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Insanity - Recommitment Proceeding - Sufficiency of Evidence of Dangerous Mental Disorder Supreme Court, having found after a lengthy hearing on recommitment pursuant to CPL 330.20 (14) that respondent, an insanity acquittee with a long history of mental illness and drug abuse who has repeatedly violated the order of conditions upon which he gained release, is still mentally ill and a polysubstance abuser given to acts of violence, and having further found that it was "highly likely" that respondent, once released, would revert to his prior pattern of uncontrollable violent behavior and would not comply with any treatment plan but would relapse into drug and alcohol abuse, erred in finding that respondent was not, at the time of the hearing, suffering from a dangerous mental disorder within the meaning of CPL 330.20 (1) (c) merely because respondent did not exhibit dangerous behavior under the structured environment of a psychiatric hospital. Compliance or lack of dangerousness in a facility does not necessarily mean that an individual does not suffer from a dangerous mental disorder. Based upon the evidence presented at the hearing that respondent will inevitably resort to assaultive or other dangerous behavior upon his release from a controlled environment, appellants have established by a fair preponderance of the evidence that respondent suffered from a dangerous mental disorder at the time of the hearing, and, accordingly, the petition for recommitment should have been granted. Furthermore, the fact that respondent was originally found to be mentally ill but not suffering from a dangerous mental disorder, following his plea of not responsible by reason of mental disease or defect, does not preclude his recommitment pursuant to CPL 330.20 (14). In any event, even if respondent should be treated like a civil committee, the evidence at the hearing met the more stringent clear and convincing evidence test.

Crimes - Insanity - Recommitment Proceeding - Service of Notice of Recommitment Application Not Required within Period of Order of Conditions In a proceeding to recommit respondent, an insanity acquittee, pursuant to CPL 330.20 (14), the Commissioner's failure to serve respondent with written notice of the recommitment application within the five-year period of the order of conditions upon which respondent gained release does not constitute a jurisdictional defect requiring dismissal of the application where the application for recommitment was timely filed within the five-year period. While the statute requires service of the application for recommitment on the defendant, it does not require that service on the defendant be made simultaneously and within the period of the underlying order of conditions.

Crimes - Insanity - Recommitment Proceeding - Sufficiency of Psychiatric Examiner's Affidavit in Support of Recommitment Application In a 1992 proceeding to recommit respondent, an insanity acquittee, pursuant to CPL 330.20 (14), the statutorily mandated psychiatric examiner's affidavit submitted in support of the recommitment application, indicating that, based upon the psychiatrist's clinical evaluation of respondent in 1990, a review of respondent's in-patient psychiatric care, respondent's history of noncompliance with his order of conditions and his two recent arrests, respondent constituted a danger to himself or others and required care and treatment in a secure psychiatric facility, is sufficient to satisfy the statute's requirements that the affidavit set forth the defendant's clinical diagnosis, a detailed analysis of the defendant's mental condition and the opinion of the psychiatric examiner, particularly since the Commissioner had lost contact with respondent upon respondent's release from a psychiatric facility in 1990 and was, thereby, unable to submit a more updated clinical analysis. In any event, the alleged insufficiency of the allegations in the affidavit are not jurisdictional defects, but rather would be pertinent only to a motion to dismiss the proceeding or a habeas corpus petition prior to hearings. The technical sufficiency of the affidavit, upon which the recommitment proceeding was commenced, should not be considered after extensive hearings established that respondent did suffer from a dangerous mental disorder.

 

349 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Salvador v. State


70862


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


205 A.D.2d 194; 618 N.Y.S.2d 142; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10873



November 3, 1994, Decided  
November 3, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owners appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York), which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and action for a declaratory judgment, granted respondents, the state, town, and Lake George Park Commission's, motions to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action the property owners' petition challenging laws and regulations implementing the commission's regulatory authority.


OVERVIEW: After their attempt to exclude a portion of their docks from fees on which they had paid property taxes to the town was denied by the commission, the property owners challenged various laws and regulations implementing the commission's regulatory authority over vessels, wharves, moorings and fee schedules, and wastewater and stormwater authority, 1979 N.Y. Laws 599; 1987 N.Y. Laws 617; N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 6, pts. 645, 646, 646-3, 646-4, conferred by N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 43-0110, 43-0112, and 43-0125. The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the property owners' complaint for failure to state a cause of action and failure to present support for the allegation that the user fees constituted a taking. The court held that the protection of Lake George's natural and environmental resources were of state concern; thus, enactment of the challenged statutes did not violate the home rule provisions of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2); there was a rational basis for the fees; the fees were not a taking; and the navigational servitude over the property owners' submerged private lands was not a taking because it was not statutory but was always there.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of the motions to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action.


HEADNOTES

Appeal - Matters Abandoned - Raising Issue for First Time on Appeal In a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and action for declaratory judgment challenging the validity of certain laws (L 1979, ch 599; L 1987, ch 617, amending ECL 17-1709 [3]) and regulations (6 NYCRR parts 645, 646) promulgated to implement the regulatory authority of respondent Lake George Park Commission over, inter alia, vessels, wharfs, moorings and fee schedules (ECL 43-0125), any claims relating to the propriety of Supreme Court's decision to deny the relief requested by petitioners in an order to show cause are deemed abandoned due to petitioners' failure to have presented any arguments with respect thereto. Furthermore, to the extent that petitioners now contend that the challenged laws should be annulled because they violate or are in conflict with the Public Lands Law, the Navigation Law and the Public Health Law, these arguments were not raised before Supreme Court and should therefore not be considered on appeal by the Appellate Division.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Home Rule Powers of Local Governments - Lake George Park Commission In a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and action for declaratory judgment challenging the validity of certain laws (L 1979, ch 599; L 1987, ch 617, amending ECL 17-1709 [3]) and regulations (6 NYCRR parts 645, 646) promulgated to implement the regulatory authority of respondent Lake George Park Commission over, inter alia, vessels, wharfs, moorings and fee schedules (ECL 43-0125), Supreme Court correctly rejected petitioners' contention that the challenged laws were enacted in violation of their right to self-government under the home rule provisions of the State Constitution (NY Const, art IX, § 2 [b] [2]) and that respondent Town failed to protect those rights on their behalf. The record demonstrates that the protection of the lake's water quality and environs is of sufficient importance to the State generally to sustain the enactment of the statutes. The protection of the lake as an environmental resource of the State is also of State concern rather than a matter of local concern only. Thus, the constitutional home rule provisions have not been violated by the enactment of these statutes.

Constitutional Law - Equal Protection of Laws - Rational Basis Standard - User Fees for Commercial Docks Petitioners, who own and operate a lodge on Lake George with dock and other related facilities, have failed to demonstrate that the schedule of user fees for commercial docks imposed by ECL 43-0125 (2) (a) as enacted by Laws of 1987 (ch 617) violates their right to equal protection because the fees apply only to those engaged in recreational boating and not to other classes of users such as homeowners and visitors to the lake. Since the subject classification is not a burden on fundamental constitutional rights nor does it create a suspect class, the rational basis test is the applicable standard of review and, under such standard, there is a rational basis to charge higher fees on the owners of commercial docks than on owners of residential docks since commercial facilities allow a greater number of boats to use the lake than do residential facilities and they require additional facilities and parking space to accommodate those users, thereby increasing the costs of regulation.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - User Fees for Commercial Docks Petitioners, who own and operate a lodge on Lake George with dock and other related facilities, have failed to demonstrate that the schedule of user fees for commercial docks imposed by ECL 43-0125 (2) (a) as enacted by Laws of 1987 (ch 617) effects an unconstitutional taking of private property without due process. The fees imposed are not a lien or a tax since they are not imposed to generate revenue or to offset the costs of governmental functions generally, but, rather, are user fees imposed to reimburse the costs of government services. Furthermore, any diminution in value caused by the fees does unlawfully not burden petitioners with a "navigational servitude" over their submerged private lands on which their docks are located since the "navigational servitude" was always there and did not result from the challenged statute. The "navigational servitude" does not arise because of the public ownership of lands under water but because of the common-law principle that the navigable waters of the State are held in trust by the State for the benefit of the people of the State without regard as to who owns the banks and beds of the waterway. Thus, although land under a navigable waterway may be privately owned, it is still subject to regulation by the State. Lastly, petitioners, as the owners of a "regulated facility" which leases berths to their guests, do use the "unique resources of the Lake George park". Under these circumstances, the user fees do not authorize a taking without just compensation.

Village of Hempstead v. SRA Realty Corp.


94-03640


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


208 A.D.2d 713; 617 N.Y.S.2d 794; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9968


October 4, 1994, Submitted  
October 17, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by plaintiff village for a permanent injunction to enjoin a violation of Village of Hempstead Code § 50-28(d), the village sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which granted defendant landowners' motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to the landowners.


OVERVIEW: For aesthetic reasons, the village enacted an ordinance that prohibited the installation or maintenance of sheet metal security gates over openings to stores facing the street. Five years before the ordinance was enacted, the landowners had installed sheet metal security gates on the windows and doors of their store because several break-ins had occurred. The village sought an injunction to require the landowners to permanently remove their gates. The trial court granted the landowners' motion to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that the ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to the landowners. The court affirmed the order, holding that the ordinance was invalid as applied to these landowners because the provision required the removal of the security gates without any provision for a cost abatement or other method to offset the cost and because it was unfair to require business owners to absorb the cost or forego any security gates.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the village's action to enjoin the violation of a village ordinance by the landowners.

Colonial Life Ins. Co. of Am. v. Curiale


69527


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


205 A.D.2d 58; 617 N.Y.S.2d 377; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9681



October 13, 1994, Decided  
October 13, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance company and respondent Superintendent of Insurance cross-appealed from the judgment of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York) which dismissed the insurance company's petition challenging the validity of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, part 361, but granted the insurance company's petition and invalidated N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 360.4(c), 360.3(a)(1)(ii).


OVERVIEW: The superintendent promulgated regulations to implement 1992 N.Y. Laws ch. 501. The insurance company petitioned under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to invalidate the regulations. The supreme court dismissed the challenge to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, part 361, but granted the petition and invalidated N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 360.4(c), 360.3(a)(1)(ii). On appeal, the court modified the judgment by converting the proceeding to a declaratory judgment action, declared that the insurance company had not shown part 361 to be invalid, declared that §§ 360.4(c), 360.3(a)(1)(ii) were invalid, and, as modified, affirmed the judgment. The court held that § 360.4(c) impermissibly expanded the definition of "small group" contained in N.Y. Ins. Law § 3231(a), by requiring small group insurers to cover individual proprietors or groups of two, contrary to the clear and unambiguous language in the statute. As to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, § 360.3(a)(1)(ii), the superintendent exceeded his authority by redefining the calculation of participation levels. Chapter 501 did not amend or change the minimum participation requirement set forth in N.Y. Ins. Laws § 4235(c)(1).


OUTCOME: The court converted to a declaratory judgment proceeding the action brought by the insurance company to challenge regulations promulgated by the superintendent, and as so modified, affirmed the supreme court's judgment partially invalidating regulations enacted by the superintendent regarding commercial insurance policies.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer - When Remedy Appropriate - Challenge to Insurance Department Regulations Converted to Declaratory Judgment Action A CPLR article 78 proceeding to annul certain Insurance Department regulations (11 NYCRR part 361) implementing the statutory directive that commercial health insurers doing business in New York employ community rating and offer open enrollment for any insurance market in which they participate (L 1992, ch 501) should have been commenced as a declaratory judgment action and the proceeding will therefore be so converted pursuant to CPLR 103 (c).

Insurance - Health and Accident Insurance - Individual and Small Group Health Insurance - Validity of Mandatory Pool Contributions by Insurers Having Lower Share of High-Risk Insureds An Insurance Department regulation, which establishes a pooling system requiring commercial health insurers having a lower share of high-risk insureds, such as petitioner, to contribute to the pool in amounts necessary to permit sharing or equalization of the risk of high-cost claims (11 NYCRR part 361), is in accord with the mandate of the enabling statute (L 1992, ch 501) that the Superintendent promulgate regulations to assure the orderly implementation of the legislative directive that commercial health insurers doing business in New York employ community rating and offer open enrollment for any insurance market in which they participate (Insurance Law § 3233 [a]). Considering the enabling statute as a whole and in view of its objective to spread the risk among more people and stabilize health insurance premiums, Supreme Court correctly determined that the Legislature intended to create a mandatory pooling system. Furthermore, Supreme Court correctly rejected the contention that the Legislature intended pool contributions to be based on policies written after the statute's effective date and not to existing policies. Since the regulation requires calculations of relative risk to be made based upon all policyholders and not just those of new policyholders, the regulation is in accordance with the legislative directive. In addition, the mandatory pool contributions are a valid exercise of the Legislature's power to regulate and do not constitute an unconstitutional tax. Nor do such contributions constitute a gift of State money to a private organization in violation of NY Constitution, article VII, § 8 (1) or an unconstitutional regulatory taking without just compensation. Petitioner cannot support its contention that it has a constitutionally protected interest in maintaining a healthier than average risk pool.

Insurance - Health and Accident Insurance - Individual and Small Group Health Insurance - Validity of Regulation Requiring Insurers of "Small Groups" to Extend Health Coverage to Individual Proprietors and Groups of Two An Insurance Department regulation which, pursuant to the mandate of the enabling statute (L 1992, ch 501) that commercial health insurers doing business in New York employ community rating and offer open enrollment for any insurance market in which they participate, requires insurers of "small groups" to also extend coverage to individual proprietors and groups of two (11 NYCRR 360.4 [c]), is contrary to the unambiguous statutory directive defining "small group" as groups of 3 to 50 (Insurance Law § 3231 [a]) and is thus invalid for exceeding the scope of the delegated authority. Insurance Law § 3231 (b), which provides that "[i]ndividual proprietors and groups of two must be classified in the individual or small group rating category by the insurer", applies only to the rating of policies and does not provide authority for requiring insurers of "small groups" to extend coverage to individual proprietors and groups of two.

Insurance - Health and Accident Insurance - Individual and Small Group Health Insurance - Validity of Regulation Changing Minimum Participation Levels among Employees in Order to Qualify for Small Group Health Coverage An Insurance Department regulation which, pursuant to the mandate of the enabling statute (L 1992, ch 501) that commercial health insurers doing business in New York employ community rating and offer open enrollment for any insurance market in which they participate, requires the insurer, for purposes of determining minimum participation levels among employees in order to qualify for small group coverage, to "include as participating all eligible employees or members of the group covered under all the alternative health maintenance organization plans made available by the group" (11 NYCRR 360.3 [a] [1] [ii]), is null and void as exceeding the scope of the delegated authority. The enabling statute did not amend or change the minimum participation requirements set forth in Insurance Law § 4235 (c) (1), which provides that the insurer does not have to offer coverage to a group if less than 50% of the employees in the group do not agree to participate in a plan, and the Superintendent therefore exceeded his authority by redefining the calculation of participation levels.

Duffy v. Wetzler


90-07800, 91-02056, 91-02059, 91-02060, 91-02061


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


207 A.D.2d 375; 616 N.Y.S.2d 48; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8246


May 21, 1991, Argued  
August 8, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court vacated this court's decision to modify an order from the lower court that declared former N.Y. Tax Law § 612(c)(3) and New York City, N.Y., Administrative Code § 11-1712(c) (former pension tax laws) unconstitutional and enjoined their enforcement. The Court remitted for consideration of whether its ruling that the invalidation of some state pension laws should be applied retroactively also applied here.


OVERVIEW: In an action for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the constitutionality of the former pension tax laws, cross-appellants federal retirees received relief in that the laws were declared unconstitutional, appellants were enjoined from enforcing them, and they were granted refunds. However, they were not granted class certification, other claims were dismissed, and attorney's fees were denied. Both appellants and cross-appellants challenged those rulings. This court modified the lower court's order by deleting the provisions granting the federal retirees injunctive relief and refunds. The case went to the United States Supreme Court, which vacated this court's order and remanded for consideration of whether its decision which required retroactive application of its ruling invalidating state laws taxing pensions of federal employees, but not state employees, applied here. This court found the rule did apply, so the injunction was proper. However, federal due process did not require a refund because the state provided an adequate form of pre-deprivation process. Moreover, the state agreed to pay refunds to those who filed timely administrative claims.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal and affirmed the lower court's order which declared the former pension tax laws unconstitutional, enjoined appellants from enforcing the pension tax laws, and denied refunds to the federal retirees.

People v. Gray


0792


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


206 A.D.2d 883; 615 N.Y.S.2d 154; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7804



July 15, 1994, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Monroe County Court (New York) convicting him of second-degree murder under the depraved indifference murder statute, N.Y. Penal Law §125.25(2). Defendant fired a handgun with a "hair trigger" at a retreating victim, following an argument.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction. The court found that the objective circumstances surrounding defendant's conduct provided a sufficient basis for a rational trier of fact to conclude that his conduct displayed the requisite wanton indifference to human life necessary to sustain the conviction. The court held that the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. Finally, the court found that there was no merit to the contention that the depraved indifference murder statute was unconstitutional under both the state and federal constitutions. Because the depraved mind murder statute was sufficiently definite to notify an individual of the conduct forbidden and provided specific standards for the trier of fact to apply, it passed constitutional muster. In addition, the court declared that the statute did not violate the constitutional guarantees of substantive due process and equal protection; it was rational for the legislature to penalize more seriously conduct such as defendant's, which, though reckless, was equal in blameworthiness to intentional murder.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for second-degree murder was affirmed.

Bourquin v. Cuomo


70499


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


201 A.D.2d 104; 628 N.Y.S.2d 618; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7357



July 14, 1994, Decided  
July 14, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens and citizens' groups (citizens) sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County, New York, which granted defendant governor's motion for summary judgment dismissing for failure to state a cause of action the citizens' complaint alleging that the issuance of an executive order violated separation of powers, the right to free speech, equal protection, and the prohibition against gifts and loans of state moneys.


OVERVIEW: The citizens protested an executive order that provided for the implementation of a Citizens' Utility Board, a private not-for-profit organization, to represent the interests of residential utility customers in the state. The citizens claimed that the executive order violated separation of powers, violated their right to free speech, deprived them of equal protection, and violated the prohibition against a gift or loan of state moneys. The citizens filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to prevent implementation of the order and the governor and the department of public service filed a cross-motion to dismiss based on lack of standing. The supreme court denied the preliminary injunction, granted the cross motion only to the extent of finding that one citizens' group did not have standing, and dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the order violated the principle of separation of powers. Although the governor had the power to enforce legislation and had flexibility in determining methods of enforcement, executive acts inconsistent with or arrogative of the legislature's prerogatives were in contravention of the separation of powers doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the supreme court dismissing the complaint, granted the citizens' cross motion for summary judgment, and declared that the executive order at issue was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Separation of Powers - Creation of Citizens' Utility Board with Access to State Agency Mailings by Executive Order

An Executive Order which directed the Public Service Commission (PSC) to certify a single State-wide Citizens' Utility Board (CUB), a not-for-profit organization, to represent the interests of residential utility customers, with access to State agency mailings (9 NYCRR 4.141), is unconstitutional as violative of the principle of separation of powers. The failure of the Legislature to pass legislation similar to the Executive Order does not provide indirect proof of a policy decision against the establishment of a CUB as an instrument of public policy. However, the Governor, by issuing the Executive Order, nonetheless impermissibly exceeded existing legislative policy, which charges the PSC, the Consumer Protection Board and the Attorney-General to protect consumers in utility proceedings. Although Public Service Law § 30 establishes a general policy favoring utility customers as to, inter alia, "lengthy delays" of service, it does not contemplate the remedial device of certification of a CUB to have access to State mailings. Furthermore, Executive Law article 20, which authorizes the creation of and sets forth the powers and duties of the Consumer Protection Board, does not provide the requisite authority for the certification of a single CUB.

Rochester Tel. Corp. v. Public Serv. Comm'n


69820


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


201 A.D.2d 31; 614 N.Y.S.2d 454; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7045



June 30, 1994, Decided  
June 30, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a telephone corporation and related parties, sought review of the determinations of respondent, Public Service Commission and related parties, which imputed revenues to it in the form of a royalty.


OVERVIEW: Concerned about the need for an adjustment to the telephone corporation's rates to reflect the uncompensated use of certain intangible utility assets by its unregulated affiliates and to offset certain costs imposed on it by its affiliates that could not be readily or directly quantified, the commission ordered a separate proceeding to consider the propriety and need for such an adjustment. After administrative hearings, the commission issued an order that concluded that it had legal authority to make the adjustment and, imputed to the telephone corporation, a royalty payment of two percent of the total capitalization of its unregulated operations, regardless of when its investment occurred. The court confirmed the determinations, holding that the commission had the authority to establish the adjustment because it was required to ensure that rates charged by the telephone corporation and other utilities were "just and reasonable," pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 91(1).


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determinations of the commission, which imputed revenues to the telephone corporation in the form of a royalty, because the commission had the authority to establish the adjustment because it was required to ensure that the rates charged by utilities were "just and reasonable."


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities - Rate Making - Authority of Public Service Commission to Impute 2% Royalty Payment to Utility with Respect to Utility's Investment in Unregulated Operations

1. Respondent Public Service Commission (PSC) had the authority for rate-making purposes to impute revenues to petitioner telephone company in the form of a royalty in an amount equal to 2% of petitioner's capitalization in unregulated operations as an adjustment to petitioner's rates to reflect the uncompensated use of certain intangible assets by petitioner's unregulated affiliates, such as petitioner's name and reputation, and to offset certain costs imposed on petitioner by its affiliates that could not be readily or directly quantified by conventional rate making. The PSC's broad authority to determine "just and reasonable" utility rates (Public Service Law § 91 [1]) includes not only the right but the duty to scrutinize transactions between a utility and its affiliates, and to make rate adjustments when the utility is being overcharged by affiliated suppliers. Furthermore, the fact that the PSC had previously approved petitioner's diversification and investment in its affiliates does not preclude the PSC from establishing the 2% royalty. In addition, the PSC's action was not preempted by the Public Utility Holding Company Act (15 USC § 79 et seq.) in view of Congress' expressed intent to preserve and continue State regulation of utilities. Lastly, the PSC did not exceed its authority by imputing the 2% royalty to petitioner since the effect of the PSC's imputation of income did not compel petitioner to expand its services outside its territory.

Public Utilities - Rate Making - Validity of Public Service Commission's Imputation of 2% Royalty Payment with Respect to Utility's Investment in Unregulated Operations

2. The determinations of respondent Public Service Commission (PSC) which imputed revenues to petitioner telephone company in the form of a royalty in an amount equal to 2% of petitioner's capitalization in unregulated operations as an adjustment to petitioner's rates to reflect the uncompensated use of certain intangible assets by petitioner's unregulated affiliates, and to offset certain costs imposed on petitioner by its affiliates that could not be readily or directly quantified by conventional rate making, have a rational basis and will therefore be sustained. The PSC rationally concluded that certain intangible assets, such as a utility's name and reputation, have value and that the utility's ratepayers, who have a legitimate concern about the disposition of utility assets, are entitled to some adjustment in their rates to recognize the utility's free transfer of such intangible assets to its unregulated affiliates. Petitioner allowed its affiliates to use its name without compensation and the PSC was therefore justified in acting to protect the ratepayers from imprudent utility conduct. Furthermore, there is evidence in the record to support the PSC's conclusion that despite the existence of cost allocation procedures, petitioner's association with its affiliates results in increased costs that cannot be precisely and accurately quantified by conventional rate making, including employee transfers, letters of credit, affiliate overcharges, cost accounting abuses and accounting inexactitudes. The PSC rationally concluded that petitioner, like any other regulated utility, has abundant incentive to shift costs from its competitive to its monopoly operations and it was therefore justified in acting to protect ratepayers from the increased costs.

Public Utilities - Rate Making - Validity of Public Service Commission's Imputation of 2% Royalty Payment with Respect to Utility's Investment in Unregulated Operations - Application of 2% Royalty as Rebuttable Presumption in Future Rate Cases

3. The determinations of respondent Public Service Commission (PSC) which imputed revenues to petitioner telephone company in the form of a royalty in an amount equal to 2% of petitioner's capitalization in unregulated operations as an adjustment to petitioner's rates to reflect the uncompensated use of certain intangible assets by petitioner's unregulated affiliates (regulated value assurance mechanism), and to offset certain costs imposed on petitioner by its affiliates that could not be readily or directly quantified by conventional rate making (positive benefits mechanism), have a rational basis and will therefore be sustained. The PSC, in using the 2% royalty as a means of accomplishing the adjustment intended by the regulated value assurance mechanism and the positive benefits mechanism, exercised its judgment in a technical area of public utility regulation that lies within its special competence and expertise. In addition, the PSC's use of a similar 2% royalty as a rebuttable presumption in future rate cases with respect to any utility's investment in unregulated operations is not irrational. The purpose of the presumption is not to mandate the imposition of a royalty in all future rate cases or to modify the manner in which evidence will be weighed, but to put utilities and other parties on notice that the royalty issue must be addressed. Furthermore, the arguments raised by several utilities concerning subsequent PSC orders relating to the applicability of the rebuttable presumption to holding companies, "upstream" affiliates and certain subsidiaries are not properly raised in this proceeding.

Public Utilities - Rate Making - Constitutionality of Public Service Commission's Imputation of 2% Royalty Payment with Respect to Utility's Investment in Unregulated Operations

4. The determinations of respondent Public Service Commission (PSC) which imputed revenues to petitioner telephone company in the form of a royalty in an amount equal to 2% of petitioner's capitalization in unregulated operations as an adjustment to petitioner's rates to reflect the uncompensated use of certain intangible assets by petitioner's unregulated affiliates, and to offset certain costs imposed on petitioner by its affiliates that could not be readily or directly quantified by conventional rate making, have a rational basis and will not be invalidated on constitutional grounds. The economic impact of the PSC's action and its effect on investor expectations are negligible, and there has been no permanent appropriation of utility property. The alleged effect of the royalty imputation on interstate commerce is indirect and speculative, and in view of the legitimate local concern addressed by the royalty, there is no violation of the Commerce Clause. There is no equal protection or due process violation since the PSC's action does not involve a fundamental right and is rationally related to a legitimate State interest.

Schulz v. State


69971


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


205 A.D.2d 912; 613 N.Y.S.2d 724; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6304



June 16, 1994, Decided  
June 16, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and an action for declaratory judgment, granted respondents' motions to dismiss the third through sixth and eighth causes of action in the petition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners commenced an action against respondents pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and sought a declaratory judgment, contending that certain expenditures of public funds by various governmental entities were made in contravention of the mandates of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 8. The supreme court dismissed the petition in its entirety, and petitioners appealed, arguing that it erred in dismissing their third, sixth, and eighth causes of action. The court affirmed the judgment holding that there was no reason to allow petitioners to relitigate their causes of action, holding that N.Y. Educ. Law § 2037 vested in the New York Commissioner of Education (commissioner), exclusive jurisdiction to resolve disputes over the validity of school district meetings or elections. Moreover, the proscription against supreme court review of election disputes was not, technically, a limitation on subject matter jurisdiction, but rather a legislative direction that cases within the scope of N.Y. Educ. Law § 2037 be resolved in the first instance by the commissioner. Furthermore, the expenditure of public funds for the production of the newsletter did not violate N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's judgment, which dismissed petitioners' cause of action, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and seeking a declaratory judgment that certain expenditures of public funds by various governmental entities were made in contravention of the mandates of the New York Constitution, against respondents in its entirety.

First United Am. Life Ins. Co. v. Curiale


69978


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


200 A.D.2d 243; 613 N.Y.S.2d 494; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6305



June 16, 1994, Decided  
June 16, 1994, Entered



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance company challenged a decision of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which converted a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 application into an action for declaratory judgment and entered a declaration in favor of respondent insurance superintendent.


OVERVIEW: The insurance company, which issued Medicare supplemental insurance policies, sought a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 11, §§ 360.4(e), 360.11(a)(1)(iii) were invalid. Under the regulations, all individual and small group health insurance policies, including Medicare supplemental policies, had to be community rated. The regulations were promulgated in order to give insurers a choice: an insurer could cease issuing the insurance before February 1, 1993, and a community rating would not apply to its existing guaranteed renewable policies, or it could continue to issue such coverage on or after February 1, 1993, in which case community rating would apply to the previously issued policies. The insurance company argued that the regulations impermissibly impaired existing contract rights and violated equal protection and due process. The lower court ruled for the insurance superintendent, and the insurance company appealed. The court affirmed the lower court. The court held that the element of choice granted to the insurer in the regulations made the prospective application of the statute constitutionally permissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that granted a declaratory judgment in favor of the insurance superintendent in the insurance company's action to invalidate the regulations.


HEADNOTES

Insurance - Health and Accident Insurance - Open Enrollment and Community Rating for Individual and Small Group Health Insurance Policies - Constitutionality of Regulations Applying Community Rating to Guaranteed Renewable Policies

Regulations promulgated by defendant Superintendent of Insurance, which, in implementing the open enrollment and community rating law (L 1992, ch 501), give an insurer issuing Medicare supplemental insurance policies in New York the choice to either stop issuing such policies in New York before February 1, 1993, in which case community rating would not apply to its guaranteed renewable policies (11 NYCRR 360.4 [e]), or to continue issuing such coverage after that date, in which case community rating would apply to the previously issued policies (11 NYCRR 360.11 [a] [1] [iii]), do not impermissibly impair existing contract rights and are not otherwise unconstitutional. In order to survive the constitutional challenge, a choice must exist, and, under the circumstances present here, the regulations provide plaintiff with a very real choice. The regulations do not require plaintiff to offer coverage or to insure against risks different from the coverage offered or risks insured against in the Medicare supplemental policies it previously issued. The terms of plaintiff's guaranteed renewable policies do not prohibit a change in premium at the time of renewal. Rather, the policies make express provision for a change in renewal premiums in accordance with plaintiff's table of premium rates applicable to all policies of the same form and class. Community rating has merely expanded the "class" to "the entire pool of risks covered by [the] policy or contract form without regard to age, sex, health status or occupation" (Insurance Law § 3231 [a]).

Board of Educ. v. Ramapo Teachers' Ass'n


70389


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


200 A.D.2d 62; 611 N.Y.S.2d 962; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5640



May 26, 1994, Decided  
May 26, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent teachers' association sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which granted the application of petitioner board of education pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7503 to stay arbitration of the association's claim that the board should return to its prior payment policy.


OVERVIEW: The board followed a practice of paying teachers returning in September a full two weeks' salary during the first week of work. The board determined that this payment policy violated N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1 and N.Y. Educ. Law § 3015(3), and it invoked a new practice whereby teachers would be paid the week of their return for one week only. The association served a demand for arbitration pursuant to the parties' collective bargaining agreement, seeking an award compelling the board to adhere to its former payment policy. The board commenced a proceeding to stay arbitration, claiming that any award would violate public policy because its past pay practice was illegal. The trial court held that the past practice did not violate the state constitution but did violate § 3015(3), which provided that nothing in that section should be construed to authorize payment in advance of services. On appeal, the court affirmed. The trial court properly found that the former payment policy violated § 3105(3) and that the matter was not subject to arbitration because the only relief the arbitrator could fashion would be contrary to law and thus violative of public policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting the board's application to stay arbitration of the association's claim that the board should return to its prior payment policy.


HEADNOTES

Arbitration - Matters Arbitrable - Prepayment of Teacher Salaries

1. The order of Supreme Court granting petitioner school district's application pursuant to CPLR 7503 for a stay of arbitration of respondent teachers' association's demand that petitioner adhere to its former pay practice, which resulted in an advance payment of one week's pay or more to teachers returning in September, should be affirmed on the ground that petitioner's past payment policy was violative of Education Law § 3015 (3). The language of the statute that "[n]othing in this section shall be construed to authorize payment in advance of rendering service to the school district" clearly prohibits payment of teachers' salaries in advance of services. As such, petitioner's past payment policy is contrary to the public policy proclaimed in the statute. While the preferred means of resolving a contractual dispute is to allow arbitration and afford an arbitrator an opportunity to fashion a remedy that does not violate public policy, here, the only relief the arbitrator could fashion under respondent's demand would be violative of public policy. Furthermore, the record does not support respondent's claim that Supreme Court improperly examined the merits of the grievance in determining the application for a stay of arbitration.

Arbitration - Matters Arbitrable - Prepayment of Teacher Salaries - Advance Salary Payments Not Unconstitutional Loan to Teachers

2. The order of Supreme Court granting petitioner school district's application pursuant to CPLR 7503 for a stay of arbitration of respondent teachers' association's demand that petitioner adhere to its former pay practice, which resulted in an advance payment of one week's pay or more to teachers returning in September, should be affirmed on the ground that petitioner's past payment policy was violative of Education Law § 3015 (3). Supreme Court did not err in ruling that petitioner's past policy was not violative of NY Constitution, article VIII, § 1, since the past payment policy was not a loan to teachers, which would be prohibited, but was a prepayment for services promised to be rendered and the failure to perform the contracted services would constitute a breach of contract.

Appeal - Matters Reviewable - Right of Successful Nonaggrieved Party to Seek Review of Adverse Holding

3. Where Supreme Court, in granting petitioner school district's application pursuant to CPLR 7503 for a stay of arbitration of respondent teachers' association's demand that petitioner adhere to its former pay practice, which resulted in an advance payment of one week's pay or more to teachers returning in September, properly determined that petitioner's past payment policy was violative of Education Law § 3015 (3) but did not constitute a loan to teachers in violation of NY Constitution, article VIII, § 1, petitioner is entitled to review of the constitutional issue even though, having succeeded before Supreme Court, it did not cross-appeal, since it may raise this argument as an alternative ground for affirmance to protect its success in Supreme Court.

Noel v. Board of Elections


92-05277


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


203 A.D.2d 576; 611 N.Y.S.2d 222; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4236


March 31, 1994, Argued  
April 25, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Putnam County (New York), which affirmed a decision of respondents, Board of Elections of the County of Putnam (board) and related entities, to terminate the employee from her civil service position and dismissed her action for reinstatement and benefits.


OVERVIEW: The board terminated the employee from her civil service position. Thereafter she filed a wrongful termination action seeking recovering of her salary, benefits, and reinstatement. The lower court dismissed the petition because the employee failed to file a timely notice of claim as to her wrongful termination causes of action. The employee appealed. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment but not its reasoning. Although the employee was not required to serve a notice of claim, she was unable to maintain a wrongful termination causes of action because her position was unclassified. Thus, she was effectively an at-will employee. In addition, the employee was unable to maintain her third cause of action because she did not name the attorney general, who was a necessary party. Based on the foregoing, the employee's petition was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which affirmed the board's decision to terminate the employee from her civil service position and dismissed her action for lost benefits and for reinstatement.

Video Aid Corp. v. Town of Wallkill


92-07196, 92-07198


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


203 A.D.2d 554; 610 N.Y.S.2d 610; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4337


March 31, 1994, Submitted  
April 25, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town appealed a decision of the Supreme Court of Orange County (New York), which ordered the town to refund fees it had imposed on plaintiff local taxpayers for water and sewer tap-in fees. The town argued that it had the power to impose the fees. It also argued that a taxpayer who had not paid under protest was not entitled to a refund, even if the fees were improperly assessed.


OVERVIEW: A town imposed fees on local businesses whenever they "tapped in" to existing water and sewer lines. Under N.Y. Town Law § 198(1)(h), a town's authority to impose a sewer connection fee was limited to the construction expense of providing service, a fee for inspecting the connection, and the expenses of performing any service or providing any special benefit to the user. Under N.Y. Town Law § 198(3)(a), a town's authority to impose tap-in fees on its water facilities was limited to the costs of installation of the supply pipe. Eight taxpayers paid the fees under protest and then filed an action against the town. The trial court declared the resolutions under which the fees were charged to be void and ordered the fees to be refunded. The town appealed. The court, citing the applicable statutes, affirmed. Although one of the taxpayers had not paid its fees under protest, the court nonetheless found that it was entitled to relief. The taxpayer filed its action promptly. Not only were the fees paid under duress where the town threatened to deny the taxpayer's building permit if it withheld funds, but it would unjustly enrich the town to allow it to keep the fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that was in favor of the taxpayers.

Schulz v. Cobleskill-Richmondville Cent. Sch. Dist. Bd. of Educ.


69546


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


197 A.D.2d 247; 610 N.Y.S.2d 694; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4143



April 21, 1994, Decided  
April 21, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed an action against respondent school district seeking a declaration that the school district's custodial agreement constituted a gift of public money to a private organization was unconstitutional and violated the competitive bidding requirements contained in N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103. The Supreme Court, Schoharie County (New York) granted taxpayer's motion for summary judgment in part. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer's suit was based on the school district service contract with a custodial management company. On appeal, the court held that the trial court properly determined that the taxpayers did no have standing under the state finance law because the taxpayers failed to clearly trace the moneys expanded or to be expended by the school district, pursuant to the custodial contract, to identifiable state funds. Additionally, the court held that the trial court's recognition of a constitutional right of standing was improper because their constitutional claim that the agreement constituted a gift or loan of public funds in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1, did not create a constitutional right of standing. However, the court held that the taxpayers residing in the district did have standing under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51. The court, also, held that the trial court correctly determined that the taxpayers' constitutional and statutory claims were not time barred, as their proceeding was commenced well within the applicable four-month statute of limitations, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217. Lastly, the court held that taxpayers did raise a valid due process claim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision. Moreover, the court held that the school district's cross motion to dismiss the taxpayer's complaint was denied with respect to all of the taxpayer's except the one taxpayer that did not live in the district.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Taxpayers' Action against School District to Annul Management Services Contract as Unauthorized Expenditure of State Funds Supreme Court properly determined that petitioners lack standing as citizen-taxpayers to maintain a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and action for a declaratory judgment against respondent School District under State Finance Law § 123-b to annul, inter alia, a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs, which, by its terms, was automatically renewed for the 1993-1994 fiscal school year by virtue of the adoption of the budget on June 30, 1993, since, while an action seeking declaratory and equitable relief may be brought against respondents under the statute if State funds are involved, petitioners have failed to clearly trace the moneys expended or to be expended by the District in payment of the agreement to identifiable State funds.

Parties - Standing - Taxpayers' Action against School District Supreme Court improperly recognized petitioners' constitutional right of standing to maintain a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action against respondent school district to annul a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs. Since this is a case involving a claimed unauthorized expenditure of State funds, the parties must follow the statutory procedures. The fact that petitioners also assert a constitutional claim that the agreement constitutes a gift or loan of public funds to a private organization in violation of NY Constitution, article VIII, § 1 does not create a constitutional right of standing.

Parties - Standing - Taxpayers' Action against School District to Annul Management Services Contract as Violative of Competitive Bidding Requirements Although petitioners lack standing as citizen-taxpayers to maintain a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and action for a declaratory judgment against respondent School District under State Finance Law § 123-b to annul, inter alia, a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs, which, by its terms, was automatically renewed for the 1993-1994 fiscal school year by virtue of the adoption of the budget on June 30, 1993, those petitioners residing within the District have standing under General Municipal Law § 51 to challenge the expenditure of taxpayers' funds in violation of the competitive bidding requirements of General Municipal Law § 103. Since a school district is now classified as a municipal corporation (General Construction Law § 66 [2]), it is fair and reasonable to remove the barrier which had previously excluded local citizen taxpayers from using the judicial process in challenging the fiscal policies and expenditures of their school districts.

Limitation of Actions - Four-Month Statute of Limitations - Proceeding against School District to Annul Management Services Contract A combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and action for a declaratory judgment brought against respondent School District to annul, inter alia, a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs, which, by its terms, was automatically renewed for the 1993-1994 fiscal school year by virtue of the adoption of the budget on June 30, 1993, as constituting an unauthorized expenditure of District taxpayers' funds is not time barred since the proceeding was commenced well within the applicable four-month Statute of Limitations (CPLR 217).

Municipal Corporations - Bids and Bidders - Taxpayers' Action against School District to Annul Management Services Contract as Violative of Competitive Bidding Requirements In a combined CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action against respondent School District to annul, inter alia, a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs, which, by its terms, was automatically renewed for the 1993-1994 fiscal school year by virtue of the adoption of the budget on June 30, 1993, as constituting an unauthorized expenditure of District taxpayers' funds in violation of the competitive bidding requirements of General Municipal Law § 103, Supreme Court erred in granting summary judgment to petitioners voiding the June 30, 1993 agreement and enjoining respondents from entering into any similar contracts without public bidding since respondents' papers raised a material question of fact, requiring a trial, as to whether the services to be performed under the agreement involved specialized skills, expertise, and the exercise of judgment and discretion so as to bring them within the "professional services" exception to competitive bidding requirements.

Civil Rights - Federal Civil Rights Claim - Taxpayers' Action against School District to Annul Management Services Contract Petitioners, who have standing as taxpayers of respondent School District under General Municipal Law § 51 to maintain a combined CPLR article 78 and declaratory judgment action to annul, inter alia, a support management services contract relating to custodial and maintenance needs, which, by its terms, was automatically renewed for the 1993-1994 fiscal school year by virtue of the adoption of the budget on June 30, 1993, as constituting an unauthorized expenditure of taxpayers' funds in violation of the competitive bidding requirements of General Municipal Law § 103, failed to allege a due process violation sufficient to sustain an action under 42 USC § 1983. Although petitioners have alleged a deprivation of property under color of State law, since State law provides an adequate remedy under General Municipal Law § 51 to compensate citizen taxpayers for any loss, the requirements of due process are satisfied.

Cupid's Video Boutique v. Roth


51530


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


203 A.D.2d 70; 610 N.Y.S.2d 24; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3633



April 7, 1994, Decided  
April 7, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent zoning appeals board sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which annulled its determination denying petitioner landowner's application for an adult use permit.


OVERVIEW: The landowner sought to annul the board's determination, which denied the landowner an adult use permit requested as of right, on the ground that the adult use was within a 500 foot radius of a residential area. The court reversed the trial court's decision annulling the board's determination. The Town Code of Babylon § 213-378, which provided that adult uses as defined in Town Code of Babylon § 213-377 should not be located within a 500 foot radius of any area zoned for residential use, was a permissible restriction. There was no express statutory language requiring that measurements be taken in a straight line from the center of the nearest entrance. The interpretation of a zoning ordinance by a local board was entitled to deference, so long as it was not irrational, unreasonable or inconsistent with the governing statute. The clear purpose of the ordinance at issue was to eliminate the secondary effects of adult uses as well as to attempt to control the future development in business districts, and not to regulate expression. The board's measurement of the 500 foot radius was not irrational, unreasonable or inconsistent with the legitimate purpose of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's determination annulling the board's determination denying the landowner's application for an adult use permit.

Dawson v. Higgins


51191


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


197 A.D.2d 127; 610 N.Y.S.2d 200; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3295



April 5, 1994, Decided  
April 5, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court (New York), which denied her motion for summary judgment and which declared City of New York, N.Y., Administrative Code § 26-408 (b)(1), and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 2204.5(a) to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The landlord purchased rent-controlled buildings which housed tenants who had resided there for decades. Later, the rent-control laws were amended and she was unable to evict her tenants on owner-occupancy grounds. She commenced an action against defendants, city and the commissioner of housing, challenging the 20-year residency bar to eviction as a physical as well as regulatory taking and a due process violation under both the federal and state constitutions. The supreme court declared the relevant statute constitutional and the decision was affirmed on appeal. The court held that the landlord failed to show that the amended rent-control laws created perpetual tenancies because a number of events would allow her to recover the apartments, and thus there was no physical taking. Further, she failed to overcome the presumption of constitutionality of the regulation and failed to prove every element of the claim beyond a reasonable doubt. There was no regulatory taking because she failed to demonstrate an immediate and compelling necessity for her use. The court concluded that the residency status protected by the challenged regulation advanced a legitimate state interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Physical Taking - Rent-Control Provision Prohibiting Eviction of 20-Year Tenants New York City's owner-occupancy eviction provisions, which, effective June 19, 1984, prohibit a landlord's recovery of a rent-controlled housing accommodation for his or her family's use where a member of the household lawfully occupying the accommodation has been a tenant in the building for 20 years or more (Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-408 [b] [1]; Rent and Eviction Regulations [9 NYCRR] § 2204.5 [a]), do not effect an unconstitutional physical taking of property without just compensation. Government has considerable latitude in regulating landlord-tenant relationships to preclude eviction in hardship, emergency and rent-control cases. Plaintiffs, who purchased their rent-controlled building in 1983, have failed to make a showing that the enumerated contingencies which still permit a landlord to recover a 20-year tenant's housing accommodation cannot occur and thus are unable to show that the 20-year residency bar to eviction has created perpetual tenancies so as to constitute a physical taking. Furthermore, the fact that a rent-regulated tenancy might itself be of indefinite duration does not, without more, render it a permanent physical occupation of property. Moreover, while housing regulations that force owners, in the first instance, to put their properties to use for residential housing could constitute a physical taking, restrictions imposed on existing tenancies to protect the current tenant, as here, are generally permissible.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Regulatory Taking - Rent-Control Provision Prohibiting Eviction of 20-Year Tenants New York City's owner-occupancy eviction provisions, which, effective June 19, 1984, prohibit a landlord's recovery of a rent-controlled housing accommodation for his or her family's use where a member of the household lawfully occupying the accommodation has been a tenant in the building for 20 years or more (Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-408 [b] [1]; Rent and Eviction Regulations [9 NYCRR] § 2204.5 [a]), do not effect an unconstitutional regulatory taking of property without just compensation by denying plaintiffs the economically viable use of their property. The challenged housing regulations have not substantially frustrated plaintiffs' reasonable investment-backed expectation of being able to evict their tenants. Plaintiffs purchased the premises in 1983 knowing that it was subject to rent control and any expectancy they might realistically have had as to the recapture of the apartments in question for their own personal use was always a speculative one, being contingent on their ability to demonstrate an immediate and compelling necessity for such use. Furthermore, an assessment of the economic impact of the regulations on plaintiffs' property cannot be made in the absence of some factual showing as to the rental income and purchase price of the building. Lastly, the nature of the regulation is not of the type to constitute a regulatory taking. The regulations at issue do not create perpetual tenancies but, rather, allow for termination of the tenancies affected by the occurrence of normal events.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Regulatory Taking - Rent-Control Provision Prohibiting Eviction of 20-Year Tenants New York City's owner-occupancy eviction provisions, which, effective June 19, 1984, prohibit a landlord's recovery of a rent-controlled housing accommodation for his or her family's use where a member of the household lawfully occupying the accommodation has been a tenant in the building for 20 years or more (Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-408 [b] [1]; Rent and Eviction Regulations [9 NYCRR] § 2204.5 [a]), do not effect an unconstitutional regulatory taking of property without just compensation. The challenged regulations substantially advance the legitimate State interest of protecting long-term tenants of rent-controlled apartments from eviction and preventing homelessness.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Involuntary Servitude - Due Process In an action challenging the constitutionality of New York City's owner-occupancy eviction provisions, which prohibit a landlord's recovery of a rent-controlled housing accommodation for his or her own use where a member of the household lawfully occupying the accommodation has been a tenant in the building for 20 years or more (Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-408 [b] [1]; Rent and Eviction Regulations [9 NYCRR] § 2204.5 [a]), plaintiffs' claim that the 20-year residency bar to eviction subjects them to involuntary servitude is frivolous since the challenged provisions do not compel them to remain landlords against their will. Plaintiffs need only sell their building to escape the minimal restrictions placed on them by the challenged provisions. Plaintiffs' due process claims are equally frivolous since rent-control provisions such as those at issue are well within the government's police powers. They bear a reasonable relationship to the valid public purpose of preserving affordable housing and preventing the dislocation of lower income and moderate income tenants.

Long Island College Hosp. v. New York State Dep't of Health


92-1473


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


203 A.D.2d 292; 609 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3133


January 24, 1994, Argued  
April 4, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the New York State Department of Health (DOH) and its Commissioner, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which annulled a denial of petitioner hospital's applications to revise its Medicaid reimbursement rates. The hospital cross-appealed the trial court's failure to declare that investment income from the hospital's designated funds was income from funded depreciation.


OVERVIEW: The hospital was a participant in the Medicaid program. The DOH amended N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 86-1.23(b) by providing that board-designated funds and the accrual of liabilities to funded depreciation accounts were not funding of depreciation. In calculating the hospital's Medicaid reimbursement rates, the DOH offset the hospital's interest expense by the amount of investment income earned on funds that the hospital had designated as funded depreciation accounts. The DOH reasoned that the accounts were unrestricted board-designated funds, whose investment income offset the hospital's interest expense. The hospital challenged the Commissioner's determinations and the validity of the amendment to § 86-1.23(b). The trial court remitted the proceeding to the DOH to determine whether the funds were properly established and whether the income from the funds was used to fund depreciation. On appeal, the court modified the order and as modified, affirmed. The DOH had to determine whether the corpora of the funds were restricted and used for accumulated depreciation. If they were not, any income could be used to offset interest expense.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order to provide for remittitur to the DOH to determine whether the hospital's funds were established for funding of depreciation and whether the expenditure of funds from the accounts was necessary and used for funding of depreciation. The court affirmed the order, as modified.
