Arcuri v. Village of Remsen


0089


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


202 A.D.2d 991; 609 N.Y.S.2d 507; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3360



March 11, 1994, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner of apartment buildings (owner) appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Oneida County (New York), which denied his motion for summary judgment and granted defendant village's motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint in the owner's action challenging the village's enactment of a law establishing a "user unit" classification for sewer capital charges and rents. The owner alleged that the law was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The village constructed a new sewer system. After public hearings, the village promulgated a "user unit" classification system for sewer capital charges and rents pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 451(1)(e). The system allocated a certain number of units to parties depending on their size and usage. The owner brought an action challenging the law as illegal and unconstitutional. The parties brought summary judgment motions. The trial court denied the owner's motion and granted the village's motion. On appeal, the court modified and affirmed. The court held that the system was not unconstitutional because it presented a reasonable response to the multiple concerns of the village, including issues as to the village's small size and antiquated water system, while ensuring that the sewer charges bore a reasonable relationship to the use of the system. However, the court held that the trial court should not have granted summary judgment to the village dismissing the compliant but should have declared the parties' rights; thus the court reinstated the complaint and granted judgment in favor of the village, declaring that the law was not illegal or unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order by reinstating the owner's complaint so as to declare the rights of the parties, granting judgment in favor of the village, and declaring that the village's law establishing the user unit classification system for sewer charges was not unconstitutional. The court otherwise affirmed.

Kaplan v. State


69266


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


202 A.D.2d 742; 608 N.Y.S.2d 366; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1837



March 3, 1994, Decided  
March 3, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner interested party sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, granted a motion to dismiss in favor of respondent state agencies on the ground of laches. The interested party challenged the constitutionality of a financing scheme embodied in legislative enactments.


OVERVIEW: The state agencies entered into contracts, pursuant to legislation, whereby the medical facilities finance agency agreed to issue bonds to finance construction of facilities by the development corporation and to lease the completed facilities to the department of mental hygiene. The comptroller agreed to make the annual payments due. The interested party commenced a proceeding to prohibit disbursement of moneys to bondholders where such monies represented amounts of federal Medicaid funds the state legislature had not appropriated, contending that the allowance of future federal Medicaid funds to pay bondholders increased the debt obligation of the state without a public referendum. The trial court dismissed the petition on the ground of laches. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The court held that the petition was brought six years following enactment of the legislation authorizing the challenged financing plan and nearly seven months following the sale of the challenged bonds. The court found that a recall of the issued bonds in reliance upon the enactment would cause traumatic disturbance to settled matters of public finances and governance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the interested party's petition challenging the constitutionality of a state financing plan.

New York City Off Track Betting Corp. v. State Racing & Wagering Bd.


69013


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


196 A.D.2d 15; 608 N.Y.S.2d 328; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1542



February 24, 1994, Decided  
February 24, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, State of New York Racing and Wagering Board and race tracks, sought review of orders entered in the Supreme Court of Schenectady County (New York), which denied defendants' motions for summary judgment in plaintiff off-track betting parlor operators' action challenging the board's implementation of a regulation that required the off-track betting parlor operators to return gains garnered from positive missed pools.


OVERVIEW: In 1983, the board promulgated N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 5204.1(c)(1), which required off-track betting parlor operators (operators) to return to later common pools the gains garnered from positive missed pools. The regulation was not implemented until October 8, 1991, when the board ordered the operators to make available missed pool moneys that had accumulated from the date the regulation was promulgated through the end of 1990. The operators filed an action alleging that the board did not have power to promulgate the regulation or the October 8, 1991, directive. The lower court denied defendants' motions for summary judgment, as the action was barred by the four-month statute of limitations. On appeal, the court reversed. The court determined that the administrative action the operators were challenging was the promulgation of the regulation that became final and binding in 1983 when the board published the regulation, that it was evident that the operators understood the regulation's consequences in 1983, and that the challenge to the regulation filed more than six years after its effective date was barred by the four-month statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by reversing the lower court's order denying defendants' motions for summary judgments filed in the off-track betting parlor operators' action challenging the board's implementation of a regulation requiring the off-track betting parlor operators to return gains garnered from positive missed pools.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions - When Cause of Action Accrues - Four-Month Statute of Limitations - Challenge to Regulation of State Racing and Wagering Board

A challenge to the validity of a regulation issued by the State Racing and Wagering Board (9 NYCRR 5204.15) is barred by the four-month Statute of Limitations since plaintiffs did not commence the action until more than six years after the effective date of the regulation, which was the date that plaintiffs understood the regulation's consequences and were able to accurately assess its impact. An administrative directive issued by the Board some eight years later did not affect the impact upon plaintiffs for limitations purposes since the directive was simply an incidental measure employed to effectuate the regulation. Therefore, even if the current action is deemed a declaratory judgment action, plaintiffs' first two causes of action are untimely. However, inasmuch as plaintiffs' third cause of action is authorized by the regulation in question, Supreme Court properly severed it since the present state of the record precludes an informed determination of either its merits or the applicability of the limitations defense.

People v. Perrin


69035


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


201 A.D.2d 853; 608 N.Y.S.2d 333; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1562



February 24, 1994, Decided  
February 24, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed an order from the County Court of Albany County (New York), which granted defendants' motions to dismiss the indictment on the grounds that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 375(30) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Police stopped a car, which had very dark-tinted windows, driven by one of the defendants after observing several objects on the dashboard, for violation of the obstructed vision statute, N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 375(30). With defendant driver's permission (the other two defendants were passengers in the car), police searched the car and found cocaine. Defendants were charged with possession of a controlled substance. Upon defendants' motion, the trial court suppressed evidence of the seized cocaine and dismissed the indictment, after making a finding that § 375(30) was unconstitutionally vague. On appeal, the court reversed and remitted the case for further proceedings. The trial court erred by failing to hold an evidentiary suppression hearing to determine whether the police acted reasonably and whether defendants consented to the search. Thus, the trial court's declaration that § 375(30) was unconstitutional, was improper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remitted for further proceedings; the trial court failed to hold a suppression hearing in order to establish whether the police action was reasonable and if consent to search was given.

Mancuso v. Levitt


51167-51168-51169


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


201 A.D.2d 386; 607 N.Y.S.2d 353; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1359



February 17, 1994, Decided  
February 17, 1994, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed without opinion by Mancuso v. Levitt, 83 N.Y.2d 952, 639 N.E.2d 417, 1994 N.Y. LEXIS 1504, 615 N.Y.S.2d 876 (1994)
Appeal denied by Mancuso v. Levitt, 84 N.Y.2d 810, 645 N.E.2d 1219, 1994 N.Y. LEXIS 4409, 621 N.Y.S.2d 519 (1994)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action regarding the constitutionality of the civil service examination procedures followed, defendants, city and related others, appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied defendants' motion to dismiss plaintiff protestors' claims with respect to a civil service examination as time barred and granted the protestors' cross-motion for leave to renew and reargue their motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The protestors challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law §§ 50-a, 50-(7) regarding civil service exam procedures. Although the protestors failed to rebut the strong constitutionality of the statutes, the trial court declared one section unconstitutional on its face and the other as applied. The court held that such was improper and modified the order, in part, by denying the protestors' request for a declaration that the statutes were unconstitutional. The court further denied the protestors cross-motion for renewal and reargument, granted defendants' motion for leave to renew and reargue, and upon renewal, granted defendants' motion to dismiss the claims as time-barred. The court noted that when the legislature expressed its intent that judicial review on substantive matter was precluded, the merits of an agency's determination were not reviewable. The court further noted that even though the statutes did not specifically follow the test set forth in pre-enactment case law for the review of civil service exams, omitting one step in the review was the legislature's prerogative, and the trial court was not warranted in regarding such step as a constitutional directive.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously modified, on the law, the facts and in the exercise of discretion, the order to the extent of denying the protestors' cross-motion for renewal and reargument; granting defendants' motion for leave to renew and reargue; and, upon renewal, granting defendants' motion to dismiss plaintiffs' claims as to the civil service examination as time-barred. The court otherwise affirmed the order, without costs.

Mackston v. State


92-01228


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


200 A.D.2d 717; 607 N.Y.S.2d 357; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 759


January 10, 1994, Submitted  
January 31, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, judges of the City Court of White Plains, New York, appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which declared the application of N.Y. Jud. Law § 221-i violative of the rights of plaintiff, a former judge of the City Court of the City of Long Beach, New York, under the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought a judgment declaring § 221-i unconstitutional insofar as it provided for an unfavorable salary differential between plaintiff and defendants. The supreme court granted plaintiff relief, finding that there was no rational basis for the salary differential between plaintiff and a judge of the City Court of White Plains. Upon appeal by defendants, the court reversed. The court held that § 221-i was not unconstitutional and that it did not violate plaintiff's right to equal protection of the laws. The court found that the governmental statistics within the record indicated that not only was the full-time population of the City of White Plains greater than that of the City of Long Beach, but the cost of living in White Plains was almost double that of Long Beach. Accordingly, since the statistics indicated that White Plains was a more expensive place to reside, the court held that there was a rational basis for the retention of geographically disparate salaries between plaintiff and a judge of the City Court of White Plains.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

Tribeca Community Ass'n v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp.


50940-50941


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


200 A.D.2d 536; 607 N.Y.S.2d 18; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 624



January 27, 1994, Decided  
January 27, 1994, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff community association sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) that granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, New York State Urban Development Corporation (UDC), public authorities, and commodities exchanges, in the community association's action to review the UDC's finding that a certain parcel of property was blighted.


OVERVIEW: The commodities exchanges claimed that their present offices were inadequate. To induce them to stay in the city, the public authorities negotiated the conveyance of a parcel of land from the city to the UDC and planned to construct a 10-story office tower upon the parcel to lease to the commodities exchanges. Partial financing of the project was to be provided by public funds. However, the UDC found that the parcel was blighted. The community association filed an action against the UDC, and the parties filed summary judgment motions. The trial court denied the community association's motion and granted the UDC's motion, dismissing the complaint. The community association appealed, and the court affirmed the trial court's order and held that there was no reason to disturb the UDC's finding. Further, in the context of the modern service economy, there was no significant distinction between an industrial project that received public funding and a commercial project for the purposes of the enabling statute, N.Y. Not-For-Profit-Corp. Law § 1411(c). The project provided a substantial public benefit and was not the equivalent of an unconstitutional gift or loan to a private party.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment in favor of the UDC in the community association's action to review the UDC's finding that a certain parcel of property was blighted.

Long Island Power Auth. v. Shoreham-Wading River Cent. Sch. Dist.


93-05730


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


195 A.D.2d 140; 606 N.Y.S.2d 325; 1994 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 172


November 23, 1993, Argued  
January 10, 1994, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant taxing jurisdictions and plaintiffs, the Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) and the Long Island Lighting Company (LILCO), appealed, in an action for a judgment declaring, inter alia, the obligations of LIPA to make payments in lieu of taxes (PILOTS) under N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1020-q(1), from an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York) which, inter alia, granted, in part, plaintiffs' motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: LILCO paid taxes on the property for the first half of the year in which the property was transferred to LIPA, and LIPA paid the remainder. On appeal, the court held that no restriction was apparent on the face of § 1020-q(1) which would postpone until after the tax year in which the property was transferred from LILCO to LIPA the commencement of PILOTS attributable to the property. The court affirmed the holding that LILCO was not obligated to the taxing jurisdictions for any real property taxes attributable to property it conveyed to LIPA that accrued after that conveyance, which occurred three months into the tax year. The court held that because N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1020-q(3) had been held unconstitutional as applied to LILCO, there was no statutory prohibition against the maintenance by LILCO of a tax certiorari proceeding with respect to the property. The court held that the lower court properly concluded that LIPA's PILOT obligation under § 1020(q)(1) was perpetual, and the court agreed with the lower court that the term "nonoperative" as used in § 1020-q(1) meant incapable of generating electricity without regard to the property's nuclear plant's state of construction.


OUTCOME: The order granting plaintiffs summary judgment was affirmed insofar as appealed and cross-appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities - Long Island Power Authority - Acquisition and Closure of Shoreham Nuclear Plant - Commencement of Payments in Lieu of Taxes Supreme Court correctly determined that, pursuant to the Long Island Power Authority Act (Public Authorities Law § 1020 et seq.), which requires plaintiff Long Island Power Authority (LIPA), as part of its acquisition of the Shoreham nuclear power plant from plaintiff Long Island Lighting Company (LILCO) for the purpose of closing and decommissioning the facility, to make certain payments in lieu of taxes (PILOTS) to defendants, the taxing jurisdictions within which the facility is located, "in the first year after the acquisition" in an amount equal to 100% "of the taxes and assessments which would have been received by such taxing jurisdictions" (Public Authorities Law § 1020-q [1]), LIPA's PILOT obligation commenced on March 1, 1992, the day after the facility was transferred to LIPA for $ 1. Supreme Court thus correctly determined that LILCO is not obligated to defendants for any real property taxes for the 1991-1992 tax year attributable to the Shoreham property after it was conveyed to LIPA, and that it is consequently entitled to a refund for any taxes that it paid, attributable to that portion of the tax year that remained after the date of its transfer of the Shoreham property. The payment made by LIPA to defendants on or about June 1, 1992 pursuant to the negotiated agreement between the parties must be characterized as the first installment of PILOTS and not as a tax payment. The statute should not be construed to make the PILOTS payable only at the end of the 1991-1992 tax year. No restriction is apparent on the face of the statute which would serve to postpone the commencement of PILOTS attributable to the Shoreham property. The Legislature intended that the Act make PILOTS a complete substitution for real property taxes and assessments that otherwise would be due on the Shoreham property and the Act's provisions supersede any inconsistent laws (Public Authorities Law § 1020-gg).

Public Authorities - Long Island Power Authority - Acquisition and Closure of Shoreham Nuclear Plant - Calculation of Payments in Lieu of Taxes Supreme Court, in finding that the payments in lieu of taxes (PILOTS) plaintiff Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) is required to make under the Long Island Power Authority Act (Public Authorities Law § 1020-q [1]) as part of its acquisition of the Shoreham nuclear power plant from plaintiff Long Island Lighting Company (LILCO) for the purpose of closing and decommissioning the facility are to be based in the first instance on the "taxes and assessments which LILCO would have been required to pay on the property conveyed to LIPA for the period March 1, 1992--February 28, 1993 had the conveyance to LIPA not occurred" properly determined that the calculation of PILOTS is to be made subject to any adjustment in the assessed value of the Shoreham property occurring as a result of various tax certiorari proceedings commenced by LILCO under RPTL article 7. Since the provision of the Act which provides that neither LIPA nor "any other entity" may seek a tax refund "of property taxes originally assessed against the Shoreham plant" (Public Authorities Law § 1020-q [3]) has been held unconstitutional as applied to LILCO, there is no statutory prohibition against the maintenance by LILCO of any tax certiorari proceeding with respect to the Shoreham property.

Public Authorities - Long Island Power Authority - Acquisition and Closure of Shoreham Nuclear Plant - Calculation of Payments in Lieu of Taxes - Duration of Payments In an action to declare the obligations of the Long Island Power Authority to make payments in lieu of taxes (PILOTS) under Public Authorities Law § 1020 as part of its acquisition of the Shoreham nuclear power plant from the Long Island Lighting Company, Supreme Court correctly concluded that LIPA's PILOT obligation is "perpetual" in an amount equal to the "taxes and assessments which would have been levied on the plant in a nonoperative state" since the statute does not limit the duration of PILOTS. Furthermore, Supreme Court properly determined that the term "nonoperative" means "incapable of generating electricity without regard to the Shoreham plant's state of construction". Defining "nonoperative" to require LIPA to make perpetual PILOTS based on the value of a fully constructed nuclear plant on the Shoreham site would be contrary to the legislative intent of the Act.

DiMiero v. Livingston-Steuben-Wyoming County Bd. of Coop. Educ. Servs.


68021


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


199 A.D.2d 875; 606 N.Y.S.2d 92; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12458



December 30, 1993, Decided  
December 30, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer, the County Board of Cooperative Educational Services, appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which denied the employer's motion for summary judgment or, alternatively, for conversion of the action to a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, in plaintiff therapists' action for a declaration that they were entitled to be enrolled in the New York State Teachers' Retirement System (retirement system).


OVERVIEW: The therapists alleged that they were entitled to membership in the retirement system as members of the "teaching and supervisory staff" of their school district as defined in N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 35(g), "teachers or instructors" as defined in N.Y. Retirement and Social Security Law § 40(b)(1)(e), and members of the "teaching or professional staff" as defined in N.Y. Educ. Law § 501(4). On appeal from the supreme court's denial of the employer's motion for summary judgment, the court concluded that because the therapists sought only to challenge determinations regarding their employment benefits, N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 review was proper. Accordingly, the court found that the supreme court erred in failing to convert the action to an art. 78 proceeding to which the four-month Statute of Limitations contained in N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 217 applied. The court therefore reversed the judgment, finding that the employer was entitled to summary judgment because the therapists' commencement of the action was untimely, the therapists having failed to file an action within four months after they were aggrieved by being notified that their membership in the retirement system was erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the supreme court denying the employer's motion for summary judgment, granted the motion, ordered the proceeding to be converted from a declaratory action to an administrative appeal, and dismissed the therapists' petition.

 

378 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Alfonso v. Fernandez


92-06950


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


195 A.D.2d 46; 606 N.Y.S.2d 259; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12416


January 25, 1993, Argued  
December 30, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised February 25, 1994. As Revised March 2, 1994. As Revised March 10, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents brought a hybrid proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 against respondent New York City public schools and others, to prohibit a condom availability component of an HIV/AIDS education program, and an action for judgment declaring that the condom distribution component was violative of their constitutional rights. The Supreme Court of New York, Richmond County, dismissed the action. The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The New York City school program was designed to educate students about and prevent the spread of HIV/AIDS. Part of the program called for high schools to make condoms available to students who requested them and did not include a provision for parental consent or "opt out." The court held that the condom availability component of the program: (1) was contrary to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2504 and common law because it constituted "health services" to unemancipated minor children without the consent of their parents or guardians; (2) violated the parents' due process rights to direct the upbringing of their children because the policy was not essential to serving the compelling governmental interest in that minors could freely obtain condoms elsewhere; and (3) did not violate the Free Exercise Clause under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 3 because it did not prohibit the parents and/or their children from practicing their religion, or directly or indirectly coerce them to engage in conduct or practices contrary to their religious beliefs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, finding the condom distribution program violative of statutory law, common law, and the Fourteenth Amendment. The court prohibited the schools from dispensing condoms to unemancipated minor students without the prior consent of their parents or guardians, or without an opt-out provision.


HEADNOTES

Schools - Students - Validity of Condom Availability Program - Parental Consent Respondents are prohibited from dispensing condoms to unemancipated minor students without the prior consent of their parents or guardians, or without an opt-out provision, as part of the expanded HIV/AIDS education program in New York City's public high schools. Whether the condoms are distributed as an adjunct of a plan of instruction on HIV/AIDS or through school health offices is of no import since the supplying of condoms is conduct which constitutes a health service separate and apart from health education (see, Public Health Law § 2504; 8 NYCRR 136.1 [d]) and thus, in the absence of a provision requiring the prior consent of unemancipated minor students' parents or guardians, or in the absence of an opt-out provision, lacks common-law or statutory authority. The primary purpose of the Board of Education is not to serve as a health provider. No judicial or legislative authority directs or permits teachers and other public school educators to dispense condoms to minor, unemancipated students without the knowledge or consent of their parents. Thus, the regulations of the State Education Department which authorize condom distribution without prior parental consent or opt out (8 NYCRR 135.3 [c] [2] [ii]) are contrary to the common law and are of no effect. Furthermore, requiring parental consent or opt out for the condom availability component of respondents' program would not violate State and Federal statutory and constitutional law regarding the provision of family planning services to unemancipated minors under the Social Security Act and Public Health Service Act nor would it stymie every health care provider by compelling parental consent whenever an unemancipated minor seeks contraceptive services.

Schools - Students - Constitutionality of Condom Availability Program - Parental Consent Respondents' plan to dispense condoms to unemancipated minor children without the consent of their parents or guardians, or an opt-out provision, as part of an expanded HIV/AIDS education program in New York City's public high schools (see, 8 NYCRR 135.3 [c] [2] [ii]) violates the constitutional substantive due process rights of the parent petitioners and similarly situated parents or guardians to direct the upbringing of their children. By excluding parental involvement, the condom availability component of respondents' program impermissibly trespasses on petitioners' parental rights by substituting respondents in loco parentis, without a compelling necessity therefor. Intrusion into the relationship between parent and child requires a showing of an overriding necessity and while the State has a compelling interest in controlling AIDS, interference in petitioners' rights is not necessary to meet this public health threat, especially since there are publicly funded nonschool programs where condoms are available to minors as part of confidential family planning, as provided under the Social Security Act and Public Health Service Act. The distribution of condoms can go forward without interfering with petitioners' rights simply by allowing parents who are interested in providing appropriate guidance and discipline to their children to "opt out" by instructing the school not to distribute to their children without their consent.

Schools - Students - Constitutionality of Condom Availability Program - Freedom of Religion Respondents' plan to dispense condoms to unemancipated minor children without the consent of their parents or guardians, or an opt-out provision, as part of an expanded HIV/AIDS education program in New York City's public high schools (see, 8 NYCRR 135.3 [c] [2] [ii]) does not violate the rights of the petitioning parents and/or their children to the free exercise of their religion. Nor does it directly or indirectly coerce them to engage in conduct or practices which are contrary to their religious beliefs. No student who fails or refuses to participate is visited with a sanction. Merely because petitioners find the program objectionable does not render it violative of their right to the free exercise of their religion.

Wood v. Irving


1456


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


199 A.D.2d 1026; 605 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12640



December 29, 1993, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City challenge a judgment from Supreme Court, in Monroe County (New York), which granted petitioner officer's motion that sought appointment as a detective and commensurate compensation.


OVERVIEW: The officer claimed that the trial court properly granted the petition. The officer established that he had been permanently appointed as a police officer and had been assigned detective duties for a period exceeding 18 months. The City contended that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 58(4)(c) was unconstitutional because it provided for promotion without a competitive examination. The court did not agree. Every legislative enactment carried a strong presumption of constitutionality including a rebuttable presumption of the existence of necessary factual support for its provisions. Further, testing for the position of detective, after the officer had served in that capacity for a period in excess of 18 months, was not practicable, and that the capacity and fitness of a police officer to serve as a detective could have been better assessed by a review of the officer's extended period of on-the-job performance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that granted the officer's motion that sought appointment as a detective and commensurate compensation.

Omni Group Farms v. County of Cayuga


1465


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT

December 29, 1993, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff discrimination claimants appealed an order from the Supreme Court, Cayuga County (New York), which denied the claimants' motion for leave to amend their complaint against defendant county to assert three causes of action for violation of their civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: Discrimination claimants filed a motion to amend their complaint against the county to add three claims for violation of their civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The lower court denied the motion. On appeal, the court modified to allow two of the proposed claims. The court found that the original complaint did not put the county on notice of the factual allegations set forth in one of the claims asserted in the proposed amended complaint. The court held that the claim could not be allowed because the county would be surprised and prejudiced by assertion of the entirely new claim and the new claim was time-barred. However, the court held that the original complaint gave notice of the same transactions or occurrences asserted in the remaining two claims in the proposed amendment, and assertion of these claims under the new theory of an alleged violation of federal civil rights was not time-barred.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the lower court to allow amendment of the discrimination claimants' complaint against the county by adding two of the three proposed additional claims.

Susan G. v. Bane


68555


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


199 A.D.2d 804; 605 N.Y.S.2d 522; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12230



December 23, 1993, Decided  
December 23, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review a determination of respondent Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services, which denied her application to expunge a child maltreatment report maintained in the New York Statewide Central Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment.


OVERVIEW: Child protective services determined that a report to the New York State Central Register of Child Abuse and Maltreatment was "indicated," under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 424(7). The mother requested that the report be expunged from the Central Register, and when her request was denied, she requested a hearing pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 422(8)(a). The Department refused to order expungement, and the mother commenced a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, alleging that the Department's determination was not supported by substantial evidence, and that she was denied due process of law. The court affirmed the determination, holding that the administrative law judge was justified in concluding that there was some credible evidence that the mother had kicked her son intentionally and that the reported incident was relevant to the future provision of child care. Furthermore, the court held that the mere presence of the report on the Central Register, and its possible dissemination to one or more prospective employers, did not, without more, deprive the mother of a liberty or property interest. The standard of proof required by N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 424-a(2)(d) was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determination of the Commissioner which denied the mother's application to expunge a child maltreatment report.

 

382 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Jetro Cash & Carry Enters. v. Department of Taxation & Fin.


67998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


194 A.D.2d 171; 605 N.Y.S.2d 538; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12232; 1993-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P70,476



December 23, 1993, Decided  
December 23, 1993, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended January 21, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wholesaler appealed the summary judgment granted by the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) in favor of defendant Department of Taxation and Finance (department), in the wholesaler's action for a declaratory judgment that the Cigarette Marketing Standards Act, N.Y. Tax Law § 20A, et seq., was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The wholesaler was a licensed cigarette stamping agent and a reseller of cigarettes. The legislature enacted the Cigarette Marketing Standards Act, N.Y. Tax Law § 20A, et seq., which established price floors below which a wholesaler could not sell cigarettes. The wholesaler sought a declaratory judgment that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated the Supremacy clause by violating the Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1, et seq. The court held that state action was immune form antitrust laws if the state articulated a clear policy to allow anti-competitive conduct and the state actively supervised the anti-competitive conduct by private entities. The court held that the state had a clear and affirmative policy to protect and stabilize the cigarette industry within the state. The court held that the statute, which limited the minimum price to basic costs plus the cost of doing business, did not control the actual price because that was set by the manufacturers. The court held that the price could not be manipulated by concessions or rebates. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's summary judgment in favor of the department.


HEADNOTES

Monopolies - Price Discrimination - Cigarette Marketing Standards Act Immune from Federal Sherman Antitrust Act by Reason of "State Action" Doctrine The Cigarette Marketing Standards Act (CMSA) (Tax Law art 20-A), which regulates the resale of cigarettes by establishing price floors below which an agent, wholesaler or dealer may not sell cigarettes, is immune from the Federal Sherman Antitrust Act (15 USC § 1-7), and thus does not constitute an unlawful resale price fixing scheme, by reason of the "State action" doctrine in that the CMSA articulates a clear and affirmative policy to allow anticompetitive conduct and the State provides "active supervision" of anticompetitive conduct undertaken by private entities. The details of the resale prices have been established as a product of deliberate State intervention and the anticompetitive scheme is that of New York, not private parties. Once the manufacturer establishes its price, the statutory scheme defines the price at which cigarettes may be resold, and it is thus the statute, not individual parties, that determines the ultimate resale price. Additionally, by structuring a detailed mechanism for determining the resale price, the State has satisfied the "active supervision" requirement of the "State action" doctrine. Furthermore, the CMSA's exclusion of rebates and concessions in setting the minimum resale price does not allow private parties to manipulate the resale price of cigarettes in violation of the statutory scheme. Rebates and concessions, which are excluded from "trade discounts" (see, 20 NYCRR 340.2 [a] [2] [iii]), have a legitimate business purpose and are lawful so long as they do not "directly or indirectly serve to reduce the price below that at which cigarettes can be lawfully sold or purchased" in this State (20 NYCRR 344.1 [b] [2]). While those customers who receive a rebate or concession may make a greater profit on the transaction, the CMSA does not enable those same customers to engage in predatory pricing by undercutting their competitors. Moreover, any manufacturer or purchaser which seeks to designate a rebate or a concession as a "trade discount" for the purpose of circumventing the CMSA is subject to both civil and criminal sanctions (see, Tax Law § 484 [a] [5]; § 1829).

Administrative Law - Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies - Constitutionality of Cigarette Marketing Standards Act The Cigarette Marketing Standards Act (CMSA) (Tax Law art 20-A), which regulates the resale of cigarettes by establishing price floors below which an agent, wholesaler or dealer may not sell cigarettes, cannot be declared unconstitutional as applied to plaintiff, a licensed cigarette stamping agent and wholesale reseller of cigarettes, by reason of the refusal of defendant Department of Taxation and Finance to permit plaintiff' to apply a lesser markup percentage than that presumed by the statute since plaintiff has failed to exhaust its administrative remedies, thereby preventing it from litigating the issue in a court of law.

 

383 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Board of Educ. v. New York State Teachers' Retirement Sys.


68423


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


199 A.D.2d 760; 605 N.Y.S.2d 432; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11971



December 16, 1993, Decided  
December 16, 1993, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended May 6, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teachers appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which denied their motion for summary judgment declaring 1990 Laws 175, § 1, to have been unconstitutional and granted the cross motion of defendants, New York State Teachers' Retirement System, New York State Comptroller, and the Retirement Board of the New York State Teachers' Retirement System, for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The state legislature reduced state aid to school districts and by § 1 enacted N.Y. Educ. Law § 521(2)(i), which deferred the school districts' 1989-1990 fiscal year contribution to the system and provided that it be paid in 15 equal installments at an 8 percent annual interest rate. The teachers brought an action seeking a declaration that 1990 Laws 175, § 1 was unconstitutional in that it violated the nonimpairment clause of the N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7. The teachers filed a motion for summary judgment and defendants filed a cross motion for summary judgment. The trial court denied the teachers' motion and granted defendants' motion. On appeal, the court modified the judgment of the trial court by declaring that the contested statute was not invalid and otherwise affirmed the judgment. The court found that the deferral provision constituted a loan. However, the court found that the loan was not imposed on the board by the legislature because 1990 Laws 175, § 1 expressly provided that the board had the option to either accept the deferral plan by adopting the valuation rate of interest or to veto it by refusing to do so.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court by declaring that the contested statute concerning deferral of the school districts' contribution to the system was not invalid and otherwise affirmed the judgment, which denied the teachers' motion for summary judgment and granted defendants' cross motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint.

Childs v. Bane


68485


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


194 A.D.2d 221; 605 N.Y.S.2d 488; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11991



December 16, 1993, Decided  
December 16, 1993, Entered



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order and judgment is modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as denied standing to plaintiff Niagara Mohawk Power Corporation and declared that 18 NYCRR 352.5 (d) and Administrative Directive 92 ADM-26 are valid; it is declared that (1) said regulation is invalid to the extent it requires standard of need to be determined upon gross income rather than income and excludes the costs of meals for persons unable to prepare meals at home from the determination of standard of need, and (2) said directive is invalid to the extent that it permits the garnishment of the wages of the spouse of a recipient; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, recipient, utility, and others, appealed an order and judgment from the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York) denying plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment, granting a cross-motion by defendant Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services for summary judgment, and declaring that N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 352.5(d) and an administrative directive were valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs challenged the amendment of § 352.5(d) and the accompanying administrative directive. The legislature directed the promulgation of those provisions after amending N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-s to require recipients faced with utility shut-off whose income exceeded the public assistance standard of need to repay this assistance with utility bills in one year. The court affirmed, subject to modifications, the order and judgment that denied plaintiffs' motion and granted the commissioner's cross-motion. The court reversed the decision denying standing to the utility. The court reversed the declarations that N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 352.5(d) and the directive were valid pursuant to the legislative directive in 1992 N.Y. Laws 42, § 128. The court declared that N.Y. Comp. Code R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 352.5(d) was invalid to the degree that it considered standard of need based on gross income instead of income and did not consider in the determination of standard of need the costs of meals for those not able to prepare meals at home. The court declared the directive invalid to the degree that it allowed the garnishing of wages of the spouse of a recipient.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with modifications, the order and judgment of the supreme court challenged by plaintiffs, which denied their summary judgment motion and granted the commissioner's cross-motion. The modifications reversed the denial of standing to the utility and the declarations that the statute and the directive were both entirely valid. The court declared the statute invalid to a degree. The court declared the directive invalid to a degree.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Standing of Public Utility to Challenge Regulation Regarding Public Assistance for Utility Service Plaintiff public utility has standing as a taxpayer under State Finance Law § 123-b to maintain an action to declare invalid an emergency regulatory amendment (18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) and administrative directive of the Department of Social Services, which, in implementing the directive of Social Services Law § 131-s (as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128), require that persons seeking assistance to prevent a shut-off of utility service, who are not otherwise eligible to receive public assistance, sign an agreement to repay the utility assistance within one year as a condition to receiving such assistance. Although the promulgation of the amended regulation and the administrative directive did not involve the expenditure of State funds, their implementation will and, inasmuch as plaintiff alleges that the expenditure of these funds will result in an unconstitutional disbursement and misapplication of State funds, it has standing to maintain this action.

Social Services - Emergency Assistance - Payments Made for Utility Service - Validity of Regulation Requiring Applicants to Sign Repayment Agreements An emergency regulatory amendment (18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) of the Department of Social Services, which, in implementing the directive of Social Services Law § 131-s (as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128), requires that persons seeking assistance to prevent a shut-off of utility service, who are not otherwise eligible to receive public assistance, sign an agreement to repay the utility assistance within one year as a condition to receiving such assistance, is not violative of the State's constitutional obligation to provide aid to the needy (NY Const, art XVII, § 1). While the State may not refuse aid to the needy, it has discretion in determining the amount of aid, in classifying recipients and in defining the term "needy". Here, the denial of benefits only applies to persons who do not fall within the statutory definition of needy because their household income exceeds the public assistance standard of need and, therefore, are not eligible to receive public assistance. Furthermore, a person whose income diminishes to the public assistance standard may apply for and receive utility assistance even though such person may have defaulted upon a previously executed repayment agreement. In addition, the amended regulation does not deny plaintiffs equal protection of the law since the authorizing statute and the regulation further the State's legitimate goal of allocating limited public assistance resources to the neediest applicants.

Social Services - Emergency Assistance - Payments Made for Utility Service - Validity of Regulation Requiring Applicants to Sign Repayment Agreements An emergency regulatory amendment (18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) of the Department of Social Services, which, in implementing the directive of Social Services Law § 131-s (as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128), requires that persons seeking assistance to prevent a shut-off of utility service, who are not otherwise eligible to receive public assistance, sign an agreement to repay the utility assistance within one year as a condition to receiving such assistance, and which further denies any subsequent assistance to persons who have not complied with a repayment agreement, does not create eligibility requirements and restrictions that exceed the statutory authority in violation of the fundamental principle of administrative law that agencies can only exercise those powers expressly delegated by the Legislature, together with those required by necessary implication. The Commissioner's power to deny assistance is derived by implication from the statutory requirement which conditions the receipt of assistance upon the signing of a repayment agreement. Without the power to deny assistance, the repayment agreement would be rendered essentially meaningless as it could be ignored with impunity because the failure to abide by it would have no immediate consequences.

Social Services - Emergency Assistance - Payments Made for Utility Service - Validity of Regulation Requiring Applicants to Sign Repayment Agreements An emergency regulatory amendment (18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) of the Department of Social Services, which, in implementing the directive of Social Services Law § 131-s (as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128), requires that persons seeking assistance to prevent a shut-off of utility service, who are not otherwise eligible to receive public assistance, sign an agreement to repay the utility assistance within one year as a condition to receiving such assistance, is not invalid for defining standard of need in a way that will expand the number of applicants required to sign repayment agreements by defining a "household" as consisting of all persons who occupy a housing unit, including related family members and all unrelated persons, and eliminating from consideration certain special nonrecurring items of need (see, 18 NYCRR 352.1 [c]), since the Legislature clearly envisioned a narrowing of the eligibility standards as a means of achieving savings. However, since the additional costs of meals for persons unable to prepare meals at home is a nonrecurring item that must by statute be included in the determination of standard of need (Social Services Law § 131-a [2] [b]), the amended regulation is invalid to the extent that it excludes those additional costs from the definition of standard of need. Furthermore, since the authorizing statute only requires persons whose household income exceeds the standard of need to sign repayment agreements, indicating a legislative intent to use income and not gross income in determining eligibility (cf., Social Services Law § 131-w), the regulation is also invalid to the extent that it requires standard of need to be determined upon gross income rather than income.

Social Services - Emergency Assistance - Payments Made for Utility Service - Enforcement of Repayment Agreements An administrative directive issued by the Department of Social Services containing the mechanisms for enforcing repayment agreements certain applicants for emergency utility assistance are required to sign as a condition to receiving assistance (Social Services Law § 131-s, as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128; 18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) is invalid to the extent that it permits the garnishment of the wages of the spouse of a recipient in violation of CPLR 5231. Unless the spouse has signed the repayment agreement, he or she cannot be considered a debtor because a debtor-creditor relationship would not have been created between the person and the social services department. Nor can such spouse be considered a "garnishee" because a spouse's wages are not a debt owed to the recipient nor are they property in which the recipient has an interest ( CPLR 105 [i]).

Administrative Law - Rule Making - Publication in State Register An administrative directive issued by the Department of Social Services containing the mechanisms for enforcing repayment agreements certain applicants for emergency utility assistance are required to sign as a condition to receiving assistance (Social Services Law § 131-s, as amended by L 1992, ch 41, § 128; 18 NYCRR 352.5 [d]) and the repayment agreement are not required to be published in the State Register since they constitute forms and instructions, interpretive statements and statements of general policy that are merely explanatory and have no legal effect (State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2] [b] [iv]).

Vogt v. Crosson


67878


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


199 A.D.2d 722; 606 N.Y.S.2d 57; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12014



December 16, 1993, Decided  
December 16, 1993, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended May 6, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the administrator for the courts, the state comptroller, and State, sought review from an order of the Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), which granted respondent county judge's motion for summary judgment and made a declaration in his favor concerning a salary increase based on salaries paid to judges in other counties.


OVERVIEW: The judge brought an action seeking a declaration that statutory provisions establishing disparate salaries among county judges were unconstitutional and in violation of his right to equal protection in that he received a lower salary than the county judges in other counties. The judge also requested payment at the same rate as a county judge in the highest paid county and resulting unpaid salary. The judge filed a motion for summary judgment, supported by an affidavit from an economics expert. The state comptroller and State cross-moved for summary judgment to dismiss the complaint, asserting that geographic and cost-of-living variables provided a rational basis for the salary differences. The judge's summary judgment motion was granted. On review, the court affirmed that portion of the judgment finding that there was sufficient proof for the judge's assertions and no rational basis for the geographical distinctions in salaries. A territorial distinction which had no rational basis would not support a state statute. However, the court modified that portion of the judgment in which the judge sought a salary equal to that given to a county judge of a specific county.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that portion of the judgment which granted summary judgment in favor of the judge and held that the judge was entitled to the same salary as county judges in five other counties and awarded him pack pay. The court modified that portion of the judgment by reversing that portion which held that the judge was entitled to pay at the same salary as a certain other county judge.

In re Robert Z.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent father appealed the order of the Family Court, New York County (New York), which denied his application to stay the adoption proceeding and granted petitioner uncle's application to adopt the child.


OVERVIEW: The child was born out of wedlock. The father was listed on the birth certificate and the mother admitted the paternity in an affidavit. Custody of the child was placed with the uncle of the mother, and the uncle sought to adopt the child. The father contested the trial court's rulings that he had no standing to contest the adoption, had not established that he would prevail in an abandonment proceeding, could not prevail in a "best interests" proceeding, and did not have a meritorious case. The court reversed the order that denied a stay and granted adoption of the child. The court found that under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111-a(2), the father had a right to notice of the adoption, but that the facts regarding whether consent to the adoption was required under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(d) were disputed. Because of the factual disputes and the allegation of the father that he was prevented from maintaining contact with the child, the court concluded that a hearing on the merits was necessary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied the father a stay of the adoption proceeding and granted the uncle's application to adopt the child. The court remanded for a hearing.

Incorporated Village of Northport v. Town of Huntington

OVERVIEW: The village objected to fees imposed under Huntington, N.Y. People v. Mastropietro


92-02955


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


198 A.D.2d 443; 604 N.Y.S.2d 149; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10945


November 1, 1993, Argued  
November 22, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised March 25, 1994.
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Mastropietro v. Bradt, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 145424 (S.D.N.Y., July 9, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a conviction for burglary in the second degree, a class C felony, both defendant and the People appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which found him to be a persistent violent offender but ruled that the statutory minimum sentence for such offenders contained in N.Y. Penal Law § 70.08(3)(b) was unconstitutional as applied to him. Defendant denied his classification as a persistent violent offender.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had twice been convicted of class D violent felonies, was convicted of burglary in the second degree and sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender under § 70.08(3)(b). Both he and the People appealed. Defendant claimed that his prior convictions were unconstitutionally obtained and that § 70.08(3)(b) was unconstitutional as applied to him. The court dismissed his appeal as abandoned, it reversed the sentence, and it vacated the trial court's judgment that the statute was unconstitutional as applied to him. Defendant failed to sustain his burden of showing that his prior convictions were unconstitutionally obtained because he was advised of his rights and freely admitted the earlier crimes. The court also ruled that the legislature's decision to classify all burglaries of a dwelling as a class C violent felony was entitled to great deference. Further, defendant had a criminal record and committed the instant offense while he was on parole. The instant offense, therefore, was no an isolated event. The court determined that his self-induced drug problem was not so extraordinary as to warrant a finding that the sentence constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: In the appeal by defendant and the People after defendant's sentencing as a persistent violent felony offender, the court dismissed defendant's appeal as abandoned and reversed the sentence on the law. The court also vacated the trial court's judgment that the statute was unconstitutional as applied to defendant, and it remitted the matter to the trial court for resentencing under the persistent violent felony offender statute.

In re Matthews


93-05578


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


198 A.D.2d 280; 603 N.Y.S.2d 534; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10359


October 12, 1993, Argued  
November 8, 1993, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, county Democratic Party officials and a former county Commissioner of Elections from the party, challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which dismissed an action to compel respondent state and county officials to file a certificate appointing the former Commissioner as Commissioner of Elections. The action also sought a declaration that N.Y. Elec. Law § 3-204 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The party officials and former county Commissioner of Elections from the Democratic Party instituted the declaratory judgment action, asserting that N.Y. Elec. Law § 3-204(4) was impermissibly vague and violated the state and federal Constitutions by empowering the county board of supervisors to interfere with the party's internal affairs. After the former county Commissioner re-nominated himself for reelection, two county supervisors voted their combined 37 votes against his re-nomination, causing him not to receive the statutorily mandated 65 weighted votes. The Republican Party members of the board of supervisors abstained. The trial court held that the contention that insufficient negative votes were cast was without foundation in law and was based on faulty reasoning. The court agreed and affirmed the judgment because the import of the statutory language was that the board of supervisors, in failing to affirm the nomination, "acted" on the resolution by rejecting it. As to the statute's constitutionality, the court agreed that a challenge of its constitutionality was premature because the procedures outlined in the challenged statute had not yet been exhausted.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the hybrid proceeding and action was affirmed without prejudice to renewal.

Marcus v. Levin


91-09373


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


198 A.D.2d 214; 603 N.Y.S.2d 323; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10210


October 12, 1993, Argued  
November 1, 1993, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voter appealed an order and judgment of the Kings County Supreme Court (New York), which denied his motion for summary judgment, granted the cross-application of defendant officials for summary judgment, and declared N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 506 and 507 constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The voter filed a declaratory relief action against the officials and requested a declaration that N.Y. Jud. Law §§ 506 and 507 were unconstitutional. The voter argued that registering to vote increased the chances of being selected for jury duty thereby chilling the right to vote. The voter also argued that jurors suffered economic losses. The trial court granted the officials' motion for summary judgment, denied the voter's motion for summary judgment, and ruled that §§ 506 and 507 were constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court concluded that §§506 and 507 were constitutional, even under an equal protection analysis, because they promoted a compelling state interest. The court determined that the state had long recognized the significance of jury trials and the importance of ensuring that jurors were drawn from as broad a cross-section of society as possible. The court ruled that even if jury duty imposed an economic burden on some individuals, that burden paled in comparison to each citizen's duty to serve as a juror when called.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against the voter.

Lane v. Town of Oyster Bay


91-06271


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


197 A.D.2d 690; 603 N.Y.S.2d 53; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9959


September 30, 1993, Argued  
October 25, 1993, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which granted respondent landowners' petition and set aside a referendum result regarding the extension of the town's sanitation collection district.


OVERVIEW: The town conducted a permissive referendum on a proposition to extend its sanitation collection district into a nearby hamlet. The proposition was passed by a majority of the voters. The landowners sought to have the referendum set aside because the town failed to comply with N.Y. Town Law § 209-e(3). The trial court found in favor of the landowners, and the town appealed. The court found that despite the provisions of § 209-e(3), the referendum was not limited to qualified electors who owned taxable real property. The court also found that the limitations on the franchise contained in § 209-e(3) were constitutional as they related to the subject sanitation collection district and, thus, should have been complied with at the referendum. The court held that the votes cast by non-landowners contaminated the election result, making it impossible to determine whether a majority of those properly entitled to vote approved the proposition. Thus, the result of the referendum was properly set aside.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the landowners.

Schulz v. State


69012


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


193 A.D.2d 171; 606 N.Y.S.2d 916; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9836



October 21, 1993, Decided  
October 21, 1993, Entered



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the judgment is affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, taxpayers and registered voters, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which granted summary judgment for respondent state in the taxpayers' and registered voters' declaratory judgment action that the funding for an improvement plan of the state's infrastructure was null and void.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted an act for the improvement of the state's infrastructure and provided for the funding of the improvements from the proceeds of bonds issued by state entities. The taxpayers and registered voters filed a declaratory judgment action for a determination that the act was null and void pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. VII, §§ 8, 11 and N.Y. Const. art. X, § 5. The court granted summary judgment for the state. The taxpayers and registered voters appealed. The court held that: (1) the taxpayers and registered voters could not claim standing under N.Y. State Fin. Law § 123-b because they challenged the legislature's authorization of bond issues; (2) laches barred the challenge to the improvements and bond finance provisions of the act because funds were expended toward the improvements; and (3) the debt incurred under the act was not a legal debt of the state that required a referendum under N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11 because the state had no legal obligation to the bondholders in the event of default.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment for the state in the taxpayers' and registered voters' declaratory judgment action that challenged the funding for the state's improvement plan was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties - Standing - Taxpayers' Action Challenging Constitutionality of Debt-Incurring Legislation - Statutory Bond Authorization for Funding Local Highway and Bridge Improvement Programs Although plaintiffs have standing as registered voters to challenge, as an alleged violation of the constitutional referendum requirement for incurring State debt (NY Const, art VII, § 11), the statutory financing mechanisms, whereby two public benefit corporations, the Metropolitan Transportation Authority and the Thruway Authority, are authorized to issue moral obligation bonds, which expressly are not to be debts of the State, for the financing of mass transportation, bridge and highway projects from dedicated funds, backed by revenue from monies collected from State-wide taxes and fees, and subject to an annual appropriation by the Legislature (L 1993, ch 56), they lack standing to challenge the legislation as violative of either the constitutional prohibition against giving or loaning the State's credit in aid of a public corporation or private enterprise (NY Const, art VII, § 8) or the constitutional prohibition against the State assuming liability for the payment of obligations issued by a public corporation (NY Const, art X, § 5). Since plaintiffs are challenging the Legislature's authorization of bond issues, they cannot claim statutory standing pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b. Furthermore, they also have no common-law standing as taxpayers to proceed with their nonvoter constitutional challenges in this situation. Moreover, while voters have separate and independent standing to challenge financing schemes which are claimed to be subject to referendum approval, neither NY Constitution, article VII, § 8 nor article X, § 5 are linked to any voting rights.

Equity - Laches - Taxpayers' Action Challenging Constitutionality of Debt-Incurring Legislation - Statutory Bond Authorization for Funding Local Highway and Bridge Improvement Programs Plaintiffs taxpayers' action commenced in 1993 challenging the constitutionality of those parts of Laws of 1993 (ch 56) which authorize additional bonding for local highway and bridge improvement programs pursuant to the original 1991 statutory financing mechanisms for funding local highway and bridge bonds (L 1991, ch 329, § 11) as being violative of the proscription against the State contracting debt without voter approval (NY Const, art VII, § 11), is barred by laches. Plaintiffs did not commence their action until almost two years after the legislation was enacted creating the challenged financing scheme and the municipalities, in reliance on the issuance of bonds by the Thruway Authority to fund various programs pursuant to the 1991 legislation, have expended funds and committed themselves to future programs with the expectation that they would be reimbursed. To abort these programs at this point would create severe fiscal problems and cause undue disruption and prejudice.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Statutory Bond Authorization for Funding Local Highway and Bridge Improvement Programs The statutory financing mechanisms, whereby two public benefit corporations, the Metropolitan Transportation Authority and the Thruway Authority, are authorized to issue moral obligation bonds, which expressly are not to be debts of the State, for the financing of mass transportation, bridge and highway projects from dedicated funds, backed by revenue from monies collected from State-wide taxes and fees, and subject to an annual appropriation by the Legislature (L 1993, ch 56), are not violative of the constitutional referendum requirement for incurring State debt (NY Const, art VII, § 11) since moral obligation debt does not constitute State debt within the meaning of the constitutional provision. In order to constitute debt within the meaning of the State and local finance articles of the New York Constitution, the State or locality must legally be obligated to bondholders in the event of default. Here, the State clearly has no legal liability either on the Thruway Authority's State highway and bridge bonds or the railway and aviation bonds. Both are expressly denominated to be obligations of the Thruway Authority and are backed solely by Thruway Authority revenue streams, not by the full faith and credit of the State. In addition, there is no constitutional violation attendant to the fact that the revenue streams backing up the Thruway Authority bonds derive from State appropriations since the State can constitutionally give or lend money to assist a municipal or other public corporation in carrying out a public purpose.

Suffolk County Democratic Comm. v. Gaffney


93-04146


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


196 A.D.2d 799; 601 N.Y.S.2d 935; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8443


September 9, 1993, Argued  
September 10, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised September 13, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action arising out of respondent county's reapportionment plant, appellants, political committee and citizen, challenged an order of the Supreme Court (New York), which granted respondent county's motion to dismiss the political committee for lack of standing, dismissed the citizen's action for violation of the N.Y. Mun. Home Rule § 10(1)(a)(13), the Voting Rights Act, the Civil Rights Act, and denied a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: In affirming the judgment, the court held that the political committee, which acted on its own behalf and not on behalf of its members lacked standing because: 1) it failed to establish that it would suffer any threatened or actual harm as a result of reapportionment; 2) the Voting Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973 only applied to persons whose right to vote was abridged or denied as a result of their race or color, not political association; and 3) it was not a member of the protected class under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1985. In affirming the dismissal of the citizen's action, the court held that: 1) the Municipal Home Rule Law did not apply, as the county operated under a charter form of government; 2) the claim for violation of the Civil Rights Act failed to allege the that the reapportionment plan was instituted pursuant to an "official policy or custom;" and 3) the citizen's failed to show that they were likely to succeed on the merits of their equal protection claim, even though the reapportionment plan was constitutionally suspect. In so ruling, the court noted that the county was given great discretion in creating legislative districts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the claim against the county with respect to the county's' reapportionment plan brought by a political committee and citizen, and affirmed the denial of a preliminary injunction with respect to an equal protection claim.

 

397 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Town of Islip v. Paliotti


91-03420, 91-07019


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


196 A.D.2d 648; 601 N.Y.S.2d 926; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8310


January 22, 1993, Submitted  
August 30, 1993, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by plaintiff town to permanently enjoin defendant billboard owners from maintaining billboards, the billboard owners sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which granted the town's motion for a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: The billboard owners maintained two billboards. The town amended its zoning ordinance to essentially prohibit the maintenance of commercial billboards that advertised a product, service, or establishment that was not the principal product, service, or establishment found on the property. The amendments contained an amortization clause. However, they were struck down in their entirety because certain inseverable provisions were unconstitutional. The town again amended its ordinance to correct the constitutional infirmities but failed to include an amortization clause. The supreme court granted the town's motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin the continued maintenance of the billboard owners' billboards. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that, in the absence of a clause that provided for a reasonable amortization period, the town could not rely upon the amendments to immediately terminate the billboard owners' nonconforming uses. Accordingly, the court held that the town was not entitled to injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the supreme court, which granted a preliminary injunction of favor of the town to enjoin the billboard owners' continued maintenance of their billboards.
