Santangelo v. State


91-03344


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


193 A.D.2d 25; 601 N.Y.S.2d 305; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8046


February 9, 1993, Argued  
August 9, 1993, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered that the order is reversed, on the law, with costs, the motion is denied, the cross motions for partial summary judgment on the issue of liability are granted, and the matter is remitted to the Court of Claims for a determination on the issue of damages.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officers challenged the decisions of the Court of Claims (New York), which dismissed appellants' claims and found that the amendment allowing retroactive effect of a statute allowing police officers to recover for injuries sustained in the line of duties was unconstitutional to the extent that it retroactively applied to revive appellants' claims when final judgment had been obtained.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officers were injured when they attempted to apprehend a patient who had escaped from a mental institution. The patient had been listed as discharged on hospital records when he remained at large more than 30 days after his escape. Appellants' original suit was dismissed because recovery was against public policy. Subsequently, the legislature created a right of action for police officers injured in the line of duty and amended the statute to be applied retroactively. Appellants renewed their claims. The court of claims dismissed appellants' claims and found that the amendment was unconstitutional to the extent that it retroactively applied to revive appellants' claims when a final judgment had been obtained. The court reversed. It found that the validity of a statute was dependent on an existing moral obligation. The court noted that it was determined when the claims were previously presented that the injuries were proximately caused by respondent state's violation of its own regulations in marking the patient discharged. As such, appellants were entitled to recover and their motions for partial summary judgment on the issue of liability were granted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment dismissing appellant police officers' claims for recovery. The statutory amendment allowing retroactive effect of a statute was constitutional and appellants were entitled to recover, even though final judgment on their claims had been obtained.


HEADNOTES

State - Claim against State - Injuries Sustained by Police Officers while Attempting to Apprehend Escaped Mental Patient - Revival of Previously Dismissed Claims The Court of Claims erred when it declared, sua sponte, that General Municipal Law § 205-e (as amended by L 1990, ch 762), which revived certain otherwise time-barred claims of police officers who were injured in the line of duty as a direct or indirect result of a person's violation of a rule, law, statute or ordinance, was unconstitutional to the extent it could be retroactively applied to revive a previously dismissed action against the State by two police officers injured in 1979 while attempting to apprehend a dangerous escaped mental patient from a State facility. The instant claims were lawfully revived by the statute which is not unconstitutional. The Legislature may waive the rights of the State and recognize claims founded on equity and justice even where, as here, the transaction upon which the claims were founded had occurred long before and had previously been litigated to final judgment. What is essential to the validity of the statute waiving the rights of the State is an existing moral obligation, which, along with a sense of justice, is present in this case involving injuries to two police officers due to the negligence of the State, which injuries were sustained while the officers were performing their jobs in the service of the public good. Furthermore, since it was previously determined that claimants' injuries were proximately caused by the State's negligence in failing to prevent the patient's escape and in failing to take the proper steps to apprehend him after his escape, claimants are granted partial summary judgment on the issue of liability.

People v. Johnson


47256


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


196 A.D.2d 408; 601 N.Y.S.2d 103; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7982



August 5, 1993, Decided  
August 5, 1993, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Johnson, 82 N.Y.2d 806, 624 N.E.2d 1037, 1993 N.Y. LEXIS 4568, 604 N.Y.S.2d 942 (1993)
Decision reached on appeal by People v. Johnson, 236 A.D.2d 299, 654 N.Y.S.2d 302, 1997 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1424 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 1997)
Related proceeding at People v. Johnson, 2016 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1248, 2016 NY Slip Op 1260 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Feb. 23, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence by the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), for rape and sodomy.


OVERVIEW: Although the court affirmed defendant's conviction for rape and sodomy, it disallowed use of prior convictions to serve as a predicate for a persistent violent felony offender sentence. The trial court could find defendant guilty even though there was only one witness, and the circumstances supported the victim's testimony. However, one sentence was too old to extend defendant's sentence. Defendant should have had a chance to challenge another prior conviction on the ground that it was unconstitutionally obtained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction in part, as it could be based on one witness's testimony. The court vacated the sentence and remanded for resentencing, because one sentence was too old to show defendant was a persistent offender, and allowed defendant to challenge the constitutionality of his other sentence.

 

400 of 992 DOCUMENTS


Berry v. Perales


67699


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


195 A.D.2d 926; 600 N.Y.S.2d 838; 1993 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7643



July 29, 1993, Decided  
July 29, 1993, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney appealed the decision of the Albany County Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed his application to review a determination of respondent employer to terminate his employment.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was employed on a probationary basis for the employer. At the conclusion if his first probationary period, he was given a satisfactory rating. At the conclusion of his second probationary period, he was given an unsatisfactory rating, but was permitted to complete an additional probationary period. At the conclusion of his third probationary period, the employee was terminated. The employee brought an action seeking to annul the first unsatisfactory rating he had received and to require his advancement to a permanent position with the employer. The lower court dismissed the employee's action, and the employee appealed. The court, however, affirmed the lower court's decision. The court held that the employee was not dismissed for an unconstitutional purpose, and that as a probationary employee he could be dismissed at any time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision.
